Verses by Henry Lawson
ABOVE CROW’S NEST, SYDNEY.
The Red Page
A blanket low and leaden,
Though rent across the west,
Whose darkness seems to deaden
The brightest and the best;
A sunset white and staring
On cloud-wrecks far away —
And haggard house-walls glaring
A farewell to the day.
A light on tower and steeple,
Where sun no longer shines —■
My people, O my people!
Rise up and read the signs!
Low looths the nearer high-line
(No sign of star or moon),
The horseman on the skyline
Rode hard this afternoon!
(Is he—and ivho shall know it? —
The spectre of a scout?
The spirit of a poet,
Whose truths were met with doubt?
Who sought and who succeeded
1 n marking danger's track —
Whose warnings were unheeded
Till all the sky was black?)
It is a shameful story
For our young, generous home—
Without the rise and glory
We'd go as Greece and Rome.
Without the sacrifices
That make a nation's name,
The elder nation's vices
Anl luxuries tee claim.
Grown vain without a conquest,
And sure without a fort,
And maddened in the one quest
For pleasure or for sport.
Self-blinded to our starkness
We'd fling the time away
To fight,, half-armed, in darkness
Who should be armed to-day.
This song is for the city,
The city in its pride —
The coming time shall pity
And shield the country-side.
Shall we live in the present
Till fearful war-clouds loom.
And till the sullen peasant
Shall leave us to our doonv?
Cloud-fortresses titanic
Along the western sky —
The tired, bowed mechanic
And pallid clerk flit by.
Lit by a light unhealthy —
The ghastly after-glare —
The veiled, and goggled wealthy
Drive ffyt —they know not where.
Night’s suHen spirit rouses,
The darkening gables lour,
From ugly four-roomed houses
Verandah'd windows glower;
The last long day-stare dies on
The scrub-ridged western side,
And round the near horizon
The spectral horsemen ride.
MY FATHER-IN-LAW AND I.
My father-in-law is a careworn man,
And a silent man is he j
But he summons a smile as well as he can
Whenever he meets with me.
The sign we make with a silent shake
That speaks of the days gone by —
Line men who meet at a funeral —
My father-in-law and I.
My father-in-law is a sober man
(And a virtuous man, I think) ;
But we spare a shilling whenever we can,
And we both drop in for a drink.
Our pints they fill, and we say, "Ah, well l”
With the sound of the world-old sigh —
Like the drink that comes after a funeral —
My father-in-law and I.
My father-in-law is a kindly man —
A domestic man is he.
He tries to look cheerful as well as he can
Whenever he meets with\ me.
But we stand and think till the second drink
In a silence that might imply
That we'd both get over a funeral,
My father-in-law and I.
From “For Australia, and Other Poems”
(Standard Publishing Co., Melb.).
In his latest collection of verses, For Aus-
tralia, and Other Poems, Henry Lawson
echoes himself —somewhat faintly. Yet in
these rimes on familiar subjects, treated
from a view-point that only Lawson’s wide
popularity has made too familiar, there is
an individuality that is not to be found in
the notes of any other Australian poet. A
rather winning individuality uttered with
an art that is naive and apparently uncon-
scious.
Candidly, Lawson’s view-point is rather
belated: the decade of bitterness has gone
by: our poetry has got over its growing
pains. The literature of this Australia of
to-day is hopeful, vitalised by a new in-
stinct of nationhood. Our poets are no longer
facing the horizons of the west: they are
eagerly looking up to the zenith, looking into
the future. They are no longer demolishing
things: they are beginning, quite cheerfully,
to build. The class-war has fizzled out into
a rather uninteresting peace; the under-dog*
roams unmuzzled about the Speaker’s chair.
Its owner, pulling perspiringly at its chain,
is unwillingly being dragged along—and each
yard there is less of him to drag. The old
State bitterness survives only in the dead
editorials of the press of the capitals: Aus-
tralians have discovered Australia —the
clinAx to the most momentous work of ex-
ploration done in the twentieth century. Our
literature is beginning to live in the future.
The night is over and gone.
And Lawson’s complainings begin to sound
rather fretful. The present scribe does not
own a motor —does not want to own one—
but to Lawson there is nothing nicer in a
motor: he is rather prejudiced against
motors. And are all
Inventors, artists, poets —
Exiled or driven mad,
Btreated, sneered at, slandered,
And driven to the bad?
And is there one critic in Australia who
felt thus at Victor Daley’s death?
They dare to write about the man
That they have never seen:
The blustering false Bohemian
That you have never been ;
Some with the false note in their voice,
And with the false tear shed,
Who in their secret heart rejoice
, For one more rival — dead....
They slander you, fresh in the sand,
They slander me alive.. . .
Because we would not crawl to them,
Their hands we would not shake.
Bcause their greed we would condemn,
Their bribes we would not take..'. .
Yet. happily. Lawson’s humor, when he
will allow it, can soften his bitterness.
And his patriotic verses are as good as
any now being written here. Yet he can
sink to prose like this, in “Australia’s
Peril”
Make farms fit to live on. build workshops
and technical schools for your sons;
Keep the wealth of the land in Australia —
make your own clotlf, machines and
guns! {
His verses to Daley, in spite of their
bitterness, have intimate touches of remin-
iscence and humor that could come only
from Lawson. It is true tliaT herein Law-
son echoes himself, yet the echo is useful in
recalling the great shout that gave it birth.
That is the penalty of fame: a man must
not fall below his best.
Bully Hayes, Buccaneer, by the late
Louis Becke. is the latest issue of the
N.S.W. Bookstall’s shilling novels. It dif-
fers from the usual Bully Hayes story in
that it is written by a man who knew.
Recollections of a Victorian Police Officer-
bv John Sadlier. formerly Inspecting-Snper-
intendent of Police (George Robertson.
Melh.), ia a plainly written narrative, from
the police point of view, of the author s ad-
venturous experiences during a picturesque
era of our early history. A large portion ot
the book deals with the facts relating to the
Kelly Gang. The illustrations are man>
and of some historical value.
The Round Table for Sept. (2a. 6d.) con-
tains a valuable article upon Downing-
street. and the latest and _ most.
ritative news of all the Dominions within tn
seas. The Australian section treats ot tn
late election and of defence and industn
problems. A file of this quarterly becoin
of increasing value.
Fiction
Triangles of Life•, and Other Stories
(Standard Co., Melb.), reprinted from The
Bulletin, show us a familiar Henry Law-
son, a little tired. There is always some
touch in a Lawson sketch that puts the
Lawson brand on it —some bit of realism
shot through by a suggestion of romance.
For instance, haphazard, this: “Down the
hard road to the crossing at Pipeclay Creek
sounded the footsteps of a man. Not the
crunching steps of an English laborer, clod-
hopping contentedly home; these sounded
more like the footsteps of one pacing steadily
to and fro. and thinking steadily and hope-
lessly—sorting out the past.” How that laSit
phrase lifts a bit of observation to poetry !
But in these stories there is a carelessness of
structure that looks like criminal negligence.
His longer narratives ramble instead of pro-
gressing. Once by this method Lawson used
to build up an atmosphere; that was its
legitimate excuse. Now he merely rambles.
In his briefer sketches there are still his
quiet humor and his quieter pathos. In-
teresting, but a little dull.
These novelists are shrewd business men.
Arnold Bennett and Oliver Onions are the
new captains of fictional industry. There is
now no demand for the old three-volume
novel; yet that form of fiction had for the
author its advantages. He need provide
only one plot for some 400,000 words, and
the extra amount of writing was compen-
sated for by the larger price received for his
book. With the reduction in size and price
of novels, the author had to think out three
plots and three sets of characters for prac-
tically the same money. It occurred to
Bennett and Onions that, having taken all
the trouble to invent a popular plot, it
would be a safe speculation to utilise that
plot and the characters with which their
authors had got familiar for two other vol-
umes. Thus the author would be two plots
and" two sets of characters to the good for
the same number of words. The result of
this business scheme is shown in Bennett’s
Clayhanger and Hilda Lessways —the third
volume is ' not yet published ; and in Oliver
Onions’ In Accordance with the Evidence,
The Dehit Account and The Story of Louie
fßell, per Dymock's Library and Sydney
Book Club). Both authors proceed in the
same way. The story is told in the first
book from the viewpoint of one of its char-
acters, and in the second and third volumes
the same series of events is re-told as they
appeared to other characters. Thus we get.
as it were, the same march of events from
three independent angles—and it is remark-
able how different the same situations ap-
pear when the authors change the person-
ality of the onlooker. Thus in Clayhanger,
Hilda was always a mysterious person,
since we were envisaging her with the eves
of the man who loved her. In Hilda Less-
ivays, the woman loving, it was Clayhanger
who became mysterious. So with Onions.
His first volume showed us a London clerk,
Jeffries, who murdered a rival in love and
was not discovered. His second volume
told us, from another viewpoint, of his
marriage with the girl and his subsequent
life and death. The third volume retells the
whole story as it appeared to a girl named
Louie, who loved Jeffries even when she
found out that he was a murderer. It was
her influence that ultimately drove that
superman to his death. In the previous books
Louie was a minor character ; but here* die
becomes the heroine, and, incidentally, dis-
places from that position in our minds the
woman whom the murderer married. Her
story is a most interesting one. her char-
acter, now that it is filled out from the
incomplete sketch that appeared in the pre-
vious volumes, is a piece of subtle
psychology. As a separate novel The Story
of Louie is of absorbing interest: as the
conclusion of a brilliant three-volume novel.
it raises the book to a higher level of
artistry. Oliver Onions is a novelist already
reaching to a comparison with the few
great writers of modern English fiction. Of
course, there is no reason why he should
not give us the same murder-story over again
in a fourth novel: there are several of the
characters in that story that might want
to tell us how it struck them. We 11....
this critic will turn eagerly to the fourth
volume. .. .and the fifth.
J*
Reginald Wright Kauffman, author of
that convincing and unsensational study of
white slavery. Daughters of Ishmael,
depicts with the same sincerity another
aspect of the sex problem in The Sentence
of Silence (Bell, yer Dymock’s Library).
Ilis theme is the ignorance, not innocence,
of sex in which parents bring up their chil-
dren.
“Parents still go about pretending that
the world is the same to-day as it was yes-
terday ; while all the time the change is
here. We go about pretending that nobody
knows what everybody practices. We edu-
cate, or we make a more or less cloudy at-
tempt to educate, our daughters and sons to
face life: and yet we never say one word
to. them about the very fact of life itself.
We send them out., our loved and cherished,
the souls and bodies that we’ve tied our
hopes to —send ’em out to meet the greatest,
the strongest force in human nature, utterly
ignorant, utterly unprepared.”
The story of Dau is the story of a youth
brought up under the sentence of silence.
Through ignorance he stumbles through the
sort of life led by, perhaps, the majority of
young men; and at the end he meets and
gradually learns to love a woman whose
charm is her sincerity and her knowledge
of the facts of life. The book is cast in the
form of a tragedy, yet the boy frees himself
from the thrall of his numerous “affairs”
with disreputable women, apparently little
the worse, and in the end wins the one
woman. The happy ending arrives with the
usual weddiug—and in one brief, shattering
paragraph comes tragedy. The woman he
had just married explains dn puzzled won-
derment and fear. “I thought you knew. I
told you ; I thought you understood. What
have I been? Nothing that you’ve-mot been.
What right have you to ask?”
Grant Hervey in reply
The screed subjoined is either a sermon
or an ad., or, maybe, just plain Grantaus-
tralian. As we have flung a few adjectives
at the Rev. Hervey, the pulpit is his for 15
minutes: —
When I began to try my hand at the apostle
business, I was aged 18. That was about 15 years
ago, and I was born at Casterton, Victoria, in Octo-
ber, 1880 : therefore I’m still on the pleasant side of
33. At that prehistoric time—the period when my
first chunk of manuscript fell upon the desk of The
Bulletin —I was breaking pig-iron in a Melbourne
foundry ; doing it cheerfully, with the business end
of a 381 b. sledge-hammer. As a certain foreman
blacksmith once said, after our ancient steam-
hammer had burst its jacketing, and had made me
tl»e residuary legatee of its vigorous job, I was Hot
Stuff on a big weld. I remember that that same
steam-hammer stood there in a semi-dismantled con-
dition for months afterwards, simply because I was
there in a leathern apron and greasy dungarees, pre-
pared to take nine falls out of the biggest forging
that came along. The Australian manufacturer was
mostly a sweat-greedv, short-sighted fool about that
period; and would rather use up a strong man or
two, and then throw him on the industrial scrap-
heap, than spend a few pounds upon a damaged
machine. There are some manufacturers in this
country who haven’t got past that aboriginal stage
of mental development even yet.
Well, one day I hammered out that First Bit of
Verse, already alluded to, and sent it to The
Bulletin. I think that was before the “Red Page”
was invented. Anyway, it was shortly before the
South African War; so. having posted the screed
with a sweaty hand in South Melbourne, I kept on
hitting the pig-iron and waited for the Editorial
Echo. When it came, one fine and cheerful Thurs-
day afternoon, it arrived exactly like this: “G.H.:
GO AND DROWN YOURSELF?’ Now, that was
scarcely encouraging. In fact, any ordinary Nice,
Tame Little Poet, with a maiden aunt in Malvern
and a right-thinking male parent doing a roaring
rag-business in The Lane, would have been dis-
tinctly shocked. One or two worthy poets that I
know—-T. B. O’Hara, for instance—-would have died
of grief. But I didn't die. There MAY be those
who regret my survival, such is the depravity of
human nature; hut my sudden death just then, a la
John Keats and the Edinburgh Review, would have
been inconvenient to my boss. You see, the nasty,
dirty, uproarious, horribly unpoetical steel Jehannum
of a shop where I tore my crust out of the universe
had just obtained a contract for a lot of electric-
lighting machinery, and I was wanted. And so I
bathed my Wounded Feelings in the slack-tub, which
was large enough to cool-off a ship’s stern-framing,
and resolved to live.
Also, I resolved that editors didn’t know every-
thing—a resolution that I have never seen any cause
to alter. Clearly, if the then editor of The Bul-
letin did hot understand the importance of encour-
aging an apostle who should go forth in book-form
and preach the gospel of a Big Australia, it was
my duty to complete his education. So I resolved
to he kind, but firm, and commenced that contract
right away.
The then editor proved a very refractory pupil,
lie simply WOULD NOT learn. And vet the pains
that I took were prodigious. Receiving nothing hut
scorn and abuse per medium of the “Correspondence
Column,” it required much fortitude to hear up
beneath the burden of such a pupil. As German
Charlie, one of my shopmates and an able foreman
fitter, once remarked: “Py Chiminy! Dot plattv
Pullf.tin schall ged you into der platty Yarra
ge-mit, und der platty gontract schall haf to wait
for your tamn funeral ge-sprache!”
Nevertheless, 1 kept on. And alter i|„, .
lesson, during which period a modern blart-fu™ h
might have been kept in full working order
my stock ot rejected manuscripts, niv first sIL 1
Verse appeared It had something^
Art of Steering Reapers-and-Binders in the film
vvas tucked away in some insignificant corner of
Bulletin. Also, the thud of Kruger’s Lonl f K
and the uproar of British pom-poms begin,™
d ? a /en Australia about this period, and 60 the
ot this New Star in Perseus was overlooked R„ t ■
the eyes at least of German Charlie—mv First m
ciple! as one small item after another commenced
to appear, I loomed larger than Goethe, Schille
and Schopenhauer. One night at the Melh™,; 1
Turn Verein, when the dismal job in unhappy X
Africa vvas sagging to its ugly end, and when I!
first distinctive Psalm-Editorial had just appeared y
1 remember that it was an early ' exhortation on
England’s changing naval policy, and that it ore
dieted the building of an independentlv-owned Auk
tralian fleet—Charlie made his prophecy • “pt
Chiminy 1 My poy,” he declaimed, waving a small
pewter barrow-load of beer, “py Chiminy i Vo
schall geep on, und you schall write und write mit
dot platty Pulletin, und all der gritics schall eo
to Hell mit. Und der bublischer-man schall? make
mit you der gontract also ! Yah! Write der nlattv
boetry—UND WAITV’ p aUy
Well, my dear Australian brethren at Home and
abroad, der Platty Boetry is written—such as it is
It is not as good as I would like it to be, but per
haps I shall improve. Anyway, the publisher-man
has paid me in advance for the privilege of making
a book of it, under the prophetic title of Australians
Yet. And now, if German Charlie is still one of
The Bulletin’s readers—the last I heard of him he
was boss of the air-compressors on a Kalgoorlie
mine—if German Charlie will now rise up and send
me the address of his present gontract, he shall have
a special copy of my little book: For German
Charlie was the first Big-Australian saint to sit in
my congregation; and the Man Who Believes in
You When You Are Getting Swatted In the Eye is
worth a thousand of the sort who smile 10 or 15
years after, when they see you coming down the
steps of a bank.
Brethren, I have been preaching to you, off and
on, from this Bulletin pulpit for a number of years.
Occasionally I have wandered away and taken a look
at other cities and other seaports, but always I have
returned a lover of Sydney above all cities. Sydney
is going to be the next New York. I want to see it
made a city worthy of Australia—a city such as
Phidias and Pericles and Praxiteles, if were all
to step off the boat in a bunch, wouhj admire.
Melbourne is not a city—it is a Blot. Mel-
bourne can never be anything but a Blot, but Syd-
ney can become one of the shining glories of the
world. Australia wants ONE CITY to be its ever-
lasting pride—a city such as Yass-Can-Blowfly can
never be —and if all the people of Sydney will only
learn to Think Australian, then they will under-
stand that Sydney, before it can possibly become our
City of the Future, must become Australia’s city,
instead of remaining the merely greedy and parochial
capital of New South Wales.
The beauty of Sydney is the beauty of sailing
cloud and shimmering sea. And this, by association
of beauty with beauty, reminds me of my interest
in Australian women. Our women take off more
clothes in Sydney than they do in Wowsertarian
Melbourne ; even as all Catholic priests are forbirkle*
to attend the theatre in Melbourne, and, therefore,
invariably go to Sydney for their holidays. No
association inferred between these facts, if you
please. I simply want to say that I am a believer in
this fine, new type of Australian woman—the type
that our premier city of Sydney is evolving for us.
It is not clothes that are modest or immodest, and I
like these artistic, line-revealing modern fashions.
I believe that every man likes them, and hopes that
the rag-fashions of yesterday will never return. All
artists and lovers of beauty should write and defend
the present mode, and should hail and celebrate the
fact that the feminine form is now shown; express-
ing the hope that such celebration will lead women
to take care of their physical beauty and to learn
how to develop it. The Melbourne Wowser shrieks
that thin silk stockings are a Sin. Well, let him
shriek. Why should it he dreadful for a man to
know that there is good pink flesh beneath a woman's
stockings? What is wrong in being allowed to know
that women have legs, so long as those legs are
shapely? As the Archbishop of the Big Australians,
I wish to say that I believe in Legs. I want to see
the Australian woman get away from the horrible,
old, insanitary ideas of her British mother. I want
to see her become so alluring and so super-feminine,
so physically sound and gloriously clean that every
Big Australian in this whole wide continent will rise
up and demand such personal prosperity as will
enable him to marrv her. For marriage means More
Men to Defend the Nation ; therefore, when I become
First President of the Australian Republic, I shall
use Beautiful Legs and Shauelv Arms as a means of
attracting hither the Best White Manhood of Europe
and America.
GRANT HENRY.

VARIOVS VERSE
[For The Bulletin.]
A Question of Time.
With happy lips that mock at time
a 1 sat beneath the trees.
eard . l beir branches whisper rhyme
hen tickled by the breeze —
Ihe sort of rhyme that hints at bliss
lake elfin echoes of a kiss.
like poppy petals red
_ T y-be happy summer throws--—^
With windy fingers on a bed
Beneath a sleeper’s nose,
Were such that sleeping or awake
My own red lips their-joy must take.
A thousand kisses in the dark
Seem but a single kiss,
They cause no scar, they leave no mark,
And yet the lips would miss
A single one-that went astray.
Or one so light it would not stay.-
The world that itches crowns to win—
Or half-crowns, failing such—
Is apt to hold that it is sin
When lips on warm lips touch—
In fact, it holds it very Wrong
In those to whom the lips belong.
Her lips did not belong to me
In the commercial sense ;
Yet, finding that their use was free
Tcould not hurry thence.
But ttrftiy own sweet use I reft
Their pleasant taste in open theft.
Perchance I stole five hundred, so
The rest I did not take ;
Many were given me I know,
My thirsty 'Soul to slake,
For she was generous and kind
And threw them round for me to find.
I did not lose a single kiss,
•But caught and kept them all;
Borne held a minute of pure bliss
.And some were crisp and small.
But for their very perfect taste
I did not let one go to waste.
The little stars played hide-and-seek
Among the leaves above,
The night was absolutely sleek
And velvet-soft for love,
And so the;happy time went by
Swift as the breathing of a sigh.
When Adarn found the new-made Eve
He did not know the time,
Nor did he want to, I believe.
For kissing was no crime.
Till in their noon of Paradise
They ate the peach and paid the price.
’Tis true I did not eat my peach,
But that old case to match,
The time was quite beyond my reach
For uncle had my watch.
And how was I to feel or know
The speed with which the moments go?
The lips that had been lent to me,
Or borrowed if you please.
Or stolen as the case may be,
My rapture to apfifiase,
As time is counted among men
Were wanted home at half-past ten
But when we started to return
We found the .wharf all dark.
Above we saw one hot star burn,
But there was naught to mark
The fact that any vessels plied
Across those waters waste and wide.
We saw at once that we were done-
We simply could not face
The city in to-morrow’s sun ;
So, weighted with disgrace.
We had gone hopelessly astray
And, kissed eight solid hours away.
’Twas purely on account of time
That everything went wrong.
If we had only heard a chime
Instead of that low song
Of waving branches we had known
The way the giddy hours had flown.
So there was nothing left to do
Except to sit and wait
Until the morning skies got blue
And sunshine filled the great.
Wide wonder of the kind old world
With various things that kissed and curled.
And as w r e could not then return
To face the city’s gaze.
We had to watch the slow sun burn
Down to the evening ways,
And then to wait till dark was slain
And morning called him up again.
The sun goes up, the sun goes down,
The boats flit to and fro,
But we can never go to town
To see the folk we know.
And so, forlorn, we linger here
And eat the shining atmosphere.
With kisses sweet we chase the day
And make the swift night fly.
The hurried morn can never stay
To wipe his dew-tears dry ;
While lip on scarlet lip will rhyme
We need not take account of time.
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.
Inez.
Hot with old Spanish blood, her lips made
red
At f hat fine forge where Eros hammers
A glory of dark tresses round her head.
The grace of olden dances in her feet.
And eyes....the dusky wells where some
warm thought
Lies hid, in nets of dream and wonder
caught.
She touches we with fingers all so light
A fairy magic trembles in the tips.
Her breath is like white flowers that bloom
by night.
And in the rapture of her close-pressed ups
All this unfolded, reckless heart of mine
Sways to the kiss of God’s best-hoarded
wine.
N.S.W.
DAVID MCKEE WRIGHT.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
ipaper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
.care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 16. 19X3.
THE SERMON THAT WAS PREACHED
BACKWARDS.
The Federal estimates, presented a few
days ago by Treasurer Bigjohn Forrest,
include a vote for the construction of the
railway from Pine Creek in the Northern
Territory to Katherine River; so apparently
th» lamentable blunder of starting work at
the wrong end is really to be perpetrated.
'The Bulletin has written much on this
subject, and the importance of it is so vast
that it makes no apology for writing some
■more. In fact, it will make no apology even
if it writes a great deal more. The iailway
tto the Territory is the most urgent —or very
mearly the most urgent—public concern in
Australia. It ranks far ahead of the Wes-
fealiaD transcontinental line in a national
*ense, though it may not be so immediately
profitable. Even on this point, however,
tjherifi is no complete certainty. It is re-
quired to bring settlers, who are also de-
fenders, to the emptiest part of Australia,
amd the part which is handiest for Asiatic
lt is needed so that it will be
possible to send troops from the one fairly
populous corner which Australia possesses
to the region which is most liable to want
troops, and which is, at present, least likely
to get them. And it is a matter of urgency,
because the Commonwealth owns a vast,
rich property which doesn’t pay for axle-
grease through lack of communication; a
few of the grandest rivers in Australia,
which yield no dividend because there is no
traffic for them to carry and no wheels for
them to turn; and two bits of railway
which are a dead loss because they end in
the middle and dead end of nowhere. By
reason of these matters the Commonwealth
has so much less money' wherewith to buy
guns, and so much less population to pay
for guns and to carry them. The develop-
ment of the great Northern property should
be a thing of haste, and judging by the
Budget of Cook’s large and prosperous-
looking Treasurer the Cook Government
has an eye upon the fact. It isn’t any
great quantity of eye, but it is a trifle of
eye to go on with —probably as much as
can be spared from the weary job of watch-
ing out against the incessant absence of
Bruce Smith when a division is called for.
Still, many a good work can be spoiled
by being done in the wrong way, and this
is a case in point. A sermon is supposed
to be beneficial, but a sermon shouldn’t be
preached backwards. A well is a great in-
stitution in a dry land, but no well that is
dug from the bottom upwards amounts to
much. A watchdog has points about him
in ordinary circumstances, but a watchdog
that rushes at the burglar the wrong way,
and attacks him with his tail, is inefficient.
The same may be said of the house whereof
the roof is built first, and has to be sup-
ported by balloons pending the arrival of the
lower premises and the importation of the
cellar. All these are instances of more or
less value, for the commencement of the
Territory railway from the northern end is
an exactly similar mistake. It is the sort
of blunder that Columbus would have made
if he had tried to find the so-called New
World by starting from New Orleans _ in-
stead of Cadiz, and sailing with the hind-
quarters of his fleet in front.
The essential, as already mentioned, is to
connect the Territory with the two or three
congested cities of the South which have a
little population, and consequently a. few
defenders to spare—to break up the isola-
tion and emptiness which are the result of
vast sea-distances and lack of markets. One
hundred miles of railway built from Oodna-
datta northward will make the first railway
connection between the Territory and Ade-
laide, Melbourne and Sydney. It will make
accessible the tail end, at least, of the table-
land, for most of the dry stretch has
already been bridged by the Port Augusta-
Oodnadatta line. After the boundary has
been crossed, every mile of new rail that is
laid will open up a bit more of the Pro-
mised Land —the one great area of Middle
Australia where the rainfall is fairly steady,
and the evaporation is small, and the nights
are cool, and the mosquitoes are few, and
even the maddest Tory dare not get off his
chest the gag that “the white man can’t
live.” And every mile of new railway will
mean one mile less of country which may
be invaded with impunity because it is cut
off from any assistance which the big
centres of population can afford.
On the other hand the Government which
builds its railway from the north down to-
wards the bottom of the map makes for the
time —that is, until the work is finished—a
pathway for the possible enemy. At present,
if a powerful and objectionable foreign
creature captured Port Darwin, he would
find himself in command of 145 miles of
track with which to carry his troops into
the interior. If another 800 miles of the
same- substance were built at that end, he
would find himself kindly and obligingly
furnished with 945 miles of line to help
him to annex the interior, in consequence
of which he would find it easy to annex a
lot more interior. Whereas 100 miles of
railway development from the south would
begin to connect the Territory with the old,
big cities, and to break up the isolation of
the Hermit Province, it would take some
1000 miles of railway-building from the
north to produce the same effect. The
sermon is the same, but the benefit may be
lost by preaching the discourse backward.
Even 800 miles of new line, built south-
ward from the Pine Creek terminus, might
not make a single acre of the Territory any
more easy of defence so far as the present
great centres of population are concerned,
but that method of construction might make
a present of many millions of good acres to
the thief who came by sea in the night.
So the patriotic theory is that the trans-
continental railway should hurry northward
from Oodnadatta. spreading out tentacles —
which will be really entrenchments or forti-
fications —east and west as it goes, to con-
nect with Sydney, Brisbane, Townsville and
the ports of northern Westralia. Thus the
space which could be commanded by an
enemy who had secured control of the ocean
would grow less every day, and the space
which could be commanded by an Aus-
tralian army —if Australia has sense enough
to maintain a thoroughly efficient army—
would become larger every day. The area
which depended wholly for its defence on
the little Australian fleet would diminish,
and the area for the defence of which the
fleet and army could co-operate would grow.
There would be no great isolated railway
system in the North which an invader could
seize and employ for his own purposes. The
habit of leaving lines lying about where, on
a great emergency, we can’t get at them
while the other party can is no wiser than
would be a similar habit of leaving guns or
money lying about. The only article be-
longing to Australia which should be need-
lessly deposited in the Port Darwin district
while that harbor is in its present unpro-
tected condition is the Public Debt. That
(praise be to Heaven!) can be left loose on
the shore anywhere. Incidentally the at-
tack on the problem from the south implies
getting .a lot more railway for the money.
It means construction at South Australian
wages and expense rates, which will be
fairly moderate, because the workers will
have two big cities behind them as sources
of supply, and a Federal railway to carry
their goods at cost. On the other hand,
expansion from the north must involve work
done at the exorbitant rates charged in an
isolated and banished community, situated
at the end of an immensely long sea-route
over which Private Enterprise has full
control.
* * * *
The ruinous and unpatriotic idea of re-
suming the construction of the transcon-
tinental railway at the foolish end rather
than at the wise one is a political com-
promise. It is the work of men who fear
to make up their minds lest by so doing
they may lose their jobs and their twopence, l '
According to the terms of the agreement
under which over 500,000 square miles be-
came a Federal property the Port Augusta-
Poft Darwin railway is to be finished. And
seeing that the Commonwealth owns the
Territory, and must needs develop its pos-
session if it wants to make any money out
of it, the building of this work right through
the great Federal area seems reasonable.
But a section of the community, in Queens-
land, N.S.W. and Victoria, wants the
Government to break faith with South Aus-
tralia, and leave the bulk of the Territory
undeveloped and useless, and build a steel
track from Port Darwin into the Eastern
States. There are votes to be lost in the
Middle and West by taking the line east,
and there are votes to be lost in the East
by looking strictly after the interests of
Australia and sticking to the centre. Poli-
ticians hate to lose votes, though the mer-
cenary struggle for them is often an ill-
paving business. An old-time crowd of
Federal Laborites abandoned the proposed
Australian capital on the Snowy River,
with its magnificent water supply and
water-power, in favor of the low flats and
stagnant waters of Canberra, in order to
purchase the support of Sydney, and it got
its reward, for it was thrown out with
ignominy. It got in again, and the end of
its second period of office was largely
brought about by Sydney. Still the desire
to acquire votes, or to steer a shady middle
course in the hope of not losing them, ap-
pears to be incurable.
The section from Pine Creek to the Kath-
erine is about all that can be built without
awaking the sleeping hound of State Rights.
So far there will only be applause from the
unthinking multitude. South of the Kath-
erine the eggs and hoots will begin. The
fact that a good deal of applause is likely
to come also from Japan is unfortunate,
but in the struggle to be non-committal
misfortunes will occur. In this matter the
Cook party is very like the Fisher party,
and the Fisher gang presents a dreadful
resemblance to the Cook crowd. Each is
willing to sell a great national interest for
the sake of public approyal. And they have
got so little of it that one is in office with-
out power, and the other is out of office
without salary. The approval was so
equally divided that neither got enough of it
to live on.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Vaccination Bill.
For plain, dull-brown futility there was
nothing in the record of the N.S.W. session
that deserved a larger leather medal than
the Compulsory Vaccination Bill. If the
leather medal had been as big as the case
called for there would have been enough
material in it to set up a boot factory. In
the first place, nobody now living—much
less anybody now grown up—was to be com-
pulsorily vaccinated. As the last person
now above ground in the Ma territory may
live for another years, that meant post-
poning any serious effort at the obliteration
of the disease till some time after the year
2000. The year 2000 is a long way off, and
if by any chance the solar, system gets off
the plumb, and the world, while skirting
the sharp corner of a patch of asteroids,
bumps against a perfectly new heavenly
body, the year 2000 may never arrive at
all. Next, the Government lacked the cour-
age to make the parent pay for tl*e vaccina-
tion of his child, though that is As essential
an act of cleanliness as the washing of the
child, and the parent has to pay for
child washing. The penalties for fail-
ure to vaccinate were whittled down till at
last an agreement was reached by which,
after a cranky father had paid £5 in fines,
he could be prosecuted no further, and his
child might be exposed to the horrors of
one of the worst diseases oh earth
without anybody interfering on its behalf.
Apparently nobody cared a straw for the
welfare of the child, and all the anxiety
was for the cranky father. Finally the
Holman Government decided that it
wouldn’t put its weight and influence be-
hind even the remaining futile shred of its
own measure, and it made the question a
non-party one. Under Party Government
nobody has much interest in non-party
questions, and members promptly go out to
see a girl about a cat, or something of the
sort. And while a girl is drawing a pint of
tortoiseshell cat in a pewter anything may
happen. What happened was that, by a
very mixed vote, the Vaccination Bill went
out on the third reading, and the proposal
to really clean up Sydney died of inanition.
Yet small-pox is a sufficiently terrible com-
plaint to justify extraordinary precautions.
It hasn’t attacked Sydney this time in a
virulent form because there has been so
much voluntary vaccination on the spot,
and because a fairly large number of the
people are new arrivals from countries
where vaccination is compulsory. But if a
spirit of cheerful contempt for the disease
takes possession of the community, and vac-
cination falls into disuse, there is no reason
to doubt that the horror is waiting round
in the next street, and feeling as good as
ever. In older days it killed the clean and
the unclean, the poor and the rich, the well-
fed king and the ill-fed peasant with equal
cheerfulness. It emptied six important
European thrones within 80 years—petty
nrinces and subsidiary potentates died so
freely of it that they weren’t worth count-
ing. " It wiped out, in the middle of last
century, more than one-third of an army
100.000 strong, and did it in about a
year, despite the fact that it was a' well-
paid, well-fed and well-cared-for army. It
attacks the unborn child, wfeieWmay recover
from the illness in the womb and be born
alive —yet a blind, shapeless horror that
would "be better dead. And in the later
days, before vaccination got the plague
down and sat on its head, it had begun to
spread from humans to sheep, and to make
the meat uneatable and the wool a som
of death. It is a sort of fashion nowadays
to scoil at vaccination as unnecessarv h
cause small-pox, by reason of vaccinating
is at present a matter of
small account. In the same way the in
habitants of a walled city set in the middle
of savages might decide that their lk
were superfluous, because the savages t
by reason of these walls, had left off hr,« l
tili ties. Or in similar fashion, because
drainage has practically killed off certain
diseases in certain Australian cities it
might be claimed that drains should now
be regarded as a needless and expensive
precaution. What threatens to discredit
vaccination is its success.
Government by Slander and Assault.
The closing days of the 22nd Parliament of
N. S. Wales were disfigured by a couple of
incidents that should do a lot of good in
the way of satisfying the electors as to the
inherent disreputableness and futility of the
Party system. C. G. Wade’s attack on
Works Minister Griffith would never have
been made in the way it was made under
any other form of government. Griffith
was accused of dishonesty, in connection
with land transactions in half a dozen dif-
ferent parts of the country. The establish-
ment of any one of the charges would have
n.eant that Griffith was so unfit for public
life as to be a menace to the State that
employed him. They were none of them
related in the remotest degree. Griffith’s
allegedly corrupt land speculations at Nar-
rabeen, and his reputedly dishonest efforts in
connection with the Suspension Bridge and
the Uhr’s Point transactions, had no more
to do with (say) the Medlow Bath matter
than surf-bathing has to do with polar ex-
ploration. Each of the six charges was
capable of proof or disproof, as the case
might be, without reference to any of the
others. The Medlow Bath matter contained
the suspicion of an element that had nothing
to do with Ministerial probity. C. G.
Wade seized on it and placed it before
all the others. Sydney Sunday Times,
which is nothing if not anti-Labor, put the
position with ruthless fairness as follows:
Why is Mr. Wade so anxious to press this matter
that he will sink all the rest, and why, also, is the
Government reluctant, even to refusal, to allow this
charge to be investigated? There are no satisfac-
tory answers to either of these questions, and the
public is driven to the conclusion that there is
something behind this Medlow matter which might
only be brought out by cross-examination in a wit-
ness-box. This much is certain, that if this charge
cannot be publicly made it is not one that concerns
the public.
That is how the thing appeals to decent-
minded folks w!k> are outside party politics.
But to actual players of the unsavory game
such a point of view is absurd. They accept
it as a contest in which thimble-rigging,
biting, kicking, marking the cards, and vil-
lainy and thieving generally are engaged in
as a matter of course. By virtue of long
usage a man is enabled to do that in party
politics which in private life would get him
barred from the society of his friends for
ever. Contemporaneously, more or less, with
Wade’s refusal to proceed with his corrup-
tion charges against Griffith, on the
ground that he had been precluded from
dealing with one of them, there occurred
the Moxham-McGirr fight. On this occa-
sion the middle-aged Liberal and the youth-
ful Laborite went to the library of the
Assembly, and in the presence of many of
the members and two policemen, fought till
they had had enough. The two incidents
put a fitting cap on the 14 censure motions
of the past few months and on the flood of
slander and blasphemy which has been
poured out on the floor of the Assembly
during the same time. None of these weari-
some and time-wasting and disgraceful im-
becilities would have occurred had the coun-
try been run on business lines. There is no
commercial concern on earth that could
stand the strain of having its governing
directors perpetually delving into each
other’s private lives, on the off-chance of
finding out something of a frivolous nature;
and in the intervals calling one another liars
and thieves, and hampering each other’s
operations in every way imaginable. How
much longer is Australia going to endure the
spectacle of its various Parliaments being
turned into places where insulting and dis-
gusting language, free fights and criminal
slanders are recognised uncomplainingly as
part of the ordinary business of government?
Or to put it another way: How long will it
be before the electors declare for the aboli-
tion of the system of government which is
to blame for these offences, by the introduc-
tion of elective Ministries?

Jervis Bay and “the People.”
There is something about the atmosphere
of the N.S.W. Legislative Council that
makes men who, under ordinary conditions,
are public-spirited and gifted with horse
sense, talk like the inmates' of a lunatic
asylum. The other night, when the ceding
of certain lands at Jervis Bay to the Com-
monwealth was being discussed, some amaz-
ing piffle was let loose. Of course when the
ancient Dangar said he opposed the cession,
as nothing would ever reconcile him to
Federation, and the whole thing was a dark
conspiracy against N.S.W., nobody was
surprised. But when the same nonsense is
talked by a man like Alt.en Taylor, who>
outside the Council chamber,_ is a man o
large views and progressive ideas,_ and nas
done the community some service, it is piti-
ful. Said Taylor :
Practically the whole of the bay i s heing
for, along with other tracts of land. I bell PU
should do nothing to hamper the Federal “ , th
ment in its efforts to provide for the defence o
Commonwealth. In doing that, however, we
not lose sight of the people’s rights.
Now what is the Commonwealth but tne
People, the whole People and nothing m
the People? Does Allen Taylor think: t«
Commonwealth is a foreign power. »
isn’t it the first right of the people to Haw
the defence of their country, lives and pr
perty provided for? Or does Apia* '
regard the people as consisting one (
small crowd of capitalists who would
grow rich oh the unearned incremen: ,
from the Federal Government s exp
at Jervis Bay? The people for whose rigid
Allen Taylor has such regard nave
cided on an adequate naval defence s .'
They have elected certain men to car

Extraordinary Announcement to Agents.
Christmas Edition of
The Bulletin, 1913.
PLEASE TAKE NOTICE that the
CHRISTMAS NUMBER of “THE
BULLETIN” will be an EXTRA
and SPECIAL ISSUE.
Date of Publication: December 13, 1915.
This SPECIAL NUMBER will con-
tain high-class Stories, Verse and other
Literary Contributions from foremost
Australasian writers.
The Illustrations will be supplied by
the popular Bulletin artists, and
others in the front rank of the artistic
world.
The Ordinary Issue of THE BULLETIN
will be published on the usual date.
Agents are requested to note
that, as the Christmas Number of
“The Bulletin” is now going to
press, the orders that reach us from
now on will be treated in order of

The Color Line.
The Ceylon Morning Leader has been get-
ting excited and protesting vigorously
against the invitation issued to a team of
Australian cricketers to visit the island,
Australia having drawn the color-line and
therefore “affronted” Ceylon. “If it were
a question of purity of race,” says the
curried Leader, “there might be a reason-
able objection to colored immigration; but
this prohibition is directed against the in-
flux of cheap labor.” And therein the
curried Leader is 95 per cent, wrong.
Thirty years ago, when Australia didn’t
know a quarter as much about colored labor
as it does to-day, it thought that sort of
labor was cheap ; now it knows it isn’t. It
has proved that much in the sugar busi-
ness, wherein white labor is giving the em-
ployers vastly more profit than ever yellow
or brown did. And in the tin districts'white
labor, with machinery, is making profits
out of ground that the “cheap” Chow aban-
doned years ago. Finally, the one part of
Australia that lias made no progress what-
ever is the Northern part, where there was
more of this “cheap” labor than anywhere
else in the Commonwealth. So only 5 per cent,
of the Australian objection to colored immi-
gration now rests on the cheap-labor foun-
dation, and that foundation is the only part
of the whole business that is unsound ; the
other 95 per cent, is based on the fact that
the British-Australian race is a white, race,
with white morals and white modes of liv-
ing, a..d it can’t keep white if it admits the
colored foreigner. At bottom is the dread of
the half-breed. How would Ceylon itself
regard a mixture of, say, Cingalee and
Hottentot?
The Overseas Club Foolishness.
English estimates of the attractions of the
Overseas Club give artless expression to the
“imperialism” which is grounded in insular
ignorance. Last week copies of the Daily
Mail came in thousands to Australia, ad-
dressed to members of the Overseas Club,
exhorting them to feed their “imperial”
minds by subscribing to that pushful little
journal. Evidently the Daily Mail regards
the ridiculous Club as a growing force in
these latitudes, whereas it is actually doomed
to perish of cold neglect. In Melbourne,
where the proportion of British-born is much
larger than in Sydney, there has not been
the least enthusiasm for the Overseas Club
idea since its original local supporters began
to understand what sort of brotherhood they
had let themselves in for. Considered as a
mere matter of common interest to Aus-
tralians who are shut out of the A.N.A. —
such as Andrew Fisher, for instance —the
Overseas Club seemed to compare favorably
with the more clannish Caledonian, Hiber-
nian and Cambrian associations ; but when it
revealed itself as an unprotected, piebald
society .to which any bounder could beleng
by paying a shilling or two per annum, or
to which lie could say he belonged whilst
paying nothing at all, the awful absurdity of
the scheme repelled all intelligent sympathy.
Australians who took it up in the first in-
stance, therefore, dropped it like a hot saus-
age. The original honorary office-holders
found themselves expected to hold out the
right hand of fellowship to a motley pro-
cession of immigrants and globe trotters
arriving from distant parts of the Hempire,
on the understanding that the O.C. was a
sort of huge Masonic lodge or labor agency.
These deluded newchums hoped to get a
billet and borrow lialf-a-crown to go on with
as soon as they struck the figureheads of a
beastly kawlonial branch. Moreover, tinted
aliens had been advised to buy the little
bauble of membership when they booked
their passage for Australia; they hung
it on their watch-chain accordingly. It was
the new English recipe for fortune-making
at the far end of that glorious Empire
whereon the sun never sets. No bounder
could be properly equipped without it. So,
even though the Overseas Club had no other
stains upon its character, its easy accessi-
bility to bounders must obviously prove
fatal. If sentiment were trying to keep the
plant alive (which Anglo-Australian senti-
ment certainly isn't trying to do at this
end) the parasites would inevitably kill it.
Private Enterprise on Its Defence.
On October 2, The Bulletin, reviewing
the reports of the State-owned factories at-
tached to the Defence Dept., accepted them
as. further evidence that Private Enter-
prise’s claim to superiority was some-
what premature. Now, comes a letter from
the Adelaide firm of llolden and
Frost claiming that Private Enterprise can
make out a better case in regard, at any
rate, .to military saddlery. Herewith the
essentials of the communication :
In the report of the Federal factory quantities are
supplied of the articles mentioned in The Bulle-
tin. and we include them in this calculation. The
prices which we quote are prices at which we are
prepared to supply to-day. The probability also, as
you point out, is, that with quantities and greater
knowledge, we, equally with the factory, would be
able to supply at still lower figures. As the matter
stands, on the quantities given in the factory report.
The Bulletin figures showed that the factory had
produced at a total of £638 18s. 4d below Private
Enterprise. On figures we append herewith, the
difference is only £35 14s. Bd:

This shows a difference of £35 14V Bd. in favor of

Suppose we take a few more lines. For in-

Saddles, steel arch. . 3109 40/9 38/11
So, on the lines of saddles (above), Private Enter-
prise is shown to be £284 19s. lOd. below factory
costs.
There are a good many points in the report which,
we think, you, with The Bulletin’s noted accuracy
in all matters of figures, should point out.
One is, that the auditor reports that this balance-
sheet is made up on an estimated value of stock in
hand ; and, furthermore, that the report of the Secre-
tary of Defence, which is published at the commence-
ment, states that “Buildings, Machinery, Plant, etc.,
have been subject to various rates of depreciation’’ ;
but these rates of depreciation do not correspond.
I or instance—Buildings are given at £3612 14s. Bd.
and 5 per cent, depreciation as £62 10s. The
same with the other depreciations. The figures ap-
pear to need explanation. As depreciation on
machinery and plant, 13 per cent, is given. These
assets total £1874 4s. 4d., yet the amount written
off is only £39 19s. 7d., which is only about 2 per
cent, instead of 13 per cent.
One big factor in the comparison of values is, of
course, the quality of the goods supplied. It is not
for us to say whether our goods are better than the
factory products, or not; but we think a report
could be obtained. Another very important matter
which should likewise be cleared up is in regard to
the amount of machine work which is put into vari-
ous parts of the harness in the harness factory, and
the hand work which the Private Enterprise contrac-
tors have to put in. The amount, in this direction,
comes to a very serious item indeed in favor of Pri-
vate Enterprise, if we were allowed to use machine
work on the same basis as the factory.
It seems almost an idle boast to say that we are
quite prepared to make the harness and requirements
for the military authorities on an all-round average
of 10 p.c. lower than factory costs, and will guaran-
tee that the quality shall be better.
However, the matter can never be adjusted by
newspaper correspondence; and, if a public inquiry
can be made into the whole matter, in which the
contractors could have an opportunity of using their
special knowledge, we feel quite sure that we could
more than prove the position which we have taken.
The Bulletin did not set out to give an
auditor’s report on the factory statement of
operations. It took the manager’s word
for the disabilities under which the infant
establishment was working, and was then
highly interested to discover an appendix to
the report showing how, in spite of the dis-
abilities, the average factory cost of pro-
duction compared with the average contract
prices during the same period. On the list
of 30 articles quoted, there were 24 where
the factory price was lower than P.E.’s price,
one article where the two ran a dead heat,
and five where P.E. was cheaper that Sosh.
According to Holden and Frost, the total
advantage to Sosh on the few lines of goods
(seven out of 30) quoted in The Bulletin
was only £35 : but what contractor’s figures
are being used in that comparison this paper
does not know. Some of them differ materi-
ally from those shown in the appendix to
the factory report. The totalling of a few
stray lines doesn’t provide a very valuable
comparison, anyhow; but what there is to
it (£35) is, on the calculations of Holden
and Frost, to be credited to Sosh.

Because the State won’t do it, and there
is no Greater Sydney authority that can do
it, a little group of doctors, headed by the
veteran Philip Sydney Jones, is trying to
get enough money into a company to estab-
lish hygienic dairies somewhere near the
place where the House adjourns to watch its
members fight in the library. The small
beginnings of a dairy are already run by
these doctors ; but there isn’t the capital to
either run the business economically or
supply more than an odd customer here and
there. And, until some public authority
takes over the whole supply, and puts the
control of it into the hands of people who
know that dirt isn’t good for milk, and
saves about two-thirds of the present cost
of distribution, and at least one-third of
the racketing which the milk gets, the busi-
ness never will be satisfactory. Naturally,
Dr. Sydney Jones being this State’s chief
campaigner against the white plague, the
little company, which is pluckily trying to
do what some public authority ought to do,
attaches most importance to the supply of
pure milk —not merely milk containing so-
and-so of butter fat, but milk that isn’t a
whole zoo of ravening microbes. As the
report of the Tuberculosis Advisory Board,
presented to the N.S.W. Parliament a while
ago, said, “dirt-contaminated, manure-
polluted and bacteria-laden milk can be re-
tailed so long as its chemical composition is
at least equal to the standard _ adopted,
which is certainly not high.... Milk below
the chemical standard may not be harmful.
Milk grossly laden with bacteria may be,
and generally is, prejudicial to. health.”
This report was presented to Parliament, in
terms of the' commission issued to the Boa-rd ;
but it wasn’t over the wholesale murder
disclosed that members got so excited that
they punched noses in the Parliamentary
library. Why , should Parliament get ex-
cited about such a matter? The victims
are mostly infants, and infants haven’t got
Votes.
The Yic. Railways are up against the ab-
surdity of Melbourne’s restricted Sunday
suburban train service. The Dept, is sup-
p’ying extra forenoon trains to some of the
country beauty spots, and is prepared to
sunply more; but under the present law sub-
urban folk can hardly get into Princes
Bridge to catch these trains, save by walk-
ing , and as only “church” trains into town
are ’permitted on the suburban lines, reason-
ably early “excursions” from town are out
of the question. For which anomaly the
great god Wowser is responsible —the great
god who finds desecration of the Sabbath,
and infringement of Labor’s Day of Rest,
in every train-service except the particular
one which he pses himself. Thus far the
local Labor party has supported him in his
policy of Sunday-joy suppression; but now
that Labor has wages to spend in desecrat-
ing the Sabbath by trjps to the fern dis-
tricts, the party begins to perceive that
public-service workers can quite easily
secure a-rest-some other day.
The very last suggestion for holding a
“Day” is Trafalgar Day, about which a
letter recently apgjdared in a Sydney paper.
This business ! isu becoming so violent that
there will soon have to be two days every
day in order to make room for all the days.
Trafalgar was really a small scrap, as was
Waterloo. There were only 60 sailing ships
engaged, many of them rotten. The event
became famous partly through the signal
that Nelson hoisted, partly through .Nel-
son’s' death, and partly because it was the
last conspicuous howl of a long-dragged-
out naval war in which both paities grew
very poor and very tired. Waterloo was
famous in somewhat the same fashion. It
was the last flurry of a very great land war.
In the times when the combatants were
young and fresh it wouldn’t have looked like
more than a big skirmish, but when they
heaved up their last resources of human meat
for a final effort, it was’decisive. The last
battle at sea is always decisive, even if it is
a fight between a punt and a dredge. And
the last battle on land is the finish, though
there may be only a beadle and a cannon on
one side, and a consumptive and a dog on
the other. Incidentally, why not combine
all the “days” and call the occasion Dog
Day? It is alleged that every dog has one.

The full-powered Tory is not as numerous
as he used to be; but one of him cut loose,
the other day, in the columns of a Sydney
paper. He informed the editor, in a sort of
angry shout, that the Australian worker was
“too expensively housed and too lavishly fed
and clad.” Then, after denouncing him for
knocking off when the bell rang, the troglo-
dyte produced this: —
The incentive to save is destroyed by the prospec-
tive payment of the old age pension, and that fine,
rugged manhood and spirit of independence—once our
boast —have largely disappeared.
That writer, who represents the old school
which made much capital out of “fine,
rugged manhood” and “spirit,” can’t see that
the olden virtues are still on the premises;
the only difference is that the worker is
now making capital out of them for himself.
He is feeding and clothing himself better;
also he is more expensively
that isn’t his fault; the “fine, rugged man-
hood” of the property-owners is seeing to
that part of the business. As regards the
thriftlessness of the allegedly luxurious
worker, it is strange if this lover of good
living is spending everything he gets and
taking no heed of the future just because he
sees the old age pension dole ahead of him.
Even at long range, the pension doesn’t look
as glittering as all that; which no doubt
explains why £13,500,000 more money is
lodged in one N. S. Wales Savings Bank
alone than was there in 1901.
♦
Last week Sydney Labor Council, by re-
scinding a resolution in favor of a cost-of-
living commission, practically decided that
cost of living should not be the standard of
wages. The Labor Council is right; wages
shouldn’t be governed by what a man eats,
but by what he produces. The man who
consumes six meals a day and loafs six
hours out of the eight he is supposed to be
working shouldn’t be entitled to twice as
much as the other man who consumes only
three meals a day and does a fair thing all
his eight working hours. This country has
a dreadful lot of work to do, and the only
way it can get rich is by doing that work ;
and the sooner employers leave off cackling
so much about the cost of living the sooner
they will also be able to leave off cackling
about the slowing-down methods of the
working-man. If the worker is paid accord-
ing to what it costs him to live, why should
he work hard? It’s up to him in that case
merely to eat hard and drink hard and wear
silk socks.
“Bulkhead” : It may seem a distant howl
from the Titanic sinking in the Atlantic to
a barge floating on the Darling River, but
they are connected. When the British
Board of Trade was aroused by the Titanic
tragedy out of a 30 years’ sleep, it hurriedly
passed a new set of life-saving rules, one
of which was that all British ships must
provide a life-jacket for each person carried.
As every five-goatpower dug-out on the Dar-
ling is a British ship, and. as the Govern-
ment of N. S. Wales swallowed, holus
bolus, the Board’s panic legislation, it fol-
lows that a shearer, beer sparrer and even
bishop going from Bourke to Wentworth on
a wool hooker must be assured of a cork
waistcoat. When these rules came into
force it was calculated that the Ma State
would require 250,000 life-jackets, and, as
everv other portion of the Empire that had
gobbled the rules wanted tens of thousands
also, supplies couldn’t be got at once. Just
here the foolery of the business began to
gibber at the gangway and make remarks
like an idiot at the ticket office. Each
jacket costs 45., plus the expense of arrang-
ing its home on board, and this heavy out-
lay has frozen out some of the small steam-
boat men who have been running services
where the Sydney ferries monopoly, for in-
stance, doesn’t reach, such as Botany, Haw-
kesbury and the up-country rivers. But
the lunatic regulation which declines to
allow a commodious, staunch and bulk-
headed steamer to carry passengers unless
a dado of lifebelts is built round her, cheer-
fully permits a cramped, frail motor-launch
to crowd un with travellers, without asking
that one single lifebelt be on board, let alone
the rest of the life-saving outfit that the
steamers must provide. Motor-boats aren’t
even bulkheaded, which is the only real pro-
tection that harbor traffic can be given. If
a Manly boat with 1600 people runs into a
Mosman boat with 1400, the 3000 life-
jackets available won’t be nearly as useful
as the dozen bulkheads that the two steamers
possess between them. It’s a sort of
laughter-raising proposition when you
examine it, but hair-raising is likely to be
its chief end in life.

Westralian Chamber of Mines is wrathful
because Premier Scaddan proposes a Mines
Regulation Act to provide for (1) abolition
of underground night-shift, (2) abolition of
underground contract work, and (3) ap-
pointment by miners themselves of check in-
spectors. The Chamber says such things will
banish British capital and ruin the industry.
Yet two large Kalgoorlie companies, Great
Boulder and Lake View Consols, have volun-
tarily abandoned night-shift, just as the
Broken Hill Prop, did three years ago, so
that for eight hours each day the dust may
settle in the workings and the fume-laden air
be renewed. Also, it is found that men who
take their rest in the natural sleep-time can
do more toil; wherefore one way and another
the companies that work only two shifts are
the gainers. The abolition of contract work
is more debatable. Brolcep Hill miners, for
instance, though they struck against it once,
would strike still harder now if it weTe pro-
posed to abolish it. As a rule, it is the best
system for the workers when the demand for
labor is keen and the supply limited, and it
is the poorest when the market is glutte'd by
unorganised men willing to cut oae another’s
throats for a crust. As to the check inspec-
tors, all the world over they are employed in
coal mines, and only a Westralian Chamber
of Mines man could see why thev should not
be employed in metal mines. Broken Hill
has had the system for years; and, though
managers were restive under it at first, they
see now that it relieves them of a lot of awk-
ward responsibility regarding the safety of:
the workings.

There is a vast mass of local color in the
recently-circulated appeal on behalf of the
Veterans’ Home, located in the old battery
at Bare Island, which was constructed by
an allegedly Liberal Government of N. S.
Wales with borrowed money to defend
Botany Heads. For example :
Great Britain has established her hospitals—
Chelsea for the army, and Greenwich for the naval
veterans—and our sister State, Victoria, has a cre-
ditable veterans’ home —the United Service Home,
at Drysdale. .. .Surely Sydney will not allow her-
self to lag behind Victoria in helping the men who
are willing to help themselves.
Here we have (1) the dragging in of the
Ma-land, (2) the usual blighted ignorance
as to the real position in that distant spot.
Only the most extraordinary influence can
get a disabled soldier admitted to Chelsea
Hospital, while Greenwich Hospital was, in
1869, turned into a college for the higher
education of naval officers, and the old, dis-
abled sailors were pensioned off or sent to
the workhouse, according to circumstances.
At the present time Great Britain has only
one hospital of the sort proposed for Aus-
tralia, and it would have lost even that if
its managing Board had not been chargedl
with the working of the entire army-pensioni
system. The clarion call to “Sydney” (New/
South Wales is swept aside as negligible))
not to be outdone by Victoria is also char-
mingly characteristic; likewise the request!
to the State Government that it should!
grant land for a site for a specially-built
veterans’ home, and d—n the expense, with
the patriotic object of putting Victoria’s
nose out of joint. Meanwhile the much
more pertinent question arises: When is the
Commonwealth going to begin thinking
about an army and navy pension scheme
such as other civilised countries enjoy?
“Bad Australian’s” sermon from Chili: —
Chile is some 2700 miles long, but its width is
only 90 or 100 miles. Most of it consists of road
metal, with some rich deposits of nitrate, copper,
silver and borax in the northern provinces. The
cultivable land is practically all comprised in the
estuaries and narrow valleys of the rivers which flow
from the Andes to the Pacific across the southern
half of the country. The population is something
under 4,000,000, but it is solid on one thing—the
necessity for being able to hang on to what it has
got. Finding, when accounts were settled after the
unpleasantness with Peru and Bolivia some years
ago, that being ready panned out a good dividend,
Chile set itself to the task of becoming still readier.
It bought more modern warships and guns, some of
them with the money torn off the fellow who wasn’t
ready; it acquired more up-to-date outfits for its
troops, mostly in the way of magazine rifles and
mountain batteries; it imported British and German,
instructors for the navy and army; and, altogether,,
after a lot of hard work on the universal training;
system, it has knocked together about the smartest;
fighting force on land or sea in South America.
And still not feeling satisfied, this one-horse strip)
of backbone has set itself to build a railway from
one end of the rock to the other. The greater part
of it is nothing more than a military road, for prac-
tically the whole of the northern section runs
through about the most desolate country on earth,
without a vestige of vegetation or animal life, an«s
where rain has been unknown within the memory of
the oldest inhabitant. Such a railroad is not the
soft snap in the way of construction that Aus-
tralia’s straight line to the Territory would be. On
one of the lower sections .about 40 miles of rock
(Abt system) is used, no other- being practicable
owing to the wild nature of the country. Farther
north, where the line traverses the great desert of
Atacama, the conditions of life are simply hor-
rible ; but the cheerful patriot faces it all without
the least idea that he is doing anything out of the
common. The only products of this northern region
are minerals; and even these will be of little use
to the line, as the fields are already served by
numerous short spurs to the Pacific, where the pro-
ducts connect with water carriage. The northern
section is 440 miles long and has been completed at
a cost of £3,055,750. The southern section of 600
miles will be ready by the end of the year. There
will then remain about 100 miles to link up t<v
Tacna, a short distance from the Peruvian frontier.t-
and when that is complete Chile will be able ,to>
deliver the goods on its own territory or .'that ‘ of
Peru without any of the annoyance it suffered from
the Peruvian warships during the last little trouble.
Let my countrymen and their Chilian-born ad
viser, Chris. Watson, paste this in their hats, and
drop skiting about their vast territory,/'their- enter-
prise and their superiority to anything with the
streak of the tarbrush in it, and get busy on the
Long North Road before the other brown man, whc -
is ready, drops in with his gun and his general un- .
thinkableness.
“Lex” on the Law’s vagaries:—
There is no direction in which the Law is more oi '
an inconsistent Ass than in its handling of perjury
cases. The writer, while Crown-defending at Dubbo.
(N.S.W.) eight or 10 years ago, called a witness to.
prove that an accused person was at, or' 1 near, a cer-
tain farm on a certain date. The witness-Was a typi-
cal good-class cocky, a citizen who had never before,
probably done an illegal action in his life. To save
his erring friend, he said that which transpired later
to be untrue —or, anyhow, the jury, hy returning an,
adverse verdict against the accused, declared it to.,
be untrue. Within the space of less than an hour-
this luckless man, who had driven into court that:
morning without a stain on his name, was indicted!
for perjury, tried, convicted and sentenced (Chief."
justice Darley officiating) to five years’ penal servi-
tude. The other side of the picture was presented im
Victoria last week when Justice Hodges dealt witts

The Greater J. D. Williams Amusement Co., Ltd. W. Barrington Miller, General Manager-
a case wherein a plaintiff in a land dispute admitted
in the witness box that certain statements made by
her in a statutory declaration in connection with the
property at issue were false. His Honor was shocked;
also, he was more than shocked on finding that there
were many documents in the case “all of a like
nature, and made by different persons.” Further: “He
was not calling attention to the matter with a view
to having the person alluded to prosecuted; if that
were his intention he would deal with the case him-
self .... But persons who made statements of this kind
were liable to 10 to 15 years’ imprisonment, and he
certainly thought the attention of the Minister of
Lands should be called to the matter.” But why
not the attention of the Attorney General? To my
mind, and I fancy to the minds of most people, the
person who lies in a court of law to save a woman’s
honor or a mate’s liberty is much less reprehensible
than the one who swears falsely in order to gain
some material advantage—a block of the public land,
for example, which is highly liable to be gained at
the expense of some more deserving but less dishonest
applicant.

Apparently Hans Sauerkraut, who was a
confirmed landsman 60 years ago, has really
learned the craft of the sea. When 10
steamers of mixed nationalities rushed, in
answer to the S.O.S. call, to the aid of the
burning Volturno, in mid-Atlantic, on a
dark night full of storms, it was Wilhelm
Bludwiirst who did the best. Out of 521
passengers or thereabouts who were saved he
dragged off 231.
A case with a bearing on Australia’s big-
gest political and social question was heard
in the Perth City Court the other day, when
“a fashionably-dressed, educated Japanese
named Yosheda was charged with permitting
his private house to be used for Flossie pur-
poses. The defence consisted almost wholly
of an attempt to prove that Yosh was a very
top-notch Japanese—he had been consular
representative in Westralia, and had only
the other day been honored with an invita-
tion to an important public function. No
doubt it was all true. But the fact remained
that, in spite of Yosh’s exceptional Jap
respectability, the Court found him guilty of
the Flossie offence in the private house
where his wife lived, and fined him £2O.
Which shows that even the tremendous
respectability of a consular agent doesn’t
prevent the Jap doing things which the
white person who isn’t more than a bit re-
spectable reckons horrible.
A few notes about gun-food and other
naval curiosities:—
The 12-inch shells, great, black, greasy cones that
look much too large for the gun-muzzles, are kept
in steel bins in the quiet, electrically-lighted maga-
zine below the turret. It is a still atmosphere, and
the low voices of the sailors, juggling the big, blunt-
nosed things with a hand tackle, are clear and dis-
tinct. One big shell knocks softly against another;
they look like affectionate young pigs in a sty. The
loading tray on which the projectile and flannel-
wrapped cordite" charges are delivered by the tackle,
is on a circular rack which follows the base of the
revolving turret around. Once in the slot, it flies
up at the touch of a lever to the open breech of the
gun—the big, shining breech that is opened with a
whistling shriek of compressed air—and is shoved
far down the barrel by the big, jointed, mechanical
rammer that uncoils, with a deafening rattle, does
its work in one wriggling thrust, and runs back out
of the way. That jointed rammer is one of the sur-
prises of the turret working. The sweating sailor
in the magazine explains with pride how an auto-
matic bolt in the shoot-door prevents the over-curious
dispenser of projectiles from putting his silly head
into ’ the shoot at the w.rong instant and getting
neatly 'guillotined. It is one of the minor risks of
his job, and, apparently, he regards the safety device
as a bonus.
They distinguish the different sorts of shells by
the colored paint rings they wear round their snouts.
The shrapnel, which is laden with steel balls about
the size of a watch, is designed to burst fan wise.
(“Meant for us.e, sir, against infantry crossin’ a
bridge.” One feels sorry for the infantry—whoever
they may be.) The lower conning “tower,” where
the ship is fought from after things above go to
smash, suggests an iron bear-pit fitted with a few
telephones and things. When the time comes, there
will be weird contrivances there for the commander
to see with; but it will be rather like fighting
shadows in another world—-a resounding world of
bare steel, flame and horror.
The doctor is very proud of some light canvas and
bamboo stretchers in which a man may be parcelled
up neatly like a mummy and passed through man-
holes and down hatchways. The special X-ray appar-
atus, in a case about as big as a harmonium, is a
special exhibit of the sick bay. It is a highly
decorative machine, beautifully plated and finished.
Two twinkling spring rolls of metallic gold braid
serve instead of the usual cumbersome electrical con-
nections. The job of the two doctors, when the ship
is in action, is to stay well under cover in their
electrically-lighted steel cells, listen patiently to the
hell-noises, see nothing, and wait for a lull. In the
Russo-Jap trouble, the medical staff of one ship were
the only members of the ship’s company who were
killed ; they went looking for work too soon.
The steel-floored engine-rooms, to which you go
down by a lift, are all gauges, dials, telephones and
steam pipes. There are no masses of grey, naked
steel to look at—no giant cranks or pistons shining
sleekly with oil. “McAndrew’s Hymn,” with its
reverent Scotch references to connecting rods, throws
and such, will have to be re-written or scrapped to
suit the compact, hooded turbines that are really
enclosed steam windmills.
Another slide downward with the engineer-lieuten-
ant, and the humming fires of the stokehold are
gleaming behind their doors. It is hot, but the heat
is not the stifling, stale heat of a crowded and badly*
ventilated theatre; the draught of the fires and the
ventilating fans keep the atmosnhere fresh, though
you are below the waterline and enclosed by hot
steel. One doesn’t realise how far behind the hygienic
times some theatres are until he has visited the
Australia’s stokehold.
