The Quinlan Opera Co. opened its season
in Sydney with “The Mastersingers of
Nuremberg” in Her Majesty’s. “If we wish
to know what Wagner means, we must
fight our way through his drama to his
music,” thunders the voice of Bayreuth.
The German composer is popularly sup-
posed to have written “The Mastersingers”
as a comic pendant to the minnesingers in
“Tannhauser.” The comedy is so labored in
the “pendant ” that we must fight our way
over the bodies of the Nuremberg appren-
tices to much of the magnificent orchestra-
tion in the first act. There is very little
chorus-singing for these “comic” youths—
certainly not enough to justify their per-
sistent horseplay while Conductor Eckhold
is drawing siren sounds from his fiddlers
and wood-winds. Town-Clerk Beckmesset - ,
as sketched by Wagner, is the low-comedy
gent, who is expected to make the audience
laugh by singing out of tune. On Saturday
bight the front of the house dawdled in
late—some of the galleryites had been wait-
ing since four in the afternoon—and could
give no more than a sickly smile to Beck-
messer. Yet Dever made the best of this
very “sick” part. Eva. the goldsmith’s
daughter, is the prize. For her sake Beck-
messer bawls like Romeow on the back
fence in any suburb. For her, also,
does tenor Spencer Thomas, as Wal-
ther, bellow in tune. What he sings
cannot be of much consequence, for he
might be doing it in Hottentot so far as the
audience is any the wiser. Thomas, how-
ever, is acceptable as a pleasing tenor with
a rounder and more extensive organ than
he obliged us with in 1932.
“ THE MAS IE RSINGE RS,” AT HER
MA TESTY'S, SYDNEY.
The tuneful cobbler, Sacks (Robert Parker),
deserts his-last to help Spencer Thomas sing
his way into the heart of Eva.
Jeanne Brola, the Eva of Saturday
night’s production, has too light a soprano
to contend with the full blast of a Wag-
nerian orchestra. Mabel Dennis, in the
small part of Magdalene, was more articu-
late than most of the company. The new
tenor, Maurice D’Oislv. as the rotund ap-
prentice David, has fine, healthy lung-
power, and downed the orchestra quite
twice. Baritone Parker, the poet cobbler-
visionary' Hans Sachs, is the central figure.
Wagner, as everyone knows, came under
the influence of Schopenhauer; and Act
111. in this long-winded music-drama is
always pointed at as showing the trail of
Schopenhauer in Sach’s half-mile of mono-
logue. Parker was good but not great as
the warbling cobbler. “The Mastersingers,”
which takes about 44 hours to drag itself
to a terminus, has a male cast of 15. This
does not include the apprentice chorus. The
orchestra, however, has the star turn, and
its turn is going on all the time. The over-
ture was a superb sample of Wagnerian
music conducted by a Wagnerian expert—
the bushy-whiskered Eckhold. The second
act has a prelude, which was one of the
most subtle imitative efforts of the com-
poser, reproducing the sleepy twitter of a
feathered choir at dusk. Unfortunately, the
singing tones of the fiddles were stamped
on by the stalled ox (returning from his in-
terval over an adjacent whisky) till the
gallery said “Hush —sh !”
* * * *
Having on Saturday given Wagner in
“The Mastersingers” a chance to poke borak
at the Italian school of opera, Quinlan did
the amiable on Monday by producing “Rigo-
letto,” thereby allowing the Italian school
to speak in its own defence. But “Rigo-
letto” isn’t a very imposing defence: no
jury would be so far carried away as to
pass the prisoner at the bar a vote of thanks
after acquitting him. This mustn’t, how-
ever, be misunderstood. There are patches
in the defence which stir the audience;
wherefore on Monday night the confirmed
grand-opera enthusiast flinched, and once
actually writhed, when applause broke in
on the proceedings and hung up the pro-
gress of the story. On the noisiest outburst
Miss Felice Lyne, making that evening her
first appearance in Sydney, tripped back on
to the stage, and did some smiling curtsies,
whereas the interruption really called for
incurable deafness. Apparently the audi-
ence, recognising three or four things that
operatic sopranos and tenors have made
familiar, fancied it was back again at a
concert hall; and this attitude was pre-
served while the orchestra was doing some
of its best work—when nobody was sing-
ing it was taken as a hint that everybody
might chatter, and ask who the man in the
front row of the d.c. was: the man in the
grey sac-suit, conspicuous among the other-
wise-black human beetles. As a matter of
fact, it was Syd Kidman, “the cattle king,”
and a king can wear what he likes. The
male chorus sang notably well, and even
acted with vigor; Miss Lyne, though her
voice has no very large qualities, managed
it admirably, especially in the familiar
“Caro Nome” number; Maurice D’Oisly’s
equally familiar “Donna e Mobile” (very
well done into English) was sung with
grace and spirit; and Samuell, in the name
part (Rigoletto is a hunchback jester, who
is overwhelmed with all the tragedy), pro-
duced again the voice which so pleased
Sydney in the former Quinlan season. And
he did more than sing his numbers: he
acted dramatically throughout. The lesser
people were, on the whole, well up to the
average of a performance which, since it
gives the orchestra no great opportunities,
will not jump to the front when the season
is being recalled three months hence.
“The Cingalee” is having a week’s revival
at Sydney Royal. It is full of bright music,
some of which has become frayed and tat-
tered through being too “catchy,” though
other parts of the score, including some of
the concerted numbers, retain a lot of their
original charm and freshness. Andrew
MacCunn’s instrumentalists on Saturday
night made them all worth listening to.
Some of the singers backed MacCunn
up and the choruses were generally
satisfactory; but the orchestra did most
of the work. The English “book” has
crow’s-feet round the eyes, and the
lyrical references to the smartness of the
Bayswater girl and other complete strangers
leave the modern Australian audience cold.
The feature dancing, apart from Miss Arun-
dale’s contribution, is uninteresting. The
mounting is adequate, but the ventilation
of the dress circle isn’t. The writer
sampled the battle-cruiser Australia’s stoke-
hold when the ship was at anchor (accord-
ing to the engineer-lieutenant, its very hot-
test phase), and he prefers it to the dress
circle of Sydney Royal as it was on Saturday
night. When a theatre is as stuffy as that,
the best part of the entertainment is the
interval. It seems injudicious on the part
of any theatrical management to make the
free open-air seem so attractive. Next
Saturday evening, the co. will start to fare-
well with “Dorothy.”

Another profitable Sydney season of “On
Our Selection” will be concluded to-morrow
night at the Palace, after which Dad will
take the family on a tour of N. S. Wales,
commencing at Newcastle on Saturday. No
other piece will be played, owing to that
old martinet, General Desire, having made
himself so prominent in the matter.

Doody and Wright are the latest to add
to the gaiety of nations at the Tivoli; their
comedy sketch doesn’t take long to “get
there,” and when it does get there it stays.
Ditto the turns of the two Mullers, “hoop
spinners extraordinary”; Diamond and
Beatrice, the instrumentalists; skaters
Reynolds and Donegan, and Fanton’s
athletes.

By the time this journal meets its clients
this week, the hand of desolation will have
removed from the National (Sydney) Lalie
Moore, Gladys and Alber, Hilary Long (who
conducts his business affairs while upside
down), and Sister Carrie Moore. It is a
fell swoop, but the Hubanik Gelli co. and
four other new acts are relied upon to dis-
perse the gloom. The H.G. lot will hand out
“tabloid” grand opera, which seems sensible
in these long-drawn nights of Marathon
opera.
* * * *
“The Last Days of Pompeii” at the
Lyceum is awarded the cinematographic
bun for remarkable snapshots. Up to the
present the Roman amphitheatre scenes in
“Quo Vadis” headed the list; but Spencer’s
latest beats them, though the picture as a
whole doesn’t approach the other. But the
scene wherein the thousands demand the
blood of the alleged murderer; and the one
where a still more tremendous crowd in the
amphitheatre await the lions’ feed; and
finally the eruption of Vesuvius (taken dur-
ing one of the volcano’s recent lapses), and
the subsequent destruction of the city—these
settle its claim to be a very remarkable
picture.

Of another paint show:—
It is on at the Royal Art Society’s Rooms, Sydney,
and comprises 80 canvases by Australian artist F..
Phillips Fox, who is back from Britain and the rest of
Europe after an absence of several years. No art lover
or student should miss this show. Fox has hung sam-
ples of his work that have had the approval of the
Royal Academy, Paris Salon, International, Salon
d’Automne and other famous galleries from 1905 to
1913. The freshness and vivacity of his color sense

The “Walter Bentley” Players are billed
to give their usual monthly performance to-
night (Thursday), at St. James’ Hall,
Phillip-street, Sydney. The varied pro-
gramme is made up of five complete sketches
and a scene from Bulwer Lytton’s “Riche-
lieu.”
The trouble with “Henry V.,” late of
Sydney, but just now living the loud, spec-
tacular life at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), is
the same old trouble which discounted the
charm of George Rignold’s memorable per-
formances in the long ago. What little
human interest the play possesses is frit-
tered away by the interruptions of the tab-
leau curtain, which practically divide it
into eight or 10 acts. The occasional ex-
planatory discourses of Chorus are further
wastes of time, although Miss Madge Tith-
eradge makes Chorus a very pleasing young
person, and her elocution is, for the most
part, quite admirable. An audience is not
gripped by this revival of Shakspeare’s
patchy jingo play, nor would Her Majesty’s
comparatively shallow stage be a favorable
pitch for its pomp and circumstance under
the best of acting conditions. Many of the
performers in this production are uncon-
vincing, as, for instance, the youthful-look-
ing Constable of France, the somewhat
artificial Pistol, the Governor of Harfleur
and several persons of exalted rank on the
programme. The supes. put no suggestion
of warlike spirit into their storming of
Harfleur. and when their picturesque leader,
Lewis Waller, speaks his large, heroic lines,
ope is reminded that Eric Maxon’s mimicry
has preceded him, even as many an original
vaudeville songster from Britain has been
already made familiar to Australian ears
by somebody who had pinched his songs
and vocal mannerisms. Miss Titheradge is
charming in the insipid character of Kath-
erine ; and Titheradge pdre, Halliwell
Hobbes, Quartermaine, Halley and others
are placed as they were when The Bulle-
tin noticed the show in Sydney. It will be
limited to two weeks at Her Majesty’s.
Then Lewis Waller and Co. will shift to the
Royal and doff their plumed helms to “A
Butterfly on the Wheel.”
* * * *
“Excuse Me,” published for the first of
12 nights at Melbourne Royal on Saturday,
is a mild tale of revelry in an Amurkan
overland express, relying more for effect on
eccentricity than on story interest. A
series of slight and disconnected episodes,
definitely erotic in purpose, present a
fraudulent air of novelty in the vitiated
atmosphere of a Pullman car. Jimmy Wel-
lington, a diligent seeker of drinks, has
taken a first-class ticket to Reno, apparently
with the object of dissuading Mrs. Jimmy
from securing one of Reno’s hurried divorces.
The fact that his missus is off to that vil-
lage by the same vehicle as her disorderly
property is a happy accident, which soon
leads to the two burying their past ani-
mosity in a fresh rapprochement. This
goes on side by side with the amorous pro-
ceedings of Ira Lathrop and Henry Mallory
and the respective damsels of their desire.
Lathrop’s affair is merely the casual reunion
of long-separated canoodlers, but Mallory’s
case is peculiar, inasmuch as. having rushed
his donah into a false nosition, he has to
announce the lady as Mrs. Mallory, and
then run over the passenger list in search of
a parson to make them legally and per-
manently one. Mallory’s unsuccessful hunt
for a holy man —for the only one on the
train is heavily disguised in a red necktie and
a bad cigar—with the humors of a couple of
train robbers, a nigger porter, and a tender-
foot Englishman, is regarded as sound and
powerful reason why “Excuse Me” should
be mistaken for the last word in “screams.”
Three acts of it are plenty, and the inclu-
sion of a long and irrelevant monologue by
Fred Niblo, in the second chapter, is an
obvious attempt at padding the weakest part.
Fred Niblo has less to do in this diversion
than in some others, but he acts Harry
Mallory with great verbal vivacity, whilst
getting crushing effects with his nicely-
brushed hair and well-creased clothes. Bob
Greig pictures Jimmy Wellington as a thirsty
imposture with most of his refreshers run-
ning to his feet, and E. Pirie Bush draws
Harold Wedgewood as a spectacle of aristo-
cratic English civilisation appalled by the
barbarism of modern Amurka. John De
Lacy plays Ira Lathrop with the full
strength of his baritone, Ray Royce’s Dr.
Temple is amiably undistinguished, and Fred
Cambourne’s Sambo, if anything, suffers
from a rush of intellect to the head. Miss
Beatrice Holloway is a success as Marjorie
Newton and Miss Marion Clarke’s Mrs. Wel-
lington has some quality, but every one of
the other ladies is gracefully unequal to the
demands of her part.
** * *
No piece of extravagance staged in Mel-
bourne of late years has approached the
superlative absurdity of “Queen of the
White Slaves,” at the Princess’s. A mild-
mannered Prince of Foochow, familiarly
known as Fong Low, is the moving spirit
in this series of practical jokes. Ralph
Grimes, a San Francisco bad man, furnishes
hong’s harem with the necessary ladies.
Grimes’s agency at ’Frisco is a Barbary
Coast Concert Hall, where eight flappers
g ggle the giddy hours away and opium
fiends drop in for a smoke. In this strong-
hold of iniquity Grimes and his donah take
their orders from the genial Low, but for-
tunately Cyril Mackay, disguised as Bert
the Detective, conspires with a wandering
sailor and a small newsgirl to thwart the
blighter’s schemes. All of them get to
China in the next act by various means,
save the boss opium fiend. He is dropped
overboard from Fong’s yacht and left to die
on a raft, detective Bert having rescued
him from the angry waves whilst his beau-
teous sister was being abducted on the
quarter-deck. After which the lady joins
the flappers in the palace gardens
at_ Foochow and is condemned to see her
faithful Bert torn limb from limb by order
of the Terrible Nine, who appear to be
mostly the same eight flappers hooded in
black saddle-cloths and numbered like
starters for the Caulfield Cup. The tribunal
of the Terrible Nine is too dimly lighted to
admit of their identity being sworn to, but
their silence throws a terrible doubt upon
their sex. Anyhow, the villain’s donah and
another girl pop up in time to prevent
Cyril Mackay from being thrown into a
burning, fiery cellar. As a result of this
unwarrantable interference Cyril and the
Queen of the White Slaves are immured
in Fong’s dungeon with the moving walls,
where they would be squeezed flat but for
the wandering sailor, the opportune news-
girl and the unaccountable donah. Their
friends in need pull them through a hole in
the roof, thus bringing the pri.son manage-
ment of the Terrible Nine into richly-
merited contempt. Having escaped from
all the torture chambers, these remarkable
American citizens capture the harem, where
the flappers are, and everything ends
funnily.
J. S. Mann’s scenery is more helpful to
the entertainment than the acting of the
co. Mackay, Vox, Leigh, Misses Fergusson
and Helen Fergus and others struggle
through their parts in a comparatively quiet
manner, whereas they ought to regard the
job as a pantomime and chuck themselves
about. Humorous opportunities go begging
for sympathetic treatment. However, the
Willoughby management, by introducing the
Royal Banzai Family into the gardens of
Fong Low’s Palace, has given the alleged
drama the attraction of a fine vaudeville
show. The Banzai Japs are the quickest,
slickest and most wonderful novelty acro-
bats, balancers and foot-workers the Com-
monwealth has experienced. The brilliant
success of their “turn” puts the amazing
feats of the Terrible Nine narrative into the
shade.
Temple Harrison ,
the detective.
“THE CONFES-
SION." AT
MELBOURNE
KING’S.
Charles Brown as
Michael Grogan,
the Sexton.
“The Confession” is to go for another
week or even longer at the King’s.
Every evening until further notice Father
Roberts will have to sit silent in the stage
police-court whilst the murderer to whom
he gave absolution swears that he saw his
riverence’s brother do the awful deed.
Roberts makes a very good attempt to look
sorry for everybody, including himself and
the guilty liar, under the mental strain of
this ordeal, although he knows quite well
that Ananias Cain will put things right in
the next act. The court scene, in fact, is
discreetly played by all persons present, and
it must strike the King’s public as a much
more reasonably constructed scene of its
kind than usually comes their way.
** * *
The Corson Clarke Co., reinforced by a
new arrival from America, will start to
argue the question “What Did Tomkins
Do?” at the Bijou on Saturday.
The Prosecuting Attorney (R. H. Stephenson).
Saturday, the 18th, is the date appointed
for Gregan McMahon’s one more production
of “Strife.” Galsworthy’s play is now being
rehearsed anew at the National Amphi-
theatre, at the top of Bourke-street, where
it will get almost as much stage room as it
deserves. On the same night Branscombe
will start a new company of Dandies at his
Arcadia, on St. Kilda Esplanade. Another
matter which The Bulletin has been de-
sired to mention in this issue is the forth-
coming visit to Melbourne of John McCor-
mack and his concert co. McCormack’s
mellifluous tenor will be heard at the Audi-
torium on Thursday, 23rd, and three sub-
sequent occasions.
** * *
Frank Fox and Miss Bonnie Leonard
made their first appearance at Melbourne
Opera House on Saturday. Mr. Fox be-
longs to the same active school of humor as
Lillie Langtry and Austin Rudd, and Miss
Leonard, billed as “The Girl with a Million
Smiles,” passably warbles a couple of songs
without furnishing any evidence in support
of her title. Tom Dawson is once more
opening his countenance for a living; Wil-
liams and Rankin still tootle effectively on
brazen instruments; and the Russian
Dancers, being on the brink of departure,
will be but graceful memories shortly after
this paragraph puts on its pink morning
wrapper and goes out to see what the world
is like.
** * *
At Melbourne Olympia on Saturday
Charles Spencer biographed the crimes of a
party named Fantomas (understood to be a
misprint for Fantod), a most elusive
miscreant. Fantod baffles the expert
intellects of the Parisian C.1.D., and
whenever he seems in imminent danger of
capture, he simply fades away, leaving
naught behind but a whiff of smoke and the
echo of his deep “Ha! Ha!” Fantod is a
warm favorite at Spencer’s at present, but
the crowd is expressing considerable sym-
pathy for a potentate named Tony, who,
having pawned his best girl for 150 dollars,
cannot raise the coin to redeem the beau-
teous and buxon* pledge.
* $ *
In a successful second week at Adelaide
Royal is “The Grafters,” the unconventional
singing-play which hasn’t any hero or hero-
ine, but has, instead, Some naive advertise-
ments of a coming Exhibition and of a
brand of whisky. Miss St. Claire scores in
a kind of Palotta part, and Miss Le Blanc
is a fine low-comedian. The male humorists,
La Blanc, Burgess, Nowlin and Vack, are
all good, but all very deliberate and long-
drawn-out, in the modern American style.
So are the Hoboes, still at the Tivoli, which
sumptuous new house has a strong and well-
balanced programme. Miss Willette Whit-
aker is immensely popular, and the trans-
parent but quite discreet dressing-room
of the eight Saxones lends a novelty to their
dancing turn. Jack Prince has an engaging
personal touch on the fiddle: Marini is here
again to sing, and Sydney Bach to “enter-
tain.” At the King’s are Decars, acrobats:
Max Duffek, who makes perfectly good
music while tying himself in knots; and a
lot of clever new American people. The
Waterloo film at West’s brings out some
historical points—among them that both
Wellington and Napoleon spent the greater
part of the eventful day indoors. It is a
fine, stirring, regardless-of-expense picture.
Wondergraph Co. has finished its long lease
of the Town Hall, but is not content with its
finely-appointed new Hindley-street house,
and, stimulated probably by the hottest
October day for 60 years, is showing also in
its open-air corner on North Terrace.
Among many concerts, prominent have been
a Brewster Jones piano recital at the Exhi-
bition and an amateur Variety evening, of
great social tonnage, at Glenelg.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Lewis Waller is going to fly in the face of Mel-
bourne precedent by giving a matinee performance of
“Henry V.” on Caulfield Cup Saturday. It will be
a special mat. for schools and colleges, earnest young
students and their tutors being supposed to have
minds far above horse-racing. But why not advertise
that King Henry will announce the winner’s name
at the Battle of Agincourt? Probably even a few
lovers of the Bard would like to know the fate of
their five bob.
From S’Africa: Aubrey Dennistoun Hoad, elder
son of the late General, will tour Australia in 1914
with his own company. “The Flail of Fate,” “Path-
finders,” “Drake” and “Dorian Gray” will be in the
repertoire. Hoad resumed his original part in “Kis-
met” when Oscar Asche and Lily Brayton opened in
Johannesburg. Sir Herbert Tree retained Hoad for
the “Othello” production at His Majesty’s, London,
which prevented the young Australian actor touring
Australia with the Asche-Brayton combination.
There will be some hustling in Australian theatres
next year. The Tait Brethren have made a specula-
tion in drama by purchasing Florence Barclay’s “The
Rosary”: Beaumont Smith, in conjunction with
Mayer, of the London Strand Theatre, will offer Rex
Beach’s “The Barrier” ; Carroll, of Queensland, will
tour a panto with Florrie Forde (warbling in the
English ’Alls for some years) as chief boy; Wil-
loughby also insists on a pantomime; and “The Glad
Eye,” the revue “Come Over Here,” and Anderson’s
“Land of Nod” will introduce new cos. Added to
these will be a sure Lauder and a possible Pavlova.
The one lone, likely exportation is “On Our Selec-
tion,” which may go to the Big Smoke.
“The Second Footman”: Never in all my experi-
ence of service have there been such excursions and
alarms in connection with ballroom dances as there were
during the festive period that has just passed Sydney by.
In the first place there was the two-sfep pro-
blem ; and the position was exacerbated, later, by
the uprising of the one-step menace. The moment
the ball began, it became all too plain to those
whose dreary job it is to stand and wait up against
walls for the advent of supper, that the younger mem-
bers of Society had been squandering their leisure
in the acquisition of the new steps. As the unre-
generate young people fled by, their feet moving in
a way distinctly different from that which was the
vogue in Good Prince Albert’s time, low moans of
“dreadful” and “shocking” issued from every wall.
The culminating point was reached at Government
House. If you want to see the Bunny Hug done with
genuine enthusiasm and faithfulness to pattern, go
to the more distant suburbs—Summer Hill, Stan-
more, and so on. Most of the girls who attended the
Stricklands’ big ball came from afar, and many of
them gave expositions of the new movement that
stirred the non-dancing section to frenzy. It was
decided by a number of elderly matrons that the
thing must be stopped—just as Queen Mary came
to a similar decision in London recently. Individual
girls were warned, objurgated, implored. Without
the least avail, however. One reason was that all
the programmes of all the dances contained hardly
anything else but two-steps and one-steps. The idea
Of those responsible apparently was that the band
should play one thing and the guests should dance
another. The spectacle of people waltzing in a
puzzled, broken, miserable sort of way to syncopated
airs like the “Gaby Glide” and “Robt. E. Lee,” was
amply sufficient to debar those who knew the steps
nominally associated with such musical efforts from
imitating the waltzers’ example. The only way to
abolish the one-step is to wipe it off the programme.
Sq long as one-steps are played, those who don’t want
to either look foolish or sit down will dance them.
The management of the “From Manager to Cross”
film writes from Melbourne: That excellent critic,
“0.K.,” makes a few comments regarding our film
(B. 9/10/’l3). Says he: “An introductory speech
is delivered by an elderly gentleman in a frock coat
and a German accent.” Just let us ' say that
each evening a different clergyman introduces the
film ; and, as our aim is to avoid sectarianism, these
clergymen represent all denominations and religious
bodies.
Just dead in Germany, Smaun Singh Hpoo, aged
31, the midget black brother who was recently on
show in Australia. His exact weight was 261 b., and
he drew a salary equal to 255. per lb. per week. A
sister a few ounces lighter dropped dead on the
stage of New York Hippodrome a few years ago,
leaving behind an insurance policy for £6OOO. Hpoo,
who was a really accomplished gymnast, was located
in Burmah, and introduced to the show business by
Max Weltzien, formerly skipper on an Australian
coastal boat.

TWO VETERANS.
[For The Bulletin.]
They sing their youthful love! Yet he is bald
And comfortably fat! He shouts and waddles.
Her corset strings, observe, are tightly hauled!
She gasps and totters and she pants and toddles.
Her armor can with straining just encompass
The pillow chest with which she makes a rumpus.
He calls her “Dear,” and strokes her bulging arm;
But fat and middle-age half choke his passion.
She flies en masse from him. Her mock alarm
Perturbs him terribly. In tender fashion
He waddles after, crying hell-for-leather,
To clasp the stays that hold his love together.
She turns! She falls on him! No, no, she leans!
(Some fourteen stone of corseted affection
Would be too much to hold for lengthy scenes.)
They step off gaily in the same direction!
The audience applauds them for the science
With which they shout at Age and Fat defiance.
N.s.w.

From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Apropos Shell Cove (B. 9/10/’l3). F.' H. Grundy,
in his “Pictures of the Past,” says it was called
Murderer’s Bay at one time. Grundy lived in bache-
lor’s quarters there. It was also known as Hungry
Bay. On the Cremorne side there is a 100 ft. reser-
vation, but on the Neutral Bay side the water front
is alienated—to the great joy of one owner, who
recently sold some of it at £25 per foot.
The other evening a citizen who chanced
to be supporting a post in Chapel-street,
P’ran (Vic.), observed the approach of what
looked like a flaming devil bolting from the
vengeance of Beelzebub, his chief. It ulti-
mately turned out to he a motor-cyclist with
his machine on tire and his coat-tails blazing
in the ureeze. He was a hurried passer-by,
with no time to notice what was behind
him, so the citizen broke a fire alarm and
sooled an engine on to him. Simultaneously,
more or less, his jigger jibbed or struck or
burst 1 or something, and the brigade got a
chance to turn the hose on to the rider’s
heated garments and souse his wrecked
machine. Then a corporation laborer swept
the ruins into the gutter, and a brilliant and
exciting “turn” was over. This is the. sort
of episode that makes one understand why
people support posts. The possibility of
adventure is a tremendous lure. Three-
quarters of the crowd in the city streets on
a shopping night are there in the vague hope
that something exciti g may transpire. It
hardly ever does, the world being a dull, grey
place, these law-abiding days. But when the
unexpected arrives and a man gets mur-
dered, or a policeman apprehends a bogus
drunk or a mad cyclist goes shrieking past
with a forest of flames in his wake, all the
boredom of past nights is forgiven and for-
gotten.
“ Jeames” on his defence :
The other day I referred to Governor Strickland,
of N; S. Wales, as “bourgeois.” Shrieks of protest
from the suburbs, where every Englishman of birth
is regarded as unassailable, socially, so long as he
remains solvent and moderately sober. The fact
that Sir Gerald s ancestors came over with the Con-
queror, or the Armada, or the original batch of Rag-
timers,''has been cited wildly, as though such a cir-
cumstance had anything whatever to do with the
case. None but those who are themselves utterly
and irremediably bourgeois could take so distorted
a view of the matter. To be bourgeois is a congeni-
tal trait, or affliction, like fiat feet, the habit of
wearing Jemima boots, and so on. Breeding does
not enter into the matter. The general tendency of
bourgeois people is to take the obvious view in all
things social and political, and to display a stunned
and scandalised appearance in the presence of inno-
vations in these lines. The bourgeois temperament
mostly runs to hideous clothes, indifferently cooked
food, a distrust of wine (drunkenness), cards (gam-
bling), mild flirtations (immorality), mixed surf-
ing (shameless immodesty), and other things that
make life bearable. Yet the bourgeois is by no
means always virtuous. The villainous old Crawley
baronet whom Thackeray portrayed so faithfully was
typically bourgeois. The bourgeois trait is one of
the hardest things to define in words; yet most
people who know the world can recognise it at a
hundred yards. The Earl of Meath, who was in Aus-
tralia the other day, was bourgeois to the back
teeth; so were the Rawsons and the Chelmsfords, and
in a slightly lesser degree, the Northcotes. Untitled
people, of no particular loftiness in the way of birth,
may, on the other hand, be enthusiastically exon-
erated of any such charge. I see by the London
papers, for example, that Major Brinton and Mrs.
William James have just been married. I have
waited on them both. Her late husband owned West
Dean Park, near Goodwood. The Major owns nothing
to speak of, barring a handsome face and the most
charming manners. Neither are in Debrett by virtue
of birth. Yet if some inexperienced footman (say)
were to term either of them bourgeois the entire
Servants’ Hall would arise, as one servitor, and
screech in dissent. Millionaire dukes may be bour-
geois—he of Newcastle, as a case in point, and the
late Duke of Sutherland. Contrariwise, the chroni-
cally hard-up and extremely deaf, but withal essenti-
ally “smart,” Mrs. Hwfa Williams has never been
accused of the offence, and never will be to her dying
day. Archbishop Wright is bourgeois: Bishop
Mercer isn’t. Wade and McGowen, of N.S.W.,
are; Holman is not, or, at any rate, not criminally
so. Alfred Deakin isn’t. Joseph Cook—-but let me
end here. Australia’s present Prime Minister can
supply a better idea of the bourgeois outlook in
one brief platform oration than any writer could in a
three-volume treatise on the subject.
Victoria is just now looking
dumbly to Cold Tea for ex-
planations and getting none..
Wonthaggi is to blame—Won-
thaggi, which is teetotal in so
far as it has no pubs, but
“clubs” of sorts and sly-grog
shops. The wives of this fav-
ored spot have neen writing to
Chief Secretary Weary—who
kept silence —and to Police
Commissioner Sainsbury—who
published their remarks —clam-
oring, in the name of sobriety,
for the licensing of proper
hotels. Premier Watt, having
condescended to notice the mat-
ter, has opined that “special
legislation” is needed to deal
with the evil, and that the offi-
cial licensing of the clubs would be effective.
It might, or it might lead to an increase of
sly-grog selling. But the establishment of that
State hotel that Peter Mcßride promised
long ago should kill both bogus clubs and
sly-groggeries. The State hotel doesn’t exist to
make as much as it can out of beertangle-
whisky foot, neither does it sell doctored or
poisonous stuff as good liquor. And the
average miner is a fair judge of liquor.
The- old scheme for linking up Hobart’s
marine suburbs with the city by means of a
bridge across the Derwent has once more
been revived, and an estimate of its pro-
bable cost is being called for. Present com-
munication is by the Rosny Estate and
Ferry Company’s line of steamers, and the
resurrection of the bridge scheme follows on
that co.’s attempt to balance its failure in
THE ENERGETIC WORKMAN.
Contractor Brown : “Bright bricklayer that Cameron, you sent me. Every time he
lays a brick he stops to cackle. ”
land speculation with a 50 per cent, rise in
season fares. The suburbs’ howl of protest
has deafened several directors, and Bellerive
and Lindisfarne are considering the advis-
ability of packing up their sticks and mov-
ing into town —where there are no empty
houses to move to. In the public interest
the service should be a Government one.
Hobart can’t back much further on to the
mountain, and the suburbs across the water
are its natural offtlet; but the Government
has already pledged itself to a pretty stiff
schedule of public works, and is not likely
to add to its present responsibilities. Belle-
rive is the Kangaroo Point of the State’s
earliest records, and one of its legends is
that Parson Knopwood’s white pony once
swam across from the Hobart side to its
stable there. It was a record if it did, as
the distance must be nearly three miles.
The bridge was one of Governor Denison’s
pet projects, and the old twin ferry, Kan-
garoo, which still earns a Government sub-
sidy by taking carts and produce over the
river, dates back to his time. The O’May
family, who sold out to the Rosny Go. a year
or so ago, built up the passenger traffic, and
incidentally Bellerive and Lindisfarne ; their
connection with the traffic goes back three
generations and starts with a Scotch grand-
father and a whaleboat.
From an Australian pressman in S’Africa :
The visit of H.M.A.S. Australia and Sydney to
Table Bay has done us good, yet left us angry.
Until a year or so ago the Cape press could not find
one word of good to say about Australia or Austra-
lians. Editors ran out of dictionary adjectives of
derogation and abuse, and turned to the Taal for
help in the emergency. Now they have somersaulted
and are preaching the gospel of Australian nation-
hood and ideals to South Africa. All through the
dog days many bad Australians, a good loss to
their country, denied Australia, as did Peter of old
his Master. But there always have been good Aus-
tralians, who, through thick and thin, through good
and evil repute, at personal risk and financial loss,
proudly stood up for their mother country whenever
occasion demanded—which was pretty often. The
visit of the two ships gave the renegades the chance
to come back; and when the newspapers suddenly
burst into paeans, they remembered they were Aus-
tralians too. We had held a meeting whereat it had
been decided to present flags to the Admiral’s ship.
With Australian grit, the ladies—the best Austra-
lians in South Africa—decided to do the work with
their own hands. “We won’t have flags flying on our
ships in which a stitch has been put by colored
labor,” was the sentiment of the ladies. So thev
went to work and turned out a couple of flags of
the true sort. We had a wattle day, pressing into
service a bloom—the Port Jackson—which has the
merit of some resemblance to the golden wattle.
Then came the great banquet in the House of Parlia-
ment, the most exclusive function ever held in the
Cape. Six invitations were allotted to the Australian
community. And who do you think got them? The
renegades, mostly! True, there was one brilliant
exception—R. P. Fitzgerald, son of Nicholas, of Vic-
toria ; but for the rest, almost without exception,
Australia was represented by men who had but
yesterday denied or belied their country, and to-
morrow will probably do it again. Who pulled the
strings we don’t know; but the thing has left the
real Australian community' in S’Africa sore and
sorry.
The police of Geelong (Vic.) have been
making a raid on the young couples who lie
on the seaside after dark. Several couples
described as “reputable,” arid who have
“kept company” for various periods, were
summoned to the City Court to be lectured
by George Murphy, P.M. The P.M. himself
had sooled the police on to do this thing.
The sin of the al fresco canoodling was said
to lie in the fact that it happened after sun-
down, when darkness shrouded it from the
“disgust” of everybody save the policemen
who crept up and took the preoccupied com-
pany-keepers by surprise. No “indecent”

endearments had passed between the unfor-
tunate defendants in any given case, but
their method of “keeping company” was de-

Murphy, P.M., being responsible for the
prosecutions, felt called upon to say that
“no decent man would marry a woman
who would lie on the beach in the arms of
one of the opposite sex.” Which is a
sweeping allegation, and one not calculated
to alarm any girl who gets herself hugged
on the sea-beach with a view to marrying
the hugger anon.
THE SNARE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Now come,” said the Devil, he said to me,
With his swart face all a-grin ;
“This day, ere ever the clock strikes three,
Shall you sin your darling sin.
For I’ve wagered a crown with Beelzebub,
Down there at the Gentlemen’s Brimstone Club,
I shall tempt you once, I shall tempt you twice,
And thrice shall you fall ere I tempt you thrice.”
“Begone, base Devil!” I made reply—
“ Begone, with your fiendish grin !
How hope you to profit by such as I,
For I have no darling sin.
But many there be, and I know them well,
All foul with sinning and ripe for Hell.
And I name no names, but the whole world knows
That I am never of such as those.”
“How now?” said the Devil. “I’ll spread my net,
And I vow I’ll gather you in !
By this and by that shall I win my bet,
And you shall sin the sin !
Come, fill up a bumper of good red wine.
Your heart shall sing, and your eye shall shin",
And you’ll know such joy as you never have known.
For the salving of man was the good wine grown.”'
“Begone, red Devil!” I made reply.
“Parch shall these lips of mine,
And my tongue shall shrink, and my throat go dry,
Ere ever I taste your wine!
But greet you shall, as I know full well,
A tipsy score of my friends in Hell.
And I name no names, but the whole world wots
Most of my fellows are drunken sots.”
“Ah, ha!” said the Devil. “You scorn the wine!
Thrice shall you sin, I say,
To win me a crown from a friend of mine,
Ere three o’ the clock this day.
Are you calling to mind some lady fair?
And is she a wife or a maiden rare?
’Twere folly to shackle young Love, hot Youth ;
Forbidden kisses are sweet, forsooth.”
“Begone, foul Devil!” I made reply,
“For never in all my life
Have I looked on a woman with lustful eye,
Be she maid, or widow, or wife.'
But my brothers! Alas! lam scandalised
At their evil passions so ill-disguised.
And I name no names, but my thanks I give
That I loathe the life my fellow-men live.”
“Ho, ho! ” roared the Devil in fiendish glee.
“ ’Tis a silver crown I win!
Thrice have you fallen ! O Pharisee,
You have sinned your darling sin!”
But, “Nay,” said I; “and I scorn your lure.
I have sinned no sin, and my heart is pure.
Come, tell me the sin that you think you see?”
But the Devil was gone.... and the clock struck
three.
VICTORIA.
C. J. DENNIS.

“Headmaster”: There are a good many
apologetic and ashamed Australians in the
State Schools of Sydney, to judge by the
fact that about a hundred departments in
the metropolitan schools did not bother to
send along an entry for the Children’s Wel-
come to the Australian Navy on its arrival.
A special departmental circular was hur-
riedly issued asking, “Is it not possible, even
at this late hour, to send a representative
class to join in the demonstration welcoming
Our Own Fleet, which should have a recep-
tion no less cordial than that given to the
American Fleet?” It was pointed out, a
month ago, to the responsible heads, that
the schools were given a week’s holiday when
the American Fleet arrived, and hence were
full of enthusiasm. But of course the feeble
department could not give our schools a
week’s holiday to celebrate the coming home
of Our Own Fleet; nevertheless, having
in this and other ways shown only luke-
warm interest, it coolly wondered at the
lack of enthusiasm. The Education De-
partment badly wants a virile Australian
administrator to stop the dry-rot which
afflicts it. Incidentally, an angry, indignant
Australian, such as the writer, may perhaps
be pardoned for wondering whether our de-
partmental heads and public men were
leagued in a conspiracy with the Federal
Liberal authorities to damn our Labor-
created Fleet’s home-coming with anaemic
interest, wishy-washy welcome and grossly
bungled arrangements.

demned to live and move and have his being
within the four walls of an ordinary house,
villa or humpy; to polish the same old faded
linoleums in each room on certain days,
year in, year out; to spend a lifetime caring
for the same old bits of furniture and fur-
nishings, and cooking the same old meals for
a conservative-appetited family? If he
hasn’t tried it I hereby sentence him to do
so for one consecutive lifetime. Then he’ll
understand. It will ease his rusty feelings
ma b chine ft whe a re y the soused
versa; and get the paperhangers in for no
other reason than to stop the same old
flowers on the parlor wall leering at him
any longer. You might suggest that the
furniture-juggler should go abroad to seek
variety, but you’ll usually find there’s the
marriage tie, or the tie of twins, or an
assorted family or something equally bind-
ing in the way. I once overheard this mono-
logue in a poor suburb: “No, I never goes to
pitcher shows. ’E goes sometimes and takes
some er th’ kids ; but it’s no ’oliday fer me
ter take the ’ole lot and mind ’em. I ain’t
’ad a evening off proper nor a day neither,
’cept when I’d be laid up, since I had me first
baby—and that’s more’n 10 year ago.” A very
ordinary, unsensational statement about a
trade that knows no eight hours, and whose
unending work is scheduled by no wages
board. If it would give that woman any
joy to shift the time-payment piano into the
scullery, and put the coal scuttle on the
front room mantel, then, I say, let her!

“11. Singemoine” on romance, etc.: —
A promising’ romance appears to have been assas-
sinated, practically at birth, by the accidental death
of a child in France. It appeared in evidence,
according to the cable, that the child had been run
over by a motor-car driven by Princess Sophia of
Saxe-Weimar-Eisenach, with whom were her mother
and Hans Bleichroder, her lover. But the Princess
is supposed to have been dead for the past month or
more. A circumstantial story was cabled in Septem-
ber, telling how she had been refused permission to
marry her Jewish admirer ; how she had shot herself
in despair; how it was reported, at first, that she
had died of heart-failure; and how Bleichroder had
hurried to, her side, but had been forbidden to attend
the funeral. Apparently the suicide story is a fabri-
cation, concocted with the idea that it is better that
a German Princess should be exposed to the world
as a, suicide than that she should marry or have a
liaison with a middle-class Jew. It is possible, on the
other hand, that the lady really is dead, and that
the inquest on her victim was unconscionably post-
poned for some reason. The romance of the suicide
vanishes just the same. Evidently she slew herself,
not because she feared separation from her Israelite
adorer, but because she knew that the fact of her
having killed the child would cause scandal in the
circumstances. About 99 per cent, of romantic
tragedies peter out in this disappointing way. The
Princess whom we were prepared to class with
Heloise turns out to be dashing about the country
with the allegedly heart-broken Yid, who had been
represented as tearing distractedly across Europe to
her funeral. An Austrian prince is for years
mourned as dead, killed by his own hand, and in
the end is identified living in happy obscurity with
his wife and family, in a humble Harlem (U.S.A.)
flat. A distinguished soldier is told, by one even
more distinguished, that the hemlock awaits him.
The world hears that he has done the right thing,
and blown his brains out. Shortly after, people
begin to meet him in China, in Peru, in Mexico —in
all sorts of out-of-the-way spots; or to think they
do. The moral is that it takes a dashed lot to
make healthy human beings shoot themselves. By
the way, I have for some time foreseen that motor-
car driving by girls will hit up the immorality
figures in the next few years. Morals are a question
even more of opportunity than of climate, and the
girl who drives her own car will get more oppor-
tunity in a week than her grandmother did in a
year. Human nature being the weak and erring
thing it is, the ear-driving girl is bound, in certain
cases, to take at least some of her chances. How-
ever, if she subsequently makes an honest man of
her companion there is no reason why anyone
should complain.

Alpar : I have a grievance that I d e
to ventilate, in a few words, if you wouldn t
mind. We N. S. Wales country teachers
were i n f orm ed, through the Public Instruc-
Gazette, that we were not to close our
schools on Eight-Hour Day. bo 1 went
along to my seminary, and found the men
all lying around idle, and nary a child at
school. A few of my best friends looked at
me with a pained expression, but 1 didn t
mind, as, like the late H Nelson. 1 had a
duty to perform. I hung on to that bit or duty
valiantly until midday, and then set out tor
home. Passing a group of workers, some-
body called Blackleg, and I feel that I
have gone and got myselt disliked by the
parents. Hundreds of teachers are in the
same boat as I am, and Id like to know
whose fault it is. It was an ill-advised,
wretched business ; and I hope 111 never
have another holiday like it.
Vox : Sydney has no statue of the Royal
George to decorate on days of national re-
joicing, so the best the Government can do
is to hire a few dozen pot plants and stand
them on the grass-plot surrounding his
grandma’s statue in Queen’s Place. Viewed
from a passing tramcar, this makes it ap-
pear as if the lady with the down-pointed
sceptre is holding an auction sale in aid of
something or other, possibly another statue.
She seems to find buyers, too. for ji cart
calls next day and removes the crockery.
THE END OF THE MODERN ROMANCE.
“And they hved happily ever after."
The Commonwealth military authorities
lately pitched a long-overdue bomb into the
hull of the venerable maritime superstition
that the artillery in the Sydney fortifications
is tnaintained purely as a laughing stock
for cantankerous skippers. Formerly the
gunners used to get a bit of practice with
live shot occasionally—if "they were lucky
enough to catch the sea clear of shipping of
every sort. On one occasion the dis-
gusted artillerymen had to stand by for an
hour while a wretched mud-punt lumbered
leisurely across the firing line; and on
another they were held up till their nerves
were raw while a big passenger liner drifted
into Watson’s Bay for medical inspection.
On April 25 the A.G.A. were booked for
battle practice at 10 a.m. They couldn’t
get a start till 1 p.m., and when they did
start they were so interrupted that they
didn’t fire their last shot till 5 o’clock —
seven hours for a job that would have been
done in an hour but for the shipping which
gaily sailed across the line of fire. The last
straw arrived when some skippers had the
cheek to complain to the Navigation Depart-
ment that firing didn’t stop the instant they
heaved above the horizon. The Military
authorities reckoned that was too strong, so
they intimated their intention of enforcing
paragraphs 71 and 72 of the Defence Act
which give the officer in charge
of any artillery or rifle range power to stop
all traffic within the danger zone during
practice. As a penaltv of £5O hangs to a
breach of the law, perhaps the casual skipper
will be a bit less bumptious in future.
* * * *
Not long back, what was hailed as the
first real eugenic marriage took place in
Massachusetts (U.S.A.), both parties reck-
oning their mating was in strict accord with
scientific principles, and that the usual sen-
timental influences were not main factors.
Now comes word that the unsentimental
bridegroom has paid a hurried visit to the
nearest police station, complaining of an
assault by a strange man whom he had
found with his wife in the eugenic sitting-
room. It had turned out just like a heap of
the common or weed variety of matrimonial
speculations—only sooner.
* * * *
The latest proposal to make some public
use of Greycliffe, the old Wentworth house
at Vaucluse, is to fix it up as the official
residence of N.S.W. Chief Justices. Some
parties have discovered a sense of unfitness
in having the Chief Justices of the State
living here and there, in rented houses or
in houses that are being bought on time-
payment. But why the Chief Justice any
more than other Judges? Certainly if the
State Governorship were abolished and the
Chief Justice handled the rubber stamp, Grey-
cliffe might be thrown in. Otherwise there
seems to be no good reason for supplementing
the salary of the “Chief,” which is already
quite high enough, compared with the pay of
the other Judges—often equally eminent
lawyers. The “Chief” gets £3500 a year;
the puisnes, £2600. Only one C.J. of
N.S.W. has had an official, rent-free State
residence, built and furnished by the Govern-
ment. That was Forbes, who lived in a
ramshackle building on or near the site of
the present Lands Office. It was contem-
plated at that time that the C.J. should
always be provided with a residence; but
the salary was small in those days.
Melbourne University professors have
been talking of late about “tutorial classes”
for university extension lectures. A pro-
fessor’s lofty manner of referring to “tutorial
classes” reminds one of George Willoughby’s
high-toned butler in “The Night of the
Party,” who buttled for nobody of less im-
portance than a duke ; and always spoke of
footmen as persons in a '“senatorial capa-
city.”
Westralia’s Arbitration Court President,
Judge Burnside, is now probably cured of
his habit of holding long conversations with
lady witnesses. His custom has been to dis-
cuss at length the cheapest way to provide a
dinner, or how best to cook salt junk, the
while, of course, fishing for information on
the cost of living. The other day, a she-clerk
said that to dress well cost her £5O a year;
and no sooner had his Honor expressed sur-
prise than the lady dived into particulars,
and before the loud coughs of the Bench had
drowned her voice, she had given a full
account of —of everything. Burnside blushed
profusely as he protested that he did not
want all these details; but he got them,
nevertheless.
* * * *
“Carmine”: London reports a great slump
in modern pictures, chiefly through the
American craze for Old Masters, and no
doubt those who take advantage of it to
snap up accredited moderns will ultimately
make money. Landseer’s “Otter Hunt,”
which cost 10,000 guineas, was sold lately
for 1200. It will never go much above that.
Stanfield’s “Wooden Walls of Old England,”
bought for 2700 guineas some years ago, is
sold now for 160. Tadema, Prout, Rosa
Bonheur and many others have shrivelled
greatly. Tadema will probably never be
worth more than half his top prices—he is
regarded now chiefly as a decorator. Rosa
Bonheur will not lift much. Stanfield’s “Off
Calais” has fallen from lluO guineas to 58.
But it is worth more, and might be looked up
at (say) 100 for Sydney, Melbourne, Ade-
laide or Ballarat. “On The Lagoon” goes
down from 1900 to 52, and “View Near Ser-
polina” from 1060 to 205. Tadema’s “Old,
Old Story” slumps from 1150 to 370, and
“Garland Seller,” a thin thing, from 1120 to
235. Yet I don’t recommend anyone to sell
even mining shares and buy Tademas. Mel-
bourne has an awfully expensive little one,
worth about a sixth part of cost. Prout
has sold at less than 100 guineas each. What
a gamble it is. There was, on the other
side, poor Romney. He gave his “Lady
Pole” and another picture for 160 guineas,
and the “Lady Pole” has lately fetched
£41,370.
SIC TRANSIT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I burnt your letters, for, alas!
Cupid, in lieu of rose and dove,
Brought marriage—such a paltry,’ small,
And somewhat acid fruit of love!
Two pillow-cases full there were;
They voiced your heart in every phase
Passionate, tender, joyous, proud;
The moods of bygone, amorous days.
I burnt them in the copper fire
Last Monday ; page on page that thrilled
Blazed up and made the copper boil,
With a week’s household washing’ filled.
Yet something of them still survives,
For, flotsam of our passion’s wreck,
The ribbons once around them tied
Make bows for our black kitten’s neck !
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.
THE BREAKING OF THE DROUGHT.
[For The Bulletin.]
The Postmaster-General has promised to consider a
request made by the Licensed Victuallers’ Associa-
tion of Australasia for permission to send liquor
through the post. —News item.
The post arrives but once a week
Outback at Barrengums;
Wild hopes and fears commingled fill
The rough, big-hearted shearers till
At last the mailman comes.
Strong men, their eyes aflood with tears,
Turn out and welcome him with cheers!
«
Each eagerly his “letter” elaims —-
Some lucky ones get two —
And lo ! their joy knows no restraint,
While they proceed the place to paint
A bright vermilion hue.
Thus, weekly, in their simple way,
They celebrate red-letter day.
VICTORIA.

A famous London physician, who for years
made a study of this disease, has, after a number of
experiments, found a prescription which affords re-
lief in almost a marvellous manner. This has been
put on the market by Mr. I. W. Nicholl, Pharmaceu-
tical Chemist, of Belfast, under the name of
“Ozerine,” and the number of unsolicited testimonials
received speak well for its efficiency as a remedy
against this appalling disease. Your chemist can
easily get it for you.*

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Alf Edden, N.S.W. Minister for Mines,
has been giving the ladies’ branch of the
Glebe Labor League a little of his personal
history. Before he was 10 he was getting
9d. per day in a coal mine, leaving home
at 5 in the morning and not getting back
until 7.30 at night. He was the youngest of
a family of 14, and he himself had a family
of 14 —13 of them living; one was killed
in a coal mine. Christmas before last he and
his missus, with their sons, their daughters
and their grandchildren, sat down to dinner
35 strong; had they met last Christmas
there would have been 42 of them. The
warrior has been offered £5OO to write his
life, he says, and he thinks he’ll start on
it. In the matter of education there was
very little difference between Mr. Wade and
himself —“he was educated with his books
of logic and his grammar books at the Uni-
versity ; I was educated in a coal mine with
a double-ended pick and a No. 6 shovel.”
THE CAPTURE.
The American Beef Trust is adopting the same
tactics to capture the Australian trade as were used
in the Argentine.— News item.
THE BERFY ONE: "Dear little pet! I knew
it would lead on this string!”
They can’t keep out of it! Hall-Jones,
ex-Minister for Public Works, ex-Premier,
ex-High Commissioner for Maoriland, has
accepted a seat in that country’s Leg. Coun-
cil. Jones not long ago called on Exalted
Heaven to witness his retirement from poli-
tics. He’s been in them more or less for
about a quarter of a century, and E. H.
might have been expected to take his word
for it. But the witness was silent. It
mostly is when the voluntary retirement of
actresses and politicians is mentioned.
Tom Quinlan, the Napoleon of the Grand
Opera Co., is a native of Bury, in Lanca-
shire, but comes of a Tipperary family. As
a youngster he had a sound musical educa-
tion, and began as choir boy, after which he
developed a fine, mellow baritone. Then,
with a view to a life-crust, he became ap-
prenticed to an accountant, and followed
figures for several years, though pursuing
a musical education all the time. His know-
ledge of accounts brought him his first job
as operatic manager: a friend in that line
invited him to become his partner. Quin-
lan, however, did not long agree with his
friend’s schemes, so bought him right out,
and has been going from big ventures to
bigger ones ever since.
Note from “Jeames” :
The Dudleys have made it up. A year ago there
was talk of a divorce, so hopeless did the rift
appear ; but at this stage the King and Queen, who
are intimately friendly with both parties, waded in
and metaphorically forbade the banns. King Edward
did this in connection with a row which concerned
the present Duke of Westminster, and a somewhat
similar move was made in the case of the Duke of
Marlborough and his wife. There are some noblemen
—the Cowleys, Rosslyns, etc., of Society—who do
not matter. They may be served with citations,
bankruptcy notices, ca. sa. orders and the like every
other week of their lives, without the stability of
the Empire being menaced. . But England’s Marl-
boroughs and Dudleys are out of that class. Since
the beginning of the Dudley row he has been in
Cairo, while she has lived at Witley Court with the
children. The latter are now growing up. Lady
Honor Ward, whom Australians will remember as a
long-legged flapper, has “come out,” and Lord
Ednam, the family heir, will be of age shortly.
Hence the desirability of a reconciliation.

John Dennistoun Wood, now in Tasmania,
is the oldest living Victorian Executive
Councillor; he was appointed March 11,
1857, when he was made Solicitor-General in
the first O’Shanassy Ministry. The next in
seniority, with a break of 18 years between,
is Chief Justice Madden, who became Minis-
ter of Justice under James McCulloch, in
1875.
Of all rich men left in South Aus. Robert
Barr Smith’s most serious rival is Carl
Blume, of Mount Gambier. If he passes
hence in the midst of a good season he will
probably pan out at well over a million.
Sheep have been his downfall. Some time
ago he handed two sons £160,000 each and
told them to go out and earn their own
living, after the manner of the father in the
parable, who gave the Prodigal Son £2OO in
notes of the Bank of Jerusalem, and in-
formed him that he had got swelled head
and had better strike out for himself.
Wilbur Glenn Voliva, Elijah 111., _ and
successor to Elijah of the Purple Wings,
has gone one better than his predecessor in
Zion City. Dowie only got as far as to
appoint the first of the Twelve Apostles;
that was himself. He promised to name the
rest in due course. His fatal madness, how-
ever, intervened. Voliva has just got the
ecclesiastical lasso on two more of the
Twelve, and they were duly consecrated at
the recent Feast of Tabernacles. One of
them is a missionary from Japan named
Royall; the other rejoices in the name of
Eustace Lawford Carey. Carey was cap-
tured in Melbourne by Voliva himself, and
is more than his right hand to the present
dictator of Zion. He is the son of a well-
known Melbourne minister, and, when fished
up from the sea of Australian iniquity, was
manager of the Northcote branch of one of
the Melbourne banks. The leader of the
Melbourne Zionites kicked up a row over the
coming to light of these two brand-new
apostles, and straightway resigned. Even a
man who can create Apostles at will has his
troubles.
The estate of the late Rowland Ferrers
Pickering pays probate duty on £15,761.
“Rowley” Pickering was sporting editor of
the Sydney Mail for many years, but his
little fortune didn’t come from his pen. His
mother and a couple of uncles —the late
Eyre Goulburn Ellis, solicitor, and Thomas
Ellis, auctioneer —left it (or most of it) to
him.
Peter Bowling’s fiery glim has been
doused once more. After leading the New-
castle miners in the disastrous strike of a
few years back, Peter met reversals that
induced him to gravitate to the Illawarra
coal district, where he became the miners’
general secretary. His enkindling policy did
not pan out any better in the South than it
did in the North, and at a recent ballot for
the billet Peter came a bad third.
Engineer Alfred Williams, who died
lately, aged 79, came to N.S.W. in 1865 to
boss the construction of the railway bridge
over the Nepean at Penrith. Later, being
appointed to the Harbors and Rivers De-
partment, he supervised the building of
ivjLan-o’-War Steps and the retaining wall at
Farm Cove, and gave an eye to many re-
clamation works and divers wharves and
jetties. Sir Edward Leader Williams, the
designer of the Manchester (Eng.) Ship
Canal, who died in 1910, was his eldest
brother.
Thomas Parker Reeve, who died at Stan-
more (N.S.W.) on Friday, in his 90th year,
was a very old Sydney business man. In
the very first ’so’s he established himself in
George-street as a general dealer, in hard-
ware chiefly, his neighbor on the south being
Anthony Hordern, draper, in a small way
then. This was Anthony 11., father of the
late Sam Hordern. Mr. Reeve continued
there for many years, and then, retiring,
gave himself up mostly to philanthropic and
church work.
Died last week J. J. Duncan, leader since
1901 of the Conservative party in the S.
Aus. House of Landlords. Of late years
the crowd behind him called itself the Lib-
eral party; but Duncan, as a dead-straight
man, never liked the new title. The fairly
well-to-do men with whom S. Aus. started
were, on the whole, a good lot; and Dun-
can’s family, which was among them, did
some very estimable things. His father was
a partner of W. W. Hughes, and the young-
ster was on the firm’s squattage on Yorke’s
Peninsula when Moonta was discovered. He
carried the good news to the old people, who
were made rich men by it. Later on a good
cut out of the money went in endowing
some of the first chairs established at the
Adelaide University.
The travelled Australian always seems to
get to the top in the countries he settles in.
Medico-General P. J. 11. Farrell, a Braid-
wood < N.S.W.) boy, long resident in the
United States, a noted soldier of many cam-
paigns, and a big practitioner of Chicago, has
been appointed by the Wilson Administra-
tion as Governor-General of the Philippine
Islands. This is about the biggest thing,
next to Dr. Morrison’s China organisation
job, that an Australian has ever struck out-
side his own little island. The popular “Pat”
Farrell will be only a fortnight’s sail from
his own native Braidwood soon, and might
pop down one of these days in a Yankee
cruiser, with a Philippino band and a star-
spangled banner, and say “Howdy” to bis
old friends.

A couple of rival collectors of Australian
history are now at work in Sydney, both
among the musty, grimy records of the
Supreme Court —-Robert McNab, the Maori-
land historian, and Dr. Watson, the Com-
monwealth historian. McNab has been suc-
cessful in discovering a number of valuable
MSS. bearing upon early Maoriland, and
Watson is putting the top bricks on a new
history he has been building.
Mrs. Hester Manning, who died the other
day at Marrickville (N.S.W.), was widow
of Arthur Wilcox Manning, brother of Jus-
tice William Montagu Manning and son
of John Edye Manning, an early Registrar
of the Supreme Court. A. W. Manning
was Under-Secretary in the Chief Secre-
tary’s office in Queensland in 1868, when he
was assaulted with a tomahawk by a sus-
pended C.P.S. The C.P.S. got penal servi-
tude for life; Manning three months later
got a pension of £6OO a year. Everyone
believed that he had retired to die, but he
lived for another 32 years.
Australia is in temporary possession of
two more of those unaccountable persons
who trapse round the earth on their uppers,
so to speak, relying on the good offices of
the occasional stranger for a crust. Mrs.
Hilda Gilbert, of N’York, is supposed to be
trying to win 5000 dollars from Jack Lon-
don by earning her way about the world.
Pitta Ryan, one of seven Edmonton (Can-
ada) newsboys, is on the same sort of lurk.
He is pledged to circumnavigate the globe
inside five years as from April 28, T 2.
The countries that encourage this sort of
foolishness deserve all the annoyance they
get from it, like the people who help to carry
on “chain-prayers” and similar futilities.
Francis Conway, whose executors have
paid probate duty on £136,000, was a Mel-
bourne cobbler who stuck to his last. He
began and ended in a Prahran bootshop,
and rarely looked beyond a stone’s-throw
from the local town hall for an investment.
In his young days he hawked boots up and
down Chapel-street, and there he opened his
first shop, near the spot where the old Town
Hall was subsequently built. As his business
increased he opened another bootery or two
in the same street, and as he prospered he
put his spare capital into land and house
property thereabouts. When the land boom
smashed, people continued to w r ear boots, so
Conway was still able to snap up bargains
in real estate. But the soul of the ex-pedlar
never expanded. In his old age he still de-
lighted to dust his goods and sweep the pave-
ment in front of his shop, whilst Prahran
reverenced him for his suburban dullness
and his growing riches. In fact, it was
greatly disappointed on learning that its
ancient bootseller had left only £136,000.
and he is 1 now suspected of having handed
over a lot of his possessions to Holy Church
to ensure his safety hereafter.
INCOMPATIBILITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ex-King Manoel, of Portugal, and his wife, who
separated before their honeymoon was over, are still
estranged. In Vienna Court circles it is not believed
that anything beyond a nominal reconciliation has
been effected. Incompatibility of temper is said to
be the cause of the estrangement.— Cable.
When plain Jim Smith and Mrs. Smith
Take up the tongs and two or three
Odd cups to smite each other with —
That’s incompatibility !
To coin a better phrase to fit it
The hapless couple do not “hit it.”
But in the royal households, rare
Are such things known. With vicious zest
They do not swipe each other there
With stewpan or with kitchen chair.
Which gives this Manoel affair
An extra ; special interest.
We have a vision of the king
(That used to be) at 3 p.m.
Enthroned upon the mat to sing
Some weird, distracting, ragtime gem,
The while his spouse, the point to settle,
Essays to stun him with the kettle.
In Smith’s poor, plebeian house, we fear,
A common feature this may be,
■Where hubby comes home on his ear,
And brings a noble load of beer ;
But rarely in the royal sphere
Does such a vulgar thing appear
As incompatibility.
Divinity that hedges kings
Left this one when he lost his job,
And papers hint at awful things
As if he were a common yob,
As if his queen (as should have been),
Forgetting to be sweet and tender,
Might part his whiskers with the fender,
And both might sit the breakfast through,
Exchanging plates and mockery
As mere suburban couples do.
It knocks our loyal dreams askew,
And leaves us curious to view
All bills for royal crockery.
VICTORIA.

For many years N. S. Wales has had two Savings
Banks, one generally known as the Post Office and
the other as the Barrack-street institution. The
one under notice was the Post Office Bank, and when
the Commonwealth took over the Post Office it should
also have taken over the bank. But it didn’t; in-
stead, the State was permitted to carry on business
at the old stands until the Commonwealth Govern-
ment established a Savings branch of its own. Then
this State Bank had to shift out, and during the
last few months it has been busy jerrying up tem-
porary banking premises on the handiest vacant
blocks it could lay its hands on. For the moment,
therefore, there are three Savings Banks in N. S.
Wales—this straight-out State institution ; the one
that has its head-office in Barrack-street, Sydney,
and is controlled by trustees who know very well
that the State would have to come again to their
rescue if they got into difficulties, as they once did ;
and the Savings branch of the Commonwealth Bank.
It is an absurd and wasteful multiplication ; and it
won’t be very much better when presently the bank
which is a State Bank and admits it and the other
bank that fs a State Bank and won’t admit it are
amalgamated. *
No figures show the prosperity of N. S. AVales
more strikingly than those relating to the growth of
this bank’s deposits:—
1901 ..£6,647,289 I 1907 ..£11,128,495
1902 .. 7,100,108 j 1908 .. 12,118,772
1903 .. 7,018,425 | 1909 .. 13,303,421
1904 .. 7,952,885 | 1910 .. 15,190.820
1905 . . 8,883,651 ! 1911 . . 17,595,695
1906 . . 9,322,923 | 1912 . . 20,128,598
The increase in 10 years is nearly threefold. But it
isn’t altogether a matter to give three cheers about.
If the country had earned this money it would be
magnificent. But it hasn’t; it has borrowed most of
it, and will have to repay it, and until it does repay
it interest will have to be found somewhere. On
June 30, 1903, the public debt of N. S. Wales was
£77,692,987 ; on June 30, 1913, it was
£106,170.747. Thus in 10 years the State has bor-
rowed £28,477,760, and other large amounts have
been invested or otherwise borrowed for private pur-
poses. It hasn’t all been foreign money, but a great
deal of it has been. The bulk of the money bor-
rowed by the State has been put into public works;
and those works have cost just as much if they will
return only 1 p.c. as if they would give 5 p.c. In
fact, they will give only a wretched return in some
cases because the money spent on them has been
grossly excessive. A good deal of this Savings Bank
money is money that has been spent on such works
—and the State would be better off if it hadn’t
been spent and if it hadn’t therefore been available
for the bank to get hold of. Of course the borrowing
hasn’t been wholly responsible for the big increase
in the deposits. Good seasons and bigger wage-
earnings have had a lot to do with it. And in this
connection it has to be remembered that high prices
have come with high wages, wherefore the
£20,000,000 of deposits to-day are probablv worth
no more to their owners than £15,000,000 would
have been in, say, 1900. From several points of
view, then, the increase in denosits, while, of course,
very satisfactory, is nothing like so cheerful a cir-
cumstance as it looks.
The next list shows what is done with the de-
posits and other resources :
p.c. of
total.

The fact that three-fourths of all the resources of
the bank are lent to the State Government explains
why the State Government fought like a bag of cats
against the proposal to transfer this bank, as well
as the other departments of the Post Office, to the
Commonwealth. In times when the regular money-
lending Cohen is shy, it is a blessed relief to a dis-
tracted Treasurer to be able to dip his hand into
such a till as this; and the people who control it
are the only people who can dip into it. Besides
helping to finance the Government the bank also
finances the Advances to Settlers Department, which
had its origin in the old Advances to Settlers Board
—an institution designed by politicians for the re-
lief of needy farming constituents. The Advances
Department is now on a sound business footing ; it is
managed by a Board of three, headed by an experi-
enced banker. The same Commissioners control the
Savings Bank Department of the bank, and there
seems no good reason why the two departments
should be even nominally kept separate.
The Advances Department shows these results: —
Nominal
Reserves.
Taken over from Advances to Settlers
Board £14,319
Profit.
1907 . . £4,735 . . 19,054
1908 . . 4,661 23,715
Losses of A. to S. Board written off,
£17,132 6,583
1909 . . 5,390 11,973
Losses of A. to S. Board written off,
£3933 8,040
1910 .. 7,566 15,606

1912 .. 10,334 35,684
’Tie amount which this lending department owes to
the Savings branch is including
accrued interest. Of this, however, only £1,420,559
is lent out on mortgage. The balance, along with
some old funded-stock money, is nearly all lent to
still another branch —the Closer Settlement Promo-
tion Department. It’s a clumsy business. The
Savings Bank proper doesn’t lend money to settlers.
It lends to the Advances Department, which does
the lending. But the Advances Department doesn’t
lend to all settlers; there are some to whom only
the Closer Settlement Promotion Department will
lend. So after the Savings branch has borrowed
from the public, the Advances branch borrows from
the Savings branch, and then the Closer Settlement
branch borrows from the Advances branch, and the
public gets its bite. There must be a nice little lot
of book-keeping in all this, stupid circumlocution.
Under the Closer Settlement Promotion Act
(1910) “any three or more persons who are quali-
fied to make and hold settlement purchases’’ may
borrow “the whole or part of the purchase money”
of private land they are acquiring, provided that
(1) all the land is bought from one vendor, and (2)
the price does not exceed the valuation of the
Closer Settlement Advisory Board and the Savings
Bank Commissioners. But since no sane banker
would advance 100 p.c. of the purchase price, the
Commissioners require to be guaranteed against loss
by the Government, which undertakes to make any
deficit good out of the Consolidated Revenue. These
advances cost the borrowers only 44 p.c. When the
daring measure was passed the interest was fixed at
4 pc.; but as the Department is now paying that
much the Minister carried an amending Bill, under
which he can charge anything not less than 4 p.c.
Since January ] of this year 44 p.c. has been
charged. The money advanced and owing amounts
to £689,744, and the profit for the year was £783
—the only provision for the bad debts which it is
pretty certain the Department won’t escape. Only
remarkably good management backed up by remark-
ably good luck can, indeed, save the Government
from pretty heavy losses over this business.

The latest transfer of reserves brings the capital up
to £300,000, of which no less than £232,314 has
been paid up out of accumulated profits since 1904.
This makes the present comparatively modest-looking
131 P-C. dividend really equal to one of 60 p.c. on
the capital actually paid by shareholders. Even
assuming that all the capital prior to 1904 had come
out of shareholders’ pockets, it’s a glorious
example of what a well-managed fire insurance office
can accomplish once it gets over the early struggles.
The infantile mortality among fire offices is, how-
ever, very large; of a considerable litter of such cos.
born about the same time as the South British
(1872), three out of every five are now gone and
forgotten. The tendency with the infant is to
attempt to cover a continent while it can only com-
fortably cater for its own native town, and that is
generally the end of it. And pretty often the young
insurance hopeful tries to attract attention by pay-
ing dividends much bigger than it can afford, which
simply hastens the disappearance. When they don’t
do that, and consequently live and thrive, they
mostly endeavor in a few years to smother the
dividends so as not to attract attention.
The premium income has taken another leap during
the year. But still the insurer is exceptionally well
protected. Premiums for the year were £415,507,
and shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves), after
the dividend had been paid, amounted to £754,365,
or 181.5 p.c. of the premiums, a remarkably strong
position, notwithstanding the very rapid growth of
business, and therefore of risks. The next table
shows that growth and a few other things:—
Net Ex- Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. penses. Rate. Rate.

Under ordinary circumstances the loss rate would be
regarded as excessive ; but this co. does a big marine
business (it used to be the South British Fire and
Marine In. Co.), and that means big risks, although
not much expense in getting the business. So the
expense rate, taken all round, is low, and would be
still lower if the co. didn’t chase business practi-
cally all over the world.
Of its total assets of £884,976, the South British
has no less than £259,521 in premises and property
and £274,863 out on mortgage. The balance is held
in public securities (principally those of Australia
and Maoriland) and liquid assets. Interest and rent
earnings for the past year amounted to £39,006, so
only £lOOO had to come from underwriting profits
to pay the dividend. The rest, of course, is put aside
to earn more, and meantime is available to meet
claims. The big lay-by then attracts new business
while keeping the old ; and the new business, well-
selected, means more profit; and more profit means
more money to lay-by; and that means still more
business. And so it goes on. There is hardly any
stopping the fire insurance co. once it takes root.

This co.’s life js limited, wherefore there is a sink-
ing fund for the return of capital. On June 30 this
fund, which is included among the reserves, amounted
to £25,206: £22,617 of it was in bank deposits
and other liquid assets, and the balance in land.
As the table shows, £26,250, being one-fourth of the
capital, was returned six years ago ; and there being
now in hand sufficient to pay back another fourth,
the transaction, according to present arrangements, •
will be carried through on January 1 next. This
will leave £52,500 of capital in the business. When
that £26,250 is repaid, the profits will shrink by
(say) £l2OO ; on the other hand, an 8 p.c. div.
with call for only £4200 instead of £6300. The
margin of profits in excess of dividend should there-
fore be a bit wider than it is now; and it is com-
fortably wide already, seeing that in 1908 it was
computed that an annual £3020 would be sufficient
to complete the fund for the return of capital in the
required time. The co. apparently hasn’t got much
in the wav of extra business profits to look forward
to ; for gross trading revenue is. less now than it was
10 years ago, probably on account of keener elec-
trical competition, while expenses are a shade higher.

Southland Farmers’ Co-operative As-
sociation Ltd. (Invercargill, M.L.) :
