Deiici:.
1910 .. 6127 Nil 4,795
(Pref. Shares.)
1911 . . 344 6 p.c. = 196 5,335
1912 . . 3412 6 „ „ 157 96 9,000
1913 . . 5568 Nil 842 15,410
When this co. was last inspected four years ago it
was told that it would probably get into trouble;
and the trouble has arrived all right. Plain mis-
management has had most to do with it—plain mis-
management added perhaps to poor co-operation.
Four years ago the co. was told that things would
not be likely to mend till it left off its stupid plan
of having a Board of 11 directors. There are 11
directors still. If there were five or even three the
co. would have a tar better chance. As it is, the
bad debts have been appalling—just as one would
expect them to be. Also the cost of management,
etc , has been absurdly high ; and with a cumber-
some Board of 11 one would expect that to be so,
too. A new manager has been put in lately, and he
is said to have been given a free hand. If so, the
thing may have a chance, provided the members
stick to their own co. But it had better clear the
deficit right out, and be done with it. As far as
can be seen most of the loss shown during the last
couple of years really belongs to years back ; bad
debts have been kept on the books which ought to
have been provided for long ago. To straighten
things up capital should be written off— £ 2 per
share will do it. Certainly there won’t be much
ordinary capital left then —something under £7OOO
—but the money has been lost, and the sooner the
loss is buried and out of sight the better for the
business, and the fairer to the new man who is ex-
pected to make a success of it. But the manager
can’t make a success of it alone. He must have the
backing of the co-operators ; and the co-operators
shouldn’t need to be whipped or wheedled into send-
ing their business that way, as they have had to be,
judging by the amount spent on salaries, travelling
and motor expenses.
The capital would be too small for the business
even if it were intact, which it isn’t. Of ordinary
capital there is, or was, £23,027, of which £15,000-
odd has been lost; the pref. capital is £B4l7 —say
altogether £16,000 intact. The deposits, over-
drafts, and other liabilities amount to £64,327. Of
the total of these two items £36,624 is represented
by book debts (including bills), £23,657 by mer-
chandise stock, and the balance by property, plant,
etc. The co. is over-trading; and since it is most
unlikely that more capital could be got in on favor-
able terms at present, the co. had better for a while
go back on its tracks, push its cash trading, reduce
its stocks (it is apparently trying to cover too many
lines) and cut a few thousands off its expenses. In-
cidentally it will, of course, do no more gambling
in grain ; and finally it may pray that it may be
spared a repetition of the floods which last year, it
is said, cost it one way and another about £3OOO.

Compared with the money out at risk in advances,
the capital is small ; but the reserves are so solid
that there would have to be either a very sudden
and severe slump or shocking mismanagement to put
the share capital in danger; and there would have
to be a much worse slump or simply appalling mis-
management before the deposits could be affected.
Further, the shareholders (whose capital matures in
a certain period and is then withdrawable) are en-
titled to view with great satisfaction the fine margin
of profit left over after the 84 p.c. div. is provided
for. It probably won’t last, being due no doubt to
the recent keen demand for money at high rates;
but the substantial lay-by will make it easier to
pay good divs. in future—provided the management
isn’t lending on too high a basis of values. That
mistake would, of course, mean foreclosures and pro-
bably the loss of reserves when the securities came
to be realised later on.

The growth of the profits during the past few years
has been very remarkable; it suggests that, notwith-
standing the very heavy writing-down in 1906-7,
something more went for a while towards the reduc-
tion of book values. Properties in possession were
the millstone round the co.’s neck; but during
Queensland’s long run of fine seasons so much of the
millstone has been got rid of that what remains is
little more than an ornament. Altogether, these
foreclosed freeholds stand now in the balance-sheet
for only £7112 ; and, considering how Brisbane pro-
perty values have moved up during the last few
years, the chances are that a profit is shown on such
sales as have been made of late. Anyway, it is
scarcely likely that the properties have realised their
exact book-value; while if a loss had been made it
would presumably be shown like it used to be. This
suggests that the management is tucking away a
trifle somewhere to provide for the losses and shrint
ages tnat will come with the inevitable leaner tiii-
Other things suggest even more plainlv that
cautious policy is nowadays adopted—the’ fact b
instance, that, whereas the visible profits are Lji i
to about p.c. on the capital, the directors are std
content to pay a modest 5 p.c. This policy is mi
right one; for, much as Queensland may believe in
its own future, it is dead-certain that for the present
the State’s prosperity depends almost entirely upon its
erratic climate; and while a few good years
add phenomenally to its wealth, one bad’year niav
not merely reduce its income enormously, ’ but also
wipe out millions of its capital. And this co sub'
ject like others to the effects of these fluctuations
has not only its own capital to protect: it has
it 157,803 of deposits as well. That is to sav 0 f
approximately *255,000 put into mortgages ’and
other shrinkable securities (most of the advances no
doubt being given by way of assistance to pur-
chasers), about £145,000 net is other people’s
money ; and the other people will want their money
no matter what happens to the co.’s securities. Tak-
ing these risks, the shareholder ought no doubt to
get a good deal more than 5 p.c., as things are; but
he must be prepared to wait and let it come later on
through the building up of solid reserves. Solid
reserves mean public confidence; and in a business of
this sort public confidence is what the fattest divi-
dends are fed on.
Arthur Cocks & Co., Ltd., was formed
in 1911 to take over a business established by the
present Mayor of Sydney, who, about 15 years’ ago
set himself up as a wholesale trader in optical
goods. For goodwill £12,000 was paid in shares bv
the co., and that and flotation exes, represent the
deficit with which the co. started:—

There is a bad debts fund of £1269; if this is
reckoned as a reserve, the deficit (that is, the cap-
ital not represented by tangible assets) is reduced to
£5174. The sums which have been put to this
fund have not been treated as profits in the fore-
going table. The capital is now £19,520 preference
and £54,093 ordinary; and in addition to this
money there is £42,664 of the public’s in the busi-
ness, including £13,000 of fixed deposits and
£10,693 of deposits at call—the latter a rather
unusual means of financing a trading business of
this sort. Circumstances alter cases, no doubt; but
in general it wouldn’t be a very safe or satisfactory
method of finance. At the end of July the co.
had resources as follows: Capital, £73,613; liabili-
ties, £42,664; undivided profits and bad debts re-
serve, £9755; total, £126,032. To represent this
it had £66,882 in stocks, £38,146 in book debts,
£4557 in cash, and £4134 in fittings and sundries.
The other £12,313 is in goodwill and alterations
and improvements to leased premises. There is no
loading in the shape of a leasehold asset. Probably
the vendors did not think any of the co.’s leaseholds
worth any more than they were paying for them.
If they did they gave the co. the benefit of it. It
will be seen that practically all the capital is repre-
sented bv stock and the small cash balances; to
finance the business—or, rather, to allow the co. to
finance its customers—the co. has to rely on deposits
and other liabilities. There is nothing in freeholds
and very little in plant and fittings. The fact that
all the capital is in stock and none in freeholds
means, of course, that the shares are not at all a
gilt-edged investment; but it also means that on a
comparatively small capital a big turnover is pos-
sible. And that means or ought to mean fine profits.
These profits are to a great extent governed by the
stock-taking; and, (hough there is nothing about it
in the official report, it was said on very good
authority a while ago that, on a reva'luation, stocks
at one branch had been written down considerably
below the value fixed at the 1912 stocktaking. This
would mean that the profit for 1911-12 was inflated,
the 1912-13 figures having to suffer in consequence.
Head office is in Sydney ; there are branches in Mel-
bourne, Adelaide, Brisbane, and in Maoriland and
England. There is a heap of energy in the main-
springs of the business. The managing director is
the sort of man who decided that he would like to
be Mayor of Sydney, and within six years was Mayor
of Sydney, though up to that time he hadn’t taken
any interest in municipal matters. No doubt there
are other rood men in the business; still when a
big sum is paid for goodwill in a case of this sort,
it is just as well to accumulate hard profit and wipe
out that goodwill as auickly as possible, in case of
accidents. There are concerns to which a certain
roodwill value attaches pretty firmly; still if you
want to see how poor an asset goodwill is as a rule,
just turn up a directory of 35 years ago, and reckon
up how manv of the old business houses then thriving
are thriving to-day.

building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf

Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
vd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per eub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. —a saviny of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a

manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

“P.X.”; A lot of people have been inter-
ested in misrepresenting the new rule of the Svdnev
Stock Exchange, and the Stock Exchange committee
never enters into controversies ; so it is not surpris-
ing that a wrong impression prevails regarding it
The rule gives the committee power to suspend another
rule concerning what is known as buying-in. If mem-
ber Jones sells member Smiff 500 Tin Fleeces and
doesn’t deliver the scrip, on giving a couple of days’
notice Smiff may go on ’Change and buy the Fleeces
at whatever price they are offered at, no matter how
high; and Jones has to pay up. ’This is the oppor-
tunity of a crowd that is cornering a stock ; it can
make Jones pay almost any price it likes if the
corner is strong enough, and if Jones is strong enough
to stand the racket. Now, the Exchange isn’t
created to help men make fictitious prices for scrip ;
in other words, it isn’t part of its functions to
encourage corners, which are merely conspiracies
to get prices in excess of true values.' So it passed
a new rule declaring that the committee should have
power to suspend the buying-in. That is, the com-
mittee said : “You shall not use our rules to further
a conspiracy. If a member has made a contract and
has not kept it, the other party still has his remedy
at law. Let him therefore go to law, and the Judge,
on evidence, will award damages for breach of con-
tract—if he is satisfied that it was a good con-
tract.” The Exchange does not cancel any contract ;
it does not affect or suspend any legal redress, of
course. It simply says: “When our committee has
reason to believe a conspiracy is afoot, the party
who thinks he is aggrieved must go to the courts
and not come to us for redress.”

White Crystal (N.S.W.) holds a sale this
week of shares forfeited for non-payment of first call of
6d. A second call is practically certain for October, and
then no doubt there will be a “machinery” call of
Is. in the approved Ballarat manner to give the
show a mill. After that, the mine is to be asked
civilly to give the mill a show.
* * * *
Block 14-Torrington (N.S.W.) has been
milling its wolfram-bismuth ore with 10 head of
stampers. So far, the returns indicate a remarkably
low average extraction of both metals, for the mill
has had to treat from 140 to 150 tons of crude ore
to produce one ton of wolfram-bismuth concentrates.
The percentage of tungstic acid in the concentrates
should be clearly set out; “wolfram” may mean
almost anything. For a recent fortnight’s run the
mill treated 578 tons of crude ore for 3 tons
19cwt. 3qr. 241 b. of concentrates, assaying from
68.56 p.c. to 67.5 p.c. of “wolfram” and from 2.64
p.c. to 2.95 p.c. of bismuth.
* * * *
Great Cobar’s mine-manager Treloar
returned to Amurika a couple of weeks back. The
departure was quiet and no speeches were made.
Matters underground on the properties are not as they
should be, and though general-manager Bellinger may
have had trouble in getting labor for his mine out
at the dead-end of things, a word or two might also
be said about the management. The Mines Depart-
ment recently pushed the co. into connecting North
Cobar for ventilation. This had been getting worse
and worse, and the worker will only stand up to a
certain amount of heat and bad air underground.
Also there are constant complaints about inability to
obtain tools and conveniences to work with. If Bel-
linger would only inaugurate some kind of a grumble
book, to be used by the workers underground, it
would provide him with some interesting reading, and
might give him a better idea of the difficulties than
the reports he gets from shift bosses, deputations
from A.M.A., etc. Then it might also be easier to
get and keep good men, and the furnaces might, with
less difficulty, be fed up to their full capacity. Ches-
ney plant is running well, but underground things
there are behind. It would be very interesting to
see a statement of the total cost of the Chesnev plant,
and also detailed statements of the treatment costs,
etc. It’s about time a little light was thrown on
to this part of the proposition. A drought is looking
the co. in the face, too, to make things more un-
pleasant.

.T. C. Moulden, the moderately-young Aus-
tralian metallurgist who has been slowly beating
down great difficulties and making a success of the
Central Zinc Co.’s spelter works at Seaton Carew
(Eng.), has lately been appointed general manager
also of the Norse Power Syndicate, a wealthy inter-
national co. (German, English and Swedish) which is
establishing electric zinc-smelting works in Sweden.
To enable him to cope with the work of the two
cos. he, a while ago, “imported” from Australia
another good Australian, Charles Wainwright, bro-
ther of the manager of the Broken Hill South : and
there are one or two Australian foremen on the
works. Moulden is just back in England from Sarps-
borg, in Sweden, where one of the new Norse works
is just getting its finishing touches.
Approximately worth of gold
was unearthed in Westralia during the nine months
ended September 30. This is £87,000 more than
was produced for the same period in 1912, and
nearly £200,000 less than was dug up in the
first three quarters of 1911. Four new contributors.
Bullfinch Prop., Victorious, Queen of the Hills and
Edna Mav, are responsible for the increase compared
with 1912.
Malcolm Maclaren and Allan Davidson
have been telling, through the Mining and Scientific
1 kess, how tellurides were first discovered at Ival-
goorlie. The scene of the discovery was the Block
40, now included in Oroya Links. The then mana-
ger, Dick Eades, had obtained rich assays from
samples that showed little or no free gold. This
puzzled Eades, and he sent the stuff to some Kal-
goorlie and Coolgardie assayers, who suggested that
the unknown mineral was some form of iron sul-
t,ut; * ts extren| e softness at once exploded
that theory. Later on, some specimens were taken
to Earle Huntley, who had had some experience of
tellurides at Mount Shamrock (Q.), and he sug-
gested that the new mineral was a telluride of gold.
Huntley tooled away fragments of the mineral, and
then made blowpipe tests, which proved the accuracy
ot his suggestion. Confirmatory tests were made by
A. G. Holroyd, and the news of the discovery was
then given to the press. Before this publication,
Huntley, Holroyd and other experimentalists had
discussed the occurrence in all its bearings, and
saw in it the solution of the problem that for three
years had puzzled mining men at Kalgoorlie—the
source of the rich deposits of “mustard” gold and
sponge gold that were so characteristic of the
oxidised zone of the Boulder mines.
* * * *
More capital has been put up in Adelaide
to give the Chaffinch another chance—enough to
pay for a bore hole; and in view of recent develop-
! n , the no, 'thern workings of the adjoining
Bullfinch Prop., those who have subscribed believe
that something good will be struck at depth. In
that case a larger co. is to be floated to take over
the show. In the light of past experience, how-
ever, boreholes in the West are not to be trusted,
and core assays have more often than not been
delusions. It may not be so in this case, but the
experience is worth remembering.
Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has declared its
first div. of Is., and pays out on November 3. Regu-
lar quarterly distributions are expected to follow.
A reliable man in Archie Hay, formerly manager of
the Hainault, has been appointed manager in place
of Fred Morgan, deceased.
Great Boulder (W.A.) has taken an option
on the Big Bell group at Paton’s Find, 20 miles
from Cue. There is a very wide ore channel in the
property, and trial crushings have averaged over the
ounce. The show is in a locality which is alleged
to have gold, silver, mica, wolfram, tin, beryls,
emeralds and goodness knows what in payable quan-
tities, so there is a chance of the Boulder hitting
the pigeon if it misses the crow.
Mountain Queen (W.A.) has decided to
take the Great Victoria on trial. Several concerns
in search of mines, including the Great Boulder,
have tested the Victoria, but so far no one has been
game enough to make a deal. One reason has been
scarcity of water for crushing; but should the Moun-
tain Queen acquire the property it would probably
convey the ore to its own plant, six miles away, for
treatment. The low average of the stone is, however,
a disability.
* * * *
Perth Full Court ruled the other day that
in cases where a mine manager is prosecuted for
employing a foreigner who cannot speak English, the
said foreigner must be produced in court, to prove
his inability to do so. This, however, is more easily
said than done. Unintelligible foreigners have a
knack of disappearing when a mine boss is sum-
moned for having them about the mine, and they are
hard to trace. Some unfriendly persons say that
they are spirited away to another field to work in
another mine under another name, but this paper
cannot say for certain whether this is true or
not.

one still from a SDecial report bv Dr. Malcolm
Maclaren: —“Our deepest workings have added no-
thing to reserves, but the amount of ore developed in
the levels and stopes above places us in much the
same position as at the close of last year—namely,
250,000 tons in sight. No claim to strict accuracy
in regard to this estimate is made. As to grade,
the decline of Bd. per ton this year may be attri-
buted to a more general adoption of contract work
in stoping. Under this system more waste rock is
being broken. There is little doubt that the two
years’ life ahead of the mine which the reserves in-
dicate will be prolonged, but to what extent and on
what scale of profit it would be idle to predict.”
Dr. Maclaren says:—“The Kalgurli lodes pass finally
out of the favorable quartz dolerite at between 1750 ft.
and 1850 ft. It is by no means the only Kalgoorlie
mine that has encountered such conditions. Five
other important ones have suffered in the same way,
together with several smaller shows. The experiences
of all these have been identical; namely, that beyond
a short distance —generally less than 100 ft. beneath
the favorable quartz dolerite —the lode channels
fail to carry ore. In gaining this experience not less
than £345,000 and probably £400,000 has been ex-
pended in driving, crosscutting and sinking in the
unfavorable calc-schist. One co., vainly hoping
against hope, has during the past three years spent
£48,000 in this pursuit, reaching a depth of 500 ft.
beneath the contact, but recovered practically no
payable ore. In this instance the lode was far
stronger and better defined than those in the Kalgurli.
The only possibility at greater depth for the Kalgurli
therefore is that the vertical ore channels may in-
tersect favorable rock on further sinking, but I don’t
think there is any hope in that direction. lam of
opinion, however,' that a little further exploration
may be made, preferably by diamond drilling, be-
neath the 1850 ft. level, not that I have any hope of
profitable ore being obtained beneath that level, but
rather to make assurance doubly sure. Final''', if we
accept (as indeed I think we must) the exploration
on the lower levels (1750 ft. and 1850 ft.) as being
fairlv comprehensive, and if we are to profit at all
by the huge and otherwise profitless expenditure in
the calc-schist, there is one inevitable conclusion —-
namely, that it is unsound mining business to sink
deeper in the njine in search of ore bodies that both
the indications present and a wide experience of
similar conditions combine in asserting to be non-
existent.” Of course this isn’t news. The Bulletin
said two years ago that the Kalgurli had touched
bottom —in other words, that unfavorable country
had come in from the east and cut its main ore
bodies clean off. k ,
Ivanhoe’s reserves go on dwindling. The
general manager’s latest estimate of ore in sight is
1.045,500 tons, of a gross value of £2,056,150,
against 1,080,850 tons, containing £2,196,897, which
he reckoned was visible six months ago. The con-
sulting engineers (8., M and Co.) estimate the quan-
tity at 1,059,418 tons, of a gross value of
£2 105,594, which is 30,177 tons and £106,296 less
than they estimated was visible half a year ago.
Allowing 30s. a ton to cover all exes, and losses in
residues, the latest estimates give a profit in sight of
48/9 and 51/8 respectively. # ,
Hope, Beaufort (Vic.), had its machinery
burnt the other day. Then some criminal or fool set
alight to the puddlers—there isn’t much doubt that
it was a deliberate attempt to burn things down.
The pitv of it is that it happened at a time when a
strike was on over the non-unionist eruption. No sane
worker tolerates the destruction of his bread-winning
appliances. Therefore while some young ass or a
brainless unionist may have been tempted to burn
and destroy, The Bulletin declines to accept the
view that this was a deliberately planned and coldly
instigated act of unionist incendiarism.
* * * *
Burnt Creek, Bet Bet (Vic.), after hav-
ing carried on almost as long as the oldest inhabi-
tant can remember, is to shut down. Gold worth
about £lBO,OOO has been drawn from it, but divi-
dends have been only about £BOOO. Thus the work
has been for the benefit of' anybody and everybody
except the shareholders. The Board, of course,
preaches that the miner has been fed. True, but he
also has had to render unto the paymaster full ser-
vice for his wage, earned in wet ground and often
in bad air. That the Burnt Creek has to close does
not arise from lack of efficiency or the interference
of the Miners’ Association, but because the gutter was
cementy and the gold in the wash below the pay
point. Anyway, what with the heap of slime and
the plant and the other etceteras, a liquidation divi-
dend of about 4s. a share ought to be available dur-
ing the next month. Still it is a pity to see a mine
like the Burnt Creek curl up ; for one thing, another
300 miners will come into the labor market just when
there isn’t much demand for them.
* * * * *
Jumbunna Coal (Vic.) has the jumps
again. These recur just as often as one Boyd, M.P.,
and Murray, ex-Railway Commissioner, blow the big
horn. Jumbunna does a coal trade of about £40,000
a year, but with Wonthaggi State mine cutting into
the State requirements of big and small coal, the co.
fails to heap up profits while eating up its mineral
reserves. This doesn’t suit the directors, who used
to enjoy a big slice of the railway contracts and the
pickings of the small-coal trade of Melbourne. Now
that the State mine furnishes competition, the Gipps-
land private mines have to provide their own back-
bone, or burst. Boyd doesn’t, of course, say this.
What he does say (with his left eyelid on the droop)
is conveyed by the following paragraph: “But in the
face of all these difficulties we look forward with
hope that within the next 12 months we shall have
opened up an area of good, profitable coal, and that
the co. will again be earning regular dividends. The
demand for coal is greater than the supply.” That
being so, one wonders why J. A. Boyd, M.P., flared
up so at the half-yearly meeting. Of course, it is
worrying to find the State coal mine living down
criticism; but if the demand for the co.’s coal is
greater than the supply, and if the directors “expect
prices of coal to harden in the near future,” why
pipe so plaintively ?
There isn’t much in the little Mining
Companies Bill which Attorney-General Brown has
laid before the Leg. Council of Victoria, but that
little makes some Fatmen’s adipose tissue very quiv-
ery. It isn’t the proposed form of the balance-sheet
that hurts the big man, for he will be able to
escape a lot of detail if the model isn’t recast. What
he will squirm over will be the penalties to which
directors will be subjected unless they do the straight
thing a little straighter than in the past. The first
essential is the publication of a prospectus whenever
anyone attempts to form a mining co. Why not the
same over any co. that proposes to make profits out
of mine patents or products ? There have been in-
stances where such a prospectus was, and still is,
badly wanted. Other provisions are designed (1) to
make directors and promoters of cos. liable to pay
compensation for untrue or reckless statements in
prospectuses, (2) to do the same with the expert who
seeks to diddle people by making wilfully misleading
statements in prospectus reports, (3) to throw open
books of account, balance-sheets and half-yearly state-
ments to shareholders and creditors, (4) to compel
auditors to draw attention to any irregularities that
may come under their notice, (5) to make mine man-
agers send in declarations with their progress reports,
and (6) to throw open to inspection all reports not
relating to matters of advice and policy. There is a
lot more in the Bill, but these are the essentials, and
they have the unadulterated approval of every decent
mining man and every straight market jobber. When
chairmen of directors have ’phones straight into big
brokers’ offices, it’s time that the meshes of the law
were tightened. The little game whereby the director
scoffs up commissions for deals between his co. and
others ought to be spoiled—he should be
made to show his grains. Ditto also the
officials who get special gratuities and bon-
uses, as this is the fatman’s way of using company’s
money to keep sweet with useful men on the staff.
The Bulletin hasn’t much belief that the Bill will
pass; but if it doesn’t, something approaching a
model for future reform has at least been provided.
* * * *
The Tyrconnel, Hodgkinson (N.Q.), re-
ports a good development; a 3ft. reef —worth 20z., it
is said —has been met in the crosscut, and there is
another section of stone behind it of lower value.
Water proved troublesome, so this crosscut was run
in at a shallower level than was originally intended.
The Tyrconnel was famous in the ’7o’s, when it gave
a fortune to a co-operative party.
• * * *
Many Peaks (Q.) has been flourishing on
a royalty of 2s. 6d. per ton received from Mount
Morgan, which for 12 months to May 31 took 99,051
tons of pyritic fluxing ore carrying a trifle over 1
p.c. copper. But for the last 12 weeks the quantity
smelted at Morgan has been only 9477 tons (July
3929 tons, August 2970 tons, September 2578 tons).
Many Peaks in 12 months to June 30 paid 2s. sd.
per share ( £9864) in dividends, but it can’t do that
during the current year unless it finds some other
source of revenue, which at present seems unlikely.
The great hopes concerning the deposits
of kerosene shale near the Narrows (Central Queens-
land) seem to have been given a knockout. After
the usual paper puff had subsided, a geologist dropped
in; and now the country correspondent thereabouts
reports that “practically nothing is being done.”
When the country correspondent says nothing is
happening in his little kingdom, it is safe to bet that
the little kingdom is very dead indeed.
The reconstruction of the Oaks Amalga-
mated (N.Q.) has been successfully carried out, and
the Oaks (No Liability) has been formed. The capi-
tal of the new co. is £25,000, in 20s. shares, of
which 10,000 are contributing, issued as paid up to
14s. per share upon payment of 4s. per share applica-
tion and allotment. The applicants for these
contribs. receive 10,000 fully paids of 20s. each _in
proportion of one paid for every one contributing
share applied for. The remaining 5000 are held in
reserve. The new co. proposes installing a com-
pletely new crushing plant, for which manager Laby
says there is enough profitable feed in sight to last
two yea rs.
Mount Lyell (Tas.) is so far back
towards its old form that it is once more able to
furnace 1000 tons of crude ore per day. From its
last 28,721 tons (in 28 days) the converter return was:
copper 589 tons, silver 41,3430 z., and gold 7420 z.
The average is a shade over 2 p.c. copper, with from
ss. to 6s. worth of precious metals.
“Silas Snell”: A flicker drama that has
been jigging in all parts of Australia lately comes
from America, although it is highly probable, the
idea of it germinated in Australia. The story is of
a comic poultry farmer in the States, with a few
acres of poor land, a tumbled shack, and a. herd of
impoverished geese, who contrives to sell his miser-
able outfit for £lO,OOO to a speculative millionaire
bv simply salting his geese. I don’t mean to say he
pickled the feathered bipeds in ordinary brine; no-
thing so common. He stuffed some coarse gold
samples into the crops of his geese, and sold the
birds to the millionaire for culinary purposes. When
a few specimens turned up in successive geese, the
millionaire concluded the land they browsed on was
littered with the metal, and got out after those
acres with his clothes full of 50dol. bills. I fancy
the first rush that ever arose out of the goose with
the gold in his gullet happened in the vicinity of
Mount Moliagul (Vic.), close upon 40 years ago.
A Dunolly poulterer struck a few specks in the crop,
panned his goose off, and realised two ’weights. He
wasted no time, and Goose Creek rush resulted. Many
times since the domestic birds of the corner State
have picked up a few grains, and directed attention
to valuable surfacing. 1 remember an instance of
my boyhood at Waddy. “Tinker” Smith found a
very decent prospect in the crop of his Christmas
goose. He went round and cadged crops of other
Christmas geese sold by him, and in three instances
got the color. There was no shallow alluvial at
Waddy, but “Tinker” washed prospects from all over
the shop where the geese ran, and got nothing. He
was nonplussed till he remembered that his geese had
recently been in pound. The pound embraced the
Pioneer lease. But the Pioneer was a thing of the
past, its tips were removed, and little sign of the
old mine remained. After assiduous fossicking, he
struck his patch. Where the big office of the Pioneer
had stood, he scraped £7OO worth of fine gold off
about 64 sq. yards of surfacing. This represented
dust that had been lost during the long years in
which the rich returns of the Pioneer were handled
in that office, and had been discovered by a goose
where nobody but a goose would have dreamed of
looking for gold.

WOMANS
LETTERS
Sydney, October 13, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
Austral-Britannia doesn’t always rule the
waves. On the day of the “At Home” on
the Australia a stiff north-easter whipped up
a jerky sea that made the pontoon alongside
the gangway heave like a sea-sick turtle.
FT-om this pontoon a tricky set of steps stag-
gered up the side of our flagship. Two irre-
sponsible ropes, very far apart, waved like
straws as despairing woman flung herself
forward on tlieir uncertain support. The
steps fell down from the edges at an angle
of 45 degrees. Guests arrived at the top of
this perilous ascent with their coat-tails or
their skirts (as the case might be) blowing
round the scenery, and their hearts thumping
in their throats. Flaving fallen upon the
hand of something in uniform that was wait-
ing to welcome them, they stood on the firm
deck with a passionate desire not to climb
any more sea staircases. Warned by the
ascent, cautious soles scrambled down the
gangway in good time, and watched their
friends and foes drop off the lower steps and
arrive with a bang on the pontoon.
Archbishop Wright, very neat and nimble
in the region of his gaiters, came down
swiftly. Mrs. Archbishop, with a worried
expression about her ankles, tottered in mid-
air, clutching at her skirts. His Legs made
a gallant attempt at rescue, but the lady
waved him off and bumped the landing stage
alone. John Lane-Mullins formed himself
into a salvage party of one, and, being of a
lean and active build, dashed up the steps
to hand down the faint-hearted. Mrs. C. E.
Waters, very fair and plump, made a joyous
descent in a Mandalay-blue silk coat over a
tussore frock. Mrs. George Earp was clad in
something surprising in blue and crimson.
Mrs. Luscombe was a vision in cream dra-
peries, tied about the waist with floral silk.
Mrs. W. A. Holman, dressed in a shot-silk
gown that flashed in the sun, gripped the
hand of her only chick. Portia. This Titian-
haired infant was frocked in the latest style,
and her small bonnet must have caused con-
vulsions of envy in the breasts of less decor-
ative babes. Mrs. W. Macleod and brother
Gerald O’Brien and the Henniker Heatons
were of the party. Mr. Justice Sly handed
Mrs. Justice safely on board the wherry’.
Mrs. Allen Taylor made a strenuous ascent
just ahead of Hugh Ward’s comely dame,
who, on the journey, gave herself up for lost
at least 16 times. Yet nobody had to be
fished out of the harbor. But the man who
said in his haste that he had never seen so
many legs in his life as he did at Randwick
on Saturday was not present at our Flag-
shirt’s great ankle display.
Chief Justice Cullen and his gentle dame
gave a garden party in excelsis to Admiral
Patey and his sea-lions. Few gardens near
Sydney can compare with the Cullens’ acre-
age of native bush. The lovely tangle of
primeval forest has been just sufficiently
touched up by a landscape gardener to keep
its natural profusion in place. Winding
paths fall down into gullies where fern and
flower grow wild. On the upper terrace,
where are trim lawns and the tame blos-
soms of suburbia, the Cullen pair welcomed
the old lions and the young cubs to Tregoyd.
The Patey profile, under a cocked hat, was
turned towards the great splash of blue
water that laps North Head; thus
anchored beside his hosts he said
how-d’you-do to the party. Parsons
galore and a solid helping of the law
gave a serious background to the frocking.
This was highly respectable, not to say dull
—the X-ray skirts and gartered knees that
pranced round the lawn at Randwick have
given us a taste for comic-opera in daylight.
Governor Strickland and his fair-haired
eldest treasure, Miss Mary, were more or
less attended by A.D.C. Talbot, who wore
an aggrieved exrtression and immaculate
frocking. Mrs. Ethel Turner Curlewis, a
determined gardener in her off-the-pen
moments, went off with hubby to ex-
plore the secret fastnesses of the
Cullens’ bush. The Spencer Brunton
lady sailed across the gravel path in
the sartorial event of the afternoon. This
was a stupendous Paris creation of sand-
colored satin, top-dressed with a floral silk
tunic of so many complications that no lay
mind could presume to say where it hooked
and eyed. Ex-Flag-FJeutenant Seton
escorted his best girl, Miss Joan Deck, of
the willow-wand figure and grey velvet eyes,
to the tea-cups. Squatter Kenneth Mackay,
of Wallendbeen, looked in. A learned sec-
tion from Sydney Uni. helped to give a tone
ro the occasional frivolity of diaphanous
frocks. One of the nicest-looking young
persons present, Miss Joyce Allen, was de-
murely clad in a white lingerie frock that
might have walked out with an archbishop.
At the Fleet Ball at State Government
House Governor Strickland and his lady,
with our own sea-dog Patey at their side,
gave the welcoming hand to a numerous
throng. General Finn and A.D.C. Talbot sped
the arriving guests to the sub-regal gloves.
Rose-tinted shades in the reception room
were most helpful to the dead-white paste
with which Society girls are smearing their
mask-like countenances. Mrs. Baronet
Cooper, a fine, broad-shouldered specimen
of Australian womanhood, glittered with
diamonds in the crush. Selections from
the large Knox family, the Henniker Heaton
maiden in dove grey plumage, the C. G.
Wade pair, a generous helping of Cabinet
Ministers and their female belongings, and
a terrific concourse ot dancers who had
scarcely room for the soles of their feet,
twirled round the congested ball-room. Mrs.
Trefl4 shepherded a sprightly flock of nice
looking dancers. Miss McCrone, a pretty
Temora girl, robed in blush-pink, and a
youthful Miss Barlow, were included in the
group. The Admirable King-Hall took one
of his last bird’s-eye views of Sydney
Society’s bare necks at this party.
A ready-reckoner declared that he
counted 40 superfluous girls, owing to a
shortage of the male dancer. The male who
is prepared only to lean up against a wall
till supper-time was plentiful, while wall-
flowers bloomed profusely on the verandahs.
The Citizens’ Reception to Admiral
Patey and his officers took the form of a
gigantic evening crush in the Town Hall.
At 8.30 p.m. on October 9 the front steps of
that useful building were packed with
slowly-moving bodies, advancing at the rate
of one inch per minute. Town Clerk Nes-
bitt and his next-in-command, the indispens-
able Layton, hovered round the Mayor; and
some of the naval frills, extra-special, were
dared to leave the spot till they had been
shaken by the 2000th hand. On the plat-
form a Union Jack buried something very
large and suggestive of the shield and bell
for presentation to the cruiser Sydney.
This presentation was to have been the
star turn of the evening. The crowd
moved forward to see the Gaud Mayor,
clothed in great majesty, hand over to
Skipper Glossop the solid symbols of our
goodwill. But the company heard with a
shock that the shield and bell were not
ready, after which it glared at two framed
representations of the gifts.
Tall women must have blessed their height
at this crush. Mrs. George Fuller and
her daughter Gladys were among the few
thus able to inspect the scenery; Mrs.
Neville Mayman was a head and a white
tulle bandeau over the multitude of short
and stout. Mrs. Joe Cook ambled through
the throng. The Pritchard Bassetts trod
warily, especially when the fierce rush for
refreshments set in. Mrs. E. W. O’Sullivan
and daughter Eva found breathing space
near the dais, sacred to anything with a
long tail to its name. The glimmer of
green and gold lights, and the swinging
canopy of feathery smilax overhead, turned
the Town Hall into a place of beauty.
But the presentation which was not a pre-
sentation gave a chill to the middle of the
entertainment.
At the State schools’ day-out on the
Cricket Ground the kiddies made a
map of Australia with a coastline
about 10 youngsters deep; and Mrs.
Admiral Creswell gave the Australia presen-
tation flag a jerk and sent it up the flag-
pole. While it flirted with the breeze, the
youngsters had time to chortle with pride
over the fact that their pennies had bought
this bunting, with some self-denial in the
matter of bulls’-eyes and marbles. Peter
Board, FJnder-Sec. for Education, handed
over the handsome rag to Lieut.
Enman, of the Australia. The squad
of attendant bluejackets was small,
but the wild enthusiasm of the native-
born was as big as if an armada was
being greeted. Governor Strickland was
officially shadowed by Admiral Creswell,
with Defence Minister Millen lurking in
their wake. Lady Edeline drove on to the
ground and had a young daughter under her
wing.
Saturday, the final flutter at the Spring
Meeting, was a gorgeous day for frocks.
W oman strode about the lawn in gossamer
muslins with cob-web lingerie, obviously a
two-legged creature. Garters with paste
buckles glittered over one knee of the occa-
sional lady in the last gasp of bizarre fashion.
Silk tights, or the frilly garments that are
usually only exhibited on back-yard clothes-
lines when washing-day comes round, were
displayed under silky transparencies that
were supposed to cover the forms of the most
daring. Low-necked corsages are bringing
their own Nemesis. Tanned and freckled
collar-bones and muddy-brown necks are the
net result of the present scheme of undress.
Hats, which perched like thimbles on the
coiffures of the Elect, have grown an inch or
two larger than they were at the Autumn
Meeting. The spiky wheat-ear trimming,
which is a cheap substitute for the exquisite
flowers of the milliners’ rose-beds, is not
alluring. The fashionable girl is necessarily
angular to fit her 1913 clothes, and she is
too much like a wheat-ear herself to stand
more spikes on her head.
The white hand-made lingerie gowns were
amongst the most attractive signs of the joy
of life at Saturday’s meet. The tall Brennan
sisters, Florence and Ad&le, were clad in
these frothy garments, with Heaven knows
how many yards of hand-sewn lace worked
into the ground plan. Mrs. Kenna was
clothed like a lily, or like a lily would be if
it could order a frock of white sponge-cloth
and a large leghorn hat. The incident of
motoring into a tram, or being tram-squeezed
in a motor, on the way to Randwick, gave
Mrs. Kenna a rather startled expression,
which didn’t wear off till teatime. Dorothy
King kindly showed us a sample of Noo Yark
style, in a plaid-green silk blouse-tunic over
a dark skirt, and a remarkable plumed toque.
Lizard-skin shoes finished this tricky toilet.
Solicitor Norris tore himself away from
the male members’ stand which tolerates (at
high prices) but does not encourage the
female sex in its sacred enclosure. Fie paced
the lawn for five minutes with pretty sister
Etta, whose champagne suit and cerise hat
was the thing for a blue-eyed girl, then slip-
ped away to the eyrie where Woman is
frankly regarded as a nuisance. * From its
Olympian heights, strewn with cigar-ends
and other male bric-h-brac, a superb view of
the Horse can be enjoyed, unbroken by a
tulle bow. Mrs. Tom Rolin put her hand-
some head into one of the new squashed satin
toques. An indeterminate blue gown that
went with this millinery was cloaked in
black, with a dashing flick of rose-color on
its collar. Sheila Chisholm, in a duck-egg
green coatee and a white skirt, looked rather
larger round the waist than a plump young
clothes-prop. Mrs. Bertie Rouse, one of the
most decorative young matrons from the
sheepwalks of Mudgee, put her attractive
countenance under a feathered grev hat that
curled all over in ostrich fluff. The Shep-
heard Laidley damsel tossed her haughty nose
under a small-brimmed headgear, and swished
a white satin race-coat round her exclusive
angles. Mrs. Tom Hughes and Mrs. Ernest
Wilshire were neck and neck as wearers of
the two best-looking black and white gowns.
The A.J.C. Committee is alleged to have
moved tons of obstacles to try to get the
roof ready for the Randwick grand-stand
crowd. Some far-off cause, a strike in Eng-
land, or the small-pox, or the Balkan row,
prevented the arrival of the iron girders in
time. Hence the wails of the roofless.
Wagner’s “Mastersingers of Nuremberg”
interrupted Society’s evening meal on Satur-
day. A stern managerial foreword threat-
ened to leave latecomers on the doormat of
Her Majesty’s Theatre if they failed to be
seated at 7 p.m. In spite of this warning, a
belated dinner party or two and a few
casuals straggled in after that witching hour.
They had to stand in gloomy silence, as part
of the background, till the curtain rolled
down on Act 1. For this mercy all the early
arrivals whose feet they would otherwise
have stamped over had much cause for thank-
fulness.
Mrs. Hugh Ward had the Holmans as her
guests in the manager’s box. Dr. Stewart
McKay was in the opposite box, with two
ornamental young women shepherded by a
discreet dame. Prof. MacCallum and his
tall lady climbed into the front row of the
d.c. Mrs. Mac is a German, and too pro-
foundly musical to be submerged in a stall
during an evening-out (it’s really two-thirds
of a night-out) with Wagner. The Arthur
Allens also forsook the fashionable burying-
ground in the mid-stalls, and camped in the
centre of the d.c. This usually-snubbed area
gathered up most of the professional musi-
cians who hadn’t clambered into the gallery.
Here conductor Joseph Bradley, of the Phil.,
drank in the singing tones of the orchestra
fiddles. Arundel Orchard, of the Liedertafel,
wore. an ecstatic expression in the same
vicinity. Miss Mary Fairfax and her
mamma —the latter just better after a severe
bout with vaccination in the third degree—
were also perched aloft. In the front row
sat a small child in large pink hair-bows and
spectacles. She gradually sank down into a
limp heap of white lingerie as the night
wore on and minstrel Walther showed no
signs of abating his melodious bellow for
love of Eva, the goldsmith’s yellow-wigged
daughter.
In the stalls, the tops of a few well-known
heads were just visible in the sixteenth-cen-
tury twilight. The musical McQuades
flanked the crescendo whiskers of the amaz-
ing Teuton conductor Eckhold, whose mem-
ory for Wagner is as large as the hold of a
cargo steamer. Soldier Finn and the ladies
of his household were further back, but
within long-distance range of the stout, pink
legs of one of the Nuremberg apprentices.
Jeanne Brola, as Eva, meandered through
“The Mastersingers” with coquettish smiles,
long yellow plaits and some pleasing top
notes. She was shadowed by a faithful
attendant (Magdalene) in flower-pot millin-
ery and voluminous drapery. Robert Parker
towered over the rest of the company as the
shoemaker poet. Hans Sachs. Tenor Spencer
Thomas, the Walther of Saturday night’s
performance, was a tow-headed dandy, his
generous waist-line decorated with a six-
teenth century sash like a slice cut from a
red and black draught-board. He warbled
his love for Eva with a lung power that made
the chequered support strain at its moorings,
and caused the legs of his long buff boots
to sag with emotion. “The Mastersingers” is
chiefly a male operatic show, though some of
the apprentices who act as chorus were of
the female persuasion. The large, pink
understandings, already alluded to, were of
this sex, and almost as impressive as the
calves that Ivy Schilling fli nes ahm . .
musical comedy. g about m
Society, wearied by a dizzy week »
stealthily yawning behind its program
when "God Save” opened the * S 8“?
By 11.30 p.m. it was getting
its wraps with a dazed expression of'w
knowing whether it was to-morrow ml ;
or the day before yesterday. ° rnins
John McCormack has farewelled this town
m a burst of enthusiasm that could do nothin*
less than bang chair-legs on the Town HaU
floor. This form of applause is more stimi
lating to the singer than to the city’s fund
ture. The choir seats worked themselves inm
an encore frenzy at Friday and Saturday
nights’ concerts, and laid hands on John him
self, while portion of the audience, under the
galleries, seized anything movable and hit it
hard on the flooring timber. When
McCormack got into his last group of
songs a male occupant of a reserved
chair was so charmed with “Oft in the Chilly
Night” that he joined in and hummed like
a top. May Huxley on Friday was gowned
in greenish-yellow plumage—the color of pale
limejuiee seen through a glass bottle. It
matched the girl’s pretty canary-like twitter
and the sleek gold of her shining hair.
Bishop Trower, who hails from Broome
(W.A.), has been staying at the Strick-
lands’ vice-regal cot. Broome is in the
semi-tropic North-West, where “black” Aus-
tralians are on the lookout for signs of
enfeebled health among the whites; but they
don’t get much encouragement from the
appearance of Bishop Trower. He was the
finest-looking specimen of robust manhood in
gaiters at the Cullens’ garden party; and
the saucy little black satin bows on his silk
hat were much admired by ladies who have
a taste for ecclesiastic millinery.
Red frocks in flimsy summer materials are
first favorites with sparkling brunettes. Miss
Winifred Ward, a young daughter of the
Daily Telegraph’s editor, was clothed in
a specially cute combination of red flicked
with black at a recent Fleeting show. The
black tail was the last gasp of fashion: it
was in the form of a sash-end that suddenly
escaped out of a small slash at the waist and
fell in a knotted end down a straight-back
breadth.
Flowers, in tight little early-Victorian
posies, and flimsy tulle bows, with the general
appearance of belonging to multi-winged but-
terflies, ran riot in some of the race hats.
Ostrich feathers in small colonies waved
above the heads of our haughtiest damsels.
This manner of adornment rages with un-
abated fury (the fury occurs when the bills
come home to Papa), and strikes overseas
visitors in a heap. Artist Ethel Phillips Fox
says she has never seen so many feathers a(
the other end of the earth at any one gather-
ing as danced in the breeze at the Cullens’
garden party.
The squatting Bucklands, of Pine
Ridge, are in town for the October
uproar of horse and fleet. The good-
looking Doris is going to act as brides-
maiden to Doris Hermann —a pretty little
dark-browed girl of the Rebecca type, a sister
of the well-known Mrs. Bertie Sheldon, who
is gadding on the other side. Miss Her-
mann’s young man is one of the numerous
Ryries. The engagement is almost brand-
new, but details of the orange-blossoming are
already oozing through in kimono confi-
dences.
Now that ex-Flag-Lieut. Seton retires from
the limelight to the instructional staff
of the Tingira, Society will mark the new
man, Poe. for its own. He flag-lieutenants
on our H.M.A.S. Australia, and was here
before, in his middy days. A large, well-
set-up sailor, with a sharply-defined manner,
he has a strong belief in the seaworthy
qualities of Australian tars.
Captain Glossop, of the H.M.A.S. Sydney,
made the speech at the Citizens’ Reception
party in the Town Flail. His hearty grati-
tude for favors to come—the bell and shield
which are to go to the Sydney being still in
the hands of craftsmen —was jerked out in a
lively fashion, with an effervescing finish.
Skipper Glossop is one of the lean, active
sailor-men, who look as if they could almost
blow through a chink in an argument with-
out losing a hair. His joyous thanks for
the bell(e) of Sydney, since the country
gave not only its sons but its daughters to
the navy, was a climax much appreciated.
Already Society campaigners are inquiring
whether the joyous Glossop is “settled” in
life.
Among the crowd that watched the lusty
babv-Fleet come home was Mr. Henry
Richards, of Chatswood, who belongs
to the family of that Captain Richards who
carried to London headquarters the dis-
natches reporting Nelson’s victory at
Trafalgar.
Barrister-Journalist J. D. Fitzgerald has
stepped ashore again after a scamper
through U.S. and Europe.
Rose Day, on October 18, is going to tear
cash from the general public from dusty morn
till thirsty eve. Fifty-six charities, with
hungry wolves gnawing at their vitals, are
waiting for the results. The Strickland lady
is going to drive round town and inquire into
the financial statement about noon.
Yours affectionately,
YANDORIAN.

‘ MEETING of Members will.be held in the Royal
Exchange on MONDAY, 27th OCTOBER, 1913, at
noon.
business :
To receive Reports and Balance-Sheet to 30th Sep-
tember, 1913.
To elect one Director in place of A. G. Milson,
Esq., who retires under Clause 82 of the Articles of
Association, and who offers himself for re-election.
To elect one Director to fill the vacancy caused by
the death of Lesley G. Herring, Esq.
To elect two Auditors in place of Messrs. T. F. H.
Mackenzie and F. C. Jordan, who are eligible for
re-election.
To consider any other business of which due notice
shall have been given in accordance with the Articles
of Association.

Manager.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 13, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The weather played a wretched trick on
the spring fashions that went out to the
Caulfield Guineas on Saturday. It was
blazing hot when they fluttered out; but a
cold change whistled up, and dresses that
were mostly chiffon were no protection
against it. Worried chauffeurs laden with
fur wraps hunted for their she-bosses on the
crowded stand, while the unmotored looked
on enviously and wondered if they would
escape pneumonia. Most of the Jews were
warmly at home because it was the Day of
Atonement. The chosen race seems always
to score, somehow. The G.-G. pair let the
races slide and took the brother and sister-
in-law down to Queenscliff to visit the chil-
dren. Miss Knatchbull-Hugessen (I’m get-
ting tired of that post-impressionist
name!) had a whole corps of Gentle-
men Helps with her. She floated in lilac
plumes and lilac silk, with violet shoes,
until the sun disappeared, when she muffled
herself hastily in a heavy fur coat. Lieut.-
Gov. John Madden sat in the vice-regal box
with his long white hair blowing under his
silk hat. His dame wore a crown of up-
standing black tulle and a voluminous
wrap. After responding to the Anthem-
burst, they proceeded, attended by ilelp
Crespin, to the old-fashioned green and gold
chariot, which is dragged by a pair of fat
chestnuts and steered by a fat coachman,
supported by a thin footman —the denizens
of the Madden box-seat since the beginning
of history.
There was an air of furtive and unseemly
gaiety about some of last week’s opera-
haunters. The grand finale was at hand —-
the long, listening job was ending. On
Wednesday “The Flying Dutchman” stirred
the house, and some hilarious squattocrats
were almost turned out for their flow of holi-
day spirits. Brain culturists who still
retained some vestiges of their musical pose,
turned in their seats and hissed like law-
abiding snakes over their too-plump or too-
scraggy shoulders. (The woman with the
just-right shoulders always smiles.) In
most cases the merriment abated before the
hovering usher arrived on the scene like an
apologetic black ghost. The Government
House people, just returned from the Fleet
celebration in Sydney, did not put in an
official appearance; but the Barttelot dame
brought the Hon. Harold Pearson as escort,
and the two laughed and chatted in ordinary,
paid-for seats. She wore a beautiful fur
coat and a wreath of diamond leaves. Mrs.
Quinlan, in white satin and diamonds, occu-
pied a whole box. Bernard Hall lurked
behind the curtain in a stage box, and his
wife just showed an artistic blue snood and
a glimpse of brown hair. Mrs. Busst, clad
in petunia, pouted: “I don’t know why I
came —I’ve been every night recently to
opera in Germany!” There, now! Mrs.
Geoff. Syme was back in her old seat. She
looked well, and showed no traces of her
recent illness.
The only Quinlan production of “Tristan
and Isolde” was most effective. The music
is sugary for Wagner; it must have a fat-
tening effect, because the principals who sing
it have got record waist-measurements. The
opera, being distinctly sung in English, re-
vealed to Mrs. Grundy that Isolde was
nothing less than a hussy. On that ground,
some moral folk sternly refused to admire
the leopard-spotted bridal train, or the wed-
ding head-dress that looked like a milk-shake
machine. Parker, as Kurvenal, the servant
of King Mark, gets all the fat, so far as the
dressing is concerned. He wears his skins
with an air, and manages a great silver-wolf
cloak with feminine skill. Just here I want
to edge in a word or so against the usual
stage couch. It is never the luxurious nest
of cushions and rugs that it should be; con-
sequently, the pronounced curves of the
massive prima donna are cruelly revealed,
and if she wears large boots, they look like
snow-shoes.
The last night of the opera drew a thor-
oughly musical audience. Instead of the
usual mixed programme, which attracts the
semi-musical populace like a circus, one
whole opera was staged—the intensely
modern “Louise.” First-nighters were not
officially on duty; but the really musical
ones came along and sat in any old seats
they could get. Their excitement when they
discovered some former neighbor in a new
location was touching to witness. There
was a great rally at sunper time in the
lounge, and the cherry-ribboned girls were
kept busy. Apparently, no one had been
so unfashionable as to dine in time for a
seven o’clock opera; or perhaps they just
pretended they hadn’t, in order to excuse
their appetites.
What with aides and visitors, the G.-G.
house party has grown to such proportions
that it requires two theatre boxes to hold it
when there is anything special to see. The
tribe spread itself comfortably in two
pens for “Louise.” The Denman lady was
late. She had been visiting her youngsters,
who are down with something or other at
Queenscliff. Her Ex. had to fit a motor trip
and a fumigating ceremony between an after-
noon tea-party and the opera. The G.-G.,
heavily decorated, arrived to time, in charge
of a batch of ladies, including Miss Knatch-
bull-Ilugessen, the Barttelot dame and Mrs.
Guise. A cloud of Helps trotted dutifully
behind. Amongst them was the new man,
Captain Pollok. His most noticeable fea-
tures are very round, black eyes,
Audrey Chirnside hovered in the back-
ground during the interval. She wore
wreaths of roses round her transparent chif-
fon cloak—a Honolulu belle effect. The
beauties of a prominent brewery family were
boxed at the far side. At last curtain-fall,
some enthusiasts stood up and clamored for
Quinlan, while the homing ushers hurriedly
covered the vacated plush seats with dingy
canvas. But Quinlan was far on his way
to Sydney and echo and a blank curtain-
face answered the uproar, while the wise
people were tumbling downstairs and fighting
their way into trams.
The Q ua mby is the club of the young
matrons, and the Denman lady is the star
mem ber. Last Thursday she was welcomed
back with fl owerß chatter and tea, and her
c j ubmat es were so glad to see her that they
tore her c i o thes, not quite off her back, but
near i y so . When the victim walked into the
up .g o i ng lift, she was a picture of coolness
anc j self-possession in turquoise crepe, with
a girlish fichu of white net round her shoul-
(iers. When the scrum was over, two
devoted Helps assisted a flustered, tattered
visitor from the premises, and deposited her
tenderly in a purple motor. Mrs. Hennessy
signified her husband’s return to power and
the dust problem with a red dres*. clamped
securely to her form with gun-metal fixings,
Airs. Leslie Stuart broke away with her
lovely brocaded vest intact; but a coil of
golden hair over one eye showed that she had
h, a( l a , struggle. Mrs. Geoff. Syme got
through safely m a tailored black chiffon.
She sheltered under a wide, cherry-colored
hat, with a rose budded on it. Mrs- Miller
wore her ostrich feathers round her neck,
I orrest dame got herself into a cosy
corner and helped several other dowagers
to a roost of refuge. Mrs. Wallach and
Mrs. Dr. Gardiner bravely wore twin crea-
tions straight from Pans—lovely blue things
with white neck ruffles. Mrs. I rell cam-
paigned in black. Mrs. Howard Lonnell
remarked cuttingly from underneath a smart
a t that it was worse than a charity com-
nuttee meeting. (A hen I tell you that her
dress was of the sort that silences all cnti-
cism. will see at once how crushing it
must have been.) Mrs. Horden, secretary
a hundred chanties, hioked indignantly
over a tussore shoulder. But what was one
tussore shoulder against such fearful odds?
On Wednesday, October 8, Professor
Peterson’s flapper-daughter was wedded
amidst great social excitement to Captain
Harold Mackworth, of the Royal Engineers,
It was the romance of a couple of months,
The Captain, who was reckoned a great
catch, came to Australia just about the
time the Peterson girl was putting up the
hair that used to dangle prettily in two long
Marguerite plaits. It is said that several
college boys went white and resolved to
forswear women and go out into the wilder-
ness when news of the engagement reached
them. The wedding was at Scots’ Church,
where the dreamy, grey-haired musical pro-
fessor led his white-clad girl up the aisle
to a gaudily-uniformed bridegroom, who
was supported by Private Secretary Wilber-
force, of the G.-G. staff, and Frank Peter-
son. Gentleman Help Barttelot’s twin boys
acted as pages (they are really only para-
graphs) and supported the bridal train,
Beryl Fanning and Concie Hamilton, two
cheery flappers, bridesmaided excitedly in
pink, with pink water-lilies on their frocks
and hats. The Peterson couple, Captain
Mackworth and the little bride held a recep-
tion subsequently in the nearly-completed
Melba Hall, at the University. The Bart-
telot pair, Mrs. Guise and Chamberlain
Nevill attended, and Emmertons, Millers,
Clarkes and others added either fashionable
feathers or brain-waves to the function,
Finally the bride put on a cream cloth suit
with a rose-colored sash and a smile, and
went off with her bridegroom to Diamond
Creek.
Ballarat takes its Arts and Crafts seri-
ously; so Alec Peacock spread his oratorical
tail during the recent opening of the Applied
Arts Exhibition. One of the interesting
personalities connected with the show is
Becky Macdonald, Mrs. President Brind’s
secretary. Becky is a smart Melbourne girl
who finds time to play the round game of
Society and write political stuff for leagues,
as well as doing the secretary job. Mrs.
Dr. Courtenay is responsible for a fine ex-
hibit of enamels, entirely her own work, from
first process to last. Melbourne matrons
who hail from Ballarat brought along
samples of their needlework, and blatted
more or less amiably. An Italian woman,
though, carried off the stitch honors with
some beautiful work of a kind that is seen
here only in the most expensive imported
trousseaus. She sits out in the sun doing
her exquisite stitches, and explains that, in
her native village, every housewife does the
same, as soon as the washing-up is done. The
village gossip flies up and down from one
shady vine-patch to another while the work
is going forward. Several Ballarat girls
have caught the craze, and, under the eye
of the expert, are trying the whole restful
process—bright silk head-scarf and all. I
think it is the head-scarf that attracts.
Ambrose Patterson has a choice assort-
ment of pictures suspended in a Centreway
room. The Denman lady drifted in unoffi-
cially last Thursday, and brought out a red
spot or two by stabbing her cheque-book.
Chamberlain Nevill fled upstairs ahead of the
partv to tell everyone who was coming ; and
the new Help, Captain Pollok, brought up
the rear, wearing his usual round-eyed ex-
pression of frozen amazement. The Best
People, who happened to be flocking to a
party below-stairs, on seeing Greatness
approach, suddenly remembered their invita-
tions and hurried along. Mrs. Hennessy,
who trifles with art, inspected the show
closely, and the Millers’ daughter Mary
struck an effective, willowy pose. Mrs.
Bowes Kelly, Mrs. Miller and Mrs. Hawley,
all expensively clad, rustled devotedly in
the train of her Ex., who peered at the can-
vases through a pair of spectacles mounted
on a skewer. Mrs. Ambrose was a charm-
ing uncatalogued feature of the show. The
little matron looked pretty as any picture in
a bunchy granny hat, a lovely complexion,
and a frock with a touch of blue in it.
The Repertory Theatre production of
“The Two Mr. Wetherbys,” in aid of the
Mission to the Streets and Lanes, attracted
to the Athenaeum an audience which con-
tained a fair number of non-theatregoers,
the result being that many of St. John
Hankin’s gibes and tilts at matrimony and
other highly respectable institutions were
greeted with outbursts of enthusiastic
silence. Mrs. Lowther Clarke, just back
from the Cold Country, where her gaitered
spouse has been making inquiries as to the
proper time for taking up the collection,
occupied a front stall. The daughter of
Merritt, M.L.C., sold confectionery, her
Amazonian stature provoking the customary
gasps of astonishment and admiration.
Three more engagements in the social vor-
tex : (1) Phyllis Clegg is billed to wed with
Simon Fraser, son of the rich Simon. (2)
The saucy little Commans girl, daughter of
Consul Commans, has said “Yes,” or words
to that effect, to Mr. Brownell, of Golds-
brough, Mort and Co. That is two flappers
disposed of matrimonially. The next case
(3) is that of the young widow Langlands
Jack. She is to wed with one of the several
Parliamentary Thompsons.
Ronald, second son of Toorak widow
Larking, and a fine specimen of Oxford
“tone,” has succumbed to Miss Mataas, a
nice Dutch girl. The Larkings will put in
another year travelling before returning to
these parts. It must be either very beauti-
ful or very boresome to belong to the
moneyed unemployed.
Pretty Fairlie Rede has opened a dogs’
coffee palace, for Most Exclusive brutes
only, at the maternal residence in Toorak.
Already a large pack of tykes in flowered
dressing-gowns bark together and annoy the
family bulldog, whose feelings have been
cruelly outraged. It appears to be his dear-
est wish to murder the whole lot. This dog
retreat will save the grey matter of the
fashionable tripper, who may now set out
contentedly and send the four-legged baby
picture postcards of the Pyramids.
Some time next month Marjorie, the
second daughter of Dr. Arthur Naylor, of
Loch (which is in Gippsland), is to take
Cecil Le Plastrier to husband. Cecil is a
patent attorney in the office of Phillips,
Ormonde and Co., and he speaks of the
bride-elect as the most wonderful invention
he has ever met.
There is sorrow in Toorak over the long
continued illness of the youthful Mrs.
Charles Bright, wife of the Deputy P.M.G.
“A little better, thanks,” is the latest sick-
room bulletin.
Mrs. Tallis turned up for the last Quinlan
night. The frocky lady with the figure had
been recovering from a chill acquired whilst
golfing.
Violet Paget, with husband Hugh Buckler
and other members of the co., plays tennis
every day when she is not travelling with
the show. This is a precaution against the
Flesh, which is the Devil. Whilst they
were appearing in Melbourne, the band of
mimes hired an asphalt tennis court,, and
the local nobility and gentry occasionally
brought along their racquets and joined in.
E. J. Tait, of Her Majesty’s, Melbourne,
'has been appointed general manager for
J.C.W., Ltd., in Sydney, and moves on at
once to that capital. I don’t know when
he will get there. Nobody knows nowadays
when he will get there, the express having
developed an apparently incurable habit of
dawdling. And it is just as bad on the
trip this way.

On Friday the Victoria League Committee fare
welled Lady Bosanquet (who is also off to the Hillo
in the Zoological Gardens. There, while the monkevs
shrieked approval, and the crocodiles wept and the
birds sang, and the elephant thudded mournfully
round beneath its immature human load, tea was
drunk and food sunk, and all was bliss. ’ It was „
great day for the Zoo. a
Draper James Marshall (why not Draper Marshall
as well as Doctor Smith?) was married on Tuesday
to Miss Stacy, of Prospect. The get-away was accom-
plished with a quietude that amounted almost to
furtiveness. Enough if I mention in a stage whisper
that the Eastern States were headed for, and the
afternoon’s express patronised.
General Maria Gordon has been here on business-
military business, 1 mean. He stayed for the Steeple
on Wednesday, and greeted more friends on the lawn
in the course of one brief afternoon, than you would
imagine any one martial head could remember for
five minutes.
John Duncan’s death came with tragic suddenness.
He fell seriously ill on Sunday, was operated
upon on Monday morning, collapsed on Tuesday night,
and died early on Wednesday morning. He refused
office in more than one Ministry. As a youngster he
was the first to take the news of the discovery of the
Wallaroo copper mines to his uncle, Walter Hughes.
He came to South Australia from Scotland in '54
aged nine, and entered Parliament when he was 26’
He had large station properties in the Lower and
Upper North, and took a big part in the develop-
ment of the State’s copper industry.

black hat. On Wednesday she wore a collarless frock
of blue ninon draped over white lace, and her daugh-

cerise and black, and her sister in navy over helio-

cream coat and skirt), Mrs. Turner, Mrs. O’Leary,
Mrs. George Bagot. The slit tailored skirt and high
boots of Rupert Clarke’s picturesque lady gave food
for conversation when all else failed.

“Nulla” : With a judicious expenditure of
sin, skill, sweat and sorrow 1 can generally manage
to make some headway with the ordinary man-power
variety of boat; but as a snag and a takedown for
the uninitiated, commend me to the outback river
craft hight the “flattie.” Together with an un-
amphibious mate, I essayed to cross the Murrumbidgee
at Hay in one t'other day. Seizing the alleged oars,
my mate strove manfully, and at the end of 10
minutes we were fully five yards from the bank,
whereupon Bill, with characteristic unselfishness,
vacated his seat with the remark that I could have
a cut. My time for five yards was not so good as
Bill’s, but, by dint of frequent changes and much
blistering and bad language, we eventually won
through. The unkindest cut of all, however, came from
an ancient, hairy individual on the opposite bank,
who only stopped howling directions to us to emit
discordant chuckles at our progress. A poet has
mentioned something about “the rude forefathers of
the hamlet, but it wasn t until the ancient ventured
the opinion that “It’d take a long while t’ git t’
Wagga,” that I realised how really rude some fore-
fathers can be.
* * * #
“Gouger” : About, the softest snap Binghi
has in the eating line up North is that slow-footed
crawler, the porcupine. Hearing footsteps, the
animal turns into an animated pin-cushion, pointing
its thousand quills all over the compass, and waits,
kidding itself that mere bluff is going to stop the
prod of a spear. When turned on his back, the
quill fighter is helpless, exactly as the turtle. After
killing him, the natives throw him on the fire, with-
out plucking him. The quills fall out after a couple
of minutes, and a few turns suffice to roast him
beautifully. The flesh tastes exactly as I would
imagine the essence of duck, veal and ham to taste,
and I can’t think of any higher praise just now.
“Ess”: “Bex” (B. 18/9/’l3) inquires iJ
there is “any particular reason why there should be
such extreme color-difference in the eyes of Austra-
lian crows. The bulk of them (in his district) have
white eyes, while the optics of the remainder are
nearly black. This is not peculiar to any one part
of Australia. Well, Australia has two species of
crow and a raven, the latter being the most common.
Leach succinctly describes them in his Australian
Bird Book: “Hazel-eyed crow ( Corvus coronoides)
Australia, nomadic, common, open country and
timber; 20 inches in length. Black, glossed with
purple; white down, hazel eyes; female smaller.
Feeds on insects, carrion, fruit; not lambs. Small-
billed crow (C. bennetti ), West-N.S.W., V., S.A.,
Stationary, very rare, frequent plains; 18.5 inches.’
Like the other, but smaller; bill small, eye white;
female similar. Insects, carrion. Australian raven
(C. marianae) , A., Tas. Nomadic, very common;
plains, timber; 20 inches. Black, glossed with
purple; throat feathers lanceolate, tinged green;
down dusky grey; eyes white; female smaller.
Omnivorous, destructive.” The latter is the scamp
responsible for most of the depredations for which
the family is vilified. Against him the crows, which
are for the most part useful birds, have a strong
case for defamation of character, for nearly every-
where he is called a crow. Excepting for the evil
streak in the raven, both have practically the same
habits; their nests are similar (open bulky struc-
tures well built of sticks and twigs, and lined with
soft bark or fibre) and each lays four or five greenish
cftgs, spotted and blotched with brown. Neverthe-
less, the raven is entitled to precedence by his
slightly greater size, and from the fact that he is
considered by anatomists to be “the most highly
developed of birds.” He is more cunning than the
crow and he can disgorge his food at will, which
makes him a most difficult bird to poison. What
distinguishes him (apart from the slight difference
between the relative lengths of the first primary and
the innnermost secondaries) is fhat .he is biacker
than the crow. His feathers are black right down to his
skin, while the base of the crow's feathers, o:i the
neck and body are snow-white. Thus, when a per-
son sees one of the sable rogues on the top of a high
gum, all he has to do is to part the feathers, and he
will be able to tell at a glance if the bird is a crow
or a raven. IT. J. MeCooev was a great champion of
the crow, which he considered the most valuable
scavenger in Australia, and without equal as a
noxious insect destroyer. Pastoralists, instead of
paying large sums annually for the destruction of
crows, should use their endeavors to protect f lv'm
because if they were exterminated the pastoralists
would he confronted with overwhelming plagues of
flies (the blowfly is already an increasing scourge
among sheep) against which he would be positively
defenceless.

“McNorth”: In Thursday Island the other
afternoon I saw a discolored sister who took my
fancy. She was sauntering along with studied care-
lessness, holding over her head a parasol like unto
Joseph's coat of many hues. It was a gorgeous thing.
Her blouse was of pink -silk, and cut low, exposing
a broad ebony bosom. Her, skirt was of the latest
mode, and none too long, in the approved fashion.
“A pleasing illustration of what contact with the
European can do for the pore black,” I thought to
myself as my eye ran down to her feet. The shock
came there. She wore neither boots nor stockings!
Tier great black feet spread out beneath her like two
ill-kept frying-pans.

Leo Lear”: What about putting the
photograph of platypus on the national stamp? Old
duck-bill has given evolution about the biggest
assistance the cause ever got. With the porcupine,
he is the sole survivor of a large animal class that
formerly inhabited the earth. He is the lowest of
existing mammals. He contains characteristics of
both reptiles and mammals. • Finally, he is of Aus-
tralia only. There are a few points I’d like cleared
up about this burrowing water animal. Why don’t
mobs of them get herded together at the source of
rivers ? I’ve watched platypus in many streams
and seldom seen them going with the current. When
they dive it’s 10 to one they come to the surface
again up stream. Is there any truth in the bush
belief that red robins and platypus inhabit the same
stretch of country? How far do platypus extend,
anyhow? On the mainland I’ve seen them south of
the tropics in Queensland, in N.S.W., Vic. and part
of S.A. How about the Gulf and the Territory?

Eddyson”: One of (ho qua >'n tost chnr-
aoters I have met with in 40 years was Galvin.
Galvin had been a civil servant, or something of the
kind, but had taken to liquor and literature rather
late in life. He could do little patches of verse
rather neatly, and might have been a piquant essay-
ist in a nation with a taste for literarv prettiness.
As it was, Australian journalism hadn’t much use
for Tim Galvin, and Tim never seemed to possess
more than sixpence, and was the gloomiest member
of a bunch of Melbourne Bohemians who anxiously
pursued art, literature and music in quest of tray-
bits. Galvin’s most marked peculiarity was a hor-
ror of corners. With regard to all other matters he
was perfectly normal, if a trifle lacking in gaiety,
but his attitude towards corners amounted to a
monomania. Street corners were his special detesta-
tion, but he had also a profound suspicion of corners
of buildings, or any constructed angle he couldn’t
see round. If you were walking the asphalt with
lim you observed a marked nervousness in him as
he approached a corner; his pace slackened, he
ere-t nearer the walls, he betrayed a disposition to
sneak forward and peer cautiously when within a few
paces, and when the dreaded angle was passed it
was with a sigh of relief and a burst of buoyancy.
Wien alone Galvin actually did steal feverishly up
to the corners, and peep round before venturing to
pass, much as a man might do who had reasonable
grounds for the belief that a famished tiger was free
in the city, and was likely to be lurking at the
turn for a stray lunch. Galvin never grew
accustomed to his trouble. After three years the
front left-hand corner of the cottage in which the
Bohemian brothers resided remained as dubious u
turning-point to Tim as any in the city. Victor
Daley s explanation that Galvin was always expecting
to come face to face with a dangerous creditor was
not held* to be satisfactory, for the reason that
poor Tim never had an opportunity of owing anybody
anything; and Galvin himself gave no reasons.
Long after the bunch of Bohemians was scattered far
and wide, Galvin was killed in a street in South
Melbourne by a bicyclist who came suddenly round
at high speed, and hurled the hapless ex-scribe against
a tree-guard. The long arm of coincidence stretches
much further. These recollections were stirred by
a recent meeting with Galvin’s widow. “And poor
Tim was always suspicious of corners,” she said.
His father was killed by a bolting horse at a street
corner seven weeks before he was born.”

“Chin”: At an hotel in Clare (S.A.) a
young fellow who was silly enough to get drunk,
but not too silly to know that bed was the best
place for him, entered by mistake a balcony bedroom
occupied by two girls. On seeing the invader one of
the girls shrieked: “Oh, there’s a man in the room!”
Whereupon the drunk: “Wher ish he? Show ’im er
me! I’ll soon chuck ’im out for yer.” Who says
chivalry is dead?
