( nmp : I’m satisfied as to the tracking
capabilities of Black Brother (the missionary’s bro-
ther, not mine). Years before the Vic. mallee was
thought of as a wheat-producing country, I was sent
up to a run in the Swan Hill district to take charge
of a draft of sheep for the Melbourne market. We
pitched camp near the homestead. It was well on in
the afternoon when we finished drafting, and we
deferred our start for the South until next morning.
In the evening 1 went up to the house to interview
the squatter, a big, grey-bearded, rotund man. Busi-
ness finished, we adjourned to the verandah, and he
introduced me to Mrs. Squatter, a prepossessing lady,
young enough to be his third daughter. Bottles and
glasses were produced, and my mouth was watering in
anticipation of sampling a promising-looking whisky
which old Mac ’was handing me, when Mrs. Mac. in-
tervened by bringing under my notice a bottle with
a home-made label containing what she proudly
described as a non-intoxicating mulberry wine of
her own manufacture. “You forget, Donald,’’ she
said to her husband, “that everyone is not as fond
of whisky as you are, and Mr. Camp might prefer
some of my wine.” 1, of course, declared for the
mulberry concoction. What else could a fellow do?
Never, since my boyhood days, when my mother, in
an excess of maternal solicitude, would tightly grip
my nose with one hand the while she tipped a
tablespoonful of castor-oil down my throat with
the other, was I made the sarcophagus of such a
nauseating dose. However, I was young and polite
(and therefore a liar), so I praised the beastly stuff
for all I was worth. Mrs. Mac beamed, and, as I
rose to go, she pressed me to take the bottle of
wine with me, at the same time explaining that
it was the last of the vat, or brew, or whatever it
was. On my way back to eamp I struck a match,
wrote across the label “POISON,” and planted the
bottle in a hollow stump off the track. Next morn-
ing Mr. and Mrs. Mac. drove down to see us off the
run Mac, overhearing me ask one of my men for
he loan of a knife, as I had lost mine on the way
ack to the camp, butted in: “If you lost your knife
between here and the house, Snowy (indicating the
black boy he had with him to open gates) will find
it. Forthwith he ordered the “nig” to run my
tracks. Sending my men forward with the sheep, I
remained with the squatter and his wife to await
the return of Snowy. I put in some good work tell-
ing Mrs. Mac how I had appreciated her wine, and
made a frank confession to the effect that the flavor
gripped my fancy so hard that I had finished the
lot before turning-in on the previous night. I was
saying what a great thing it would be if her bever-
age could be largely introduced so as to push the
whisky bottles clean off the pub-shelves, when Snowy
suddenly re-introduced himself with: “Baal find ’em
Knife, Mr. Taggat—only this one-phella bottle
planted longa stump.” The cold eye of pretty Mrs.
Mac and the guffaw of the old man are with me
until this day.

GETTIN’ GOLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When we began to sink our shaft,
By neighbors we were told
’Twas duffer ground an’ we were daft;
But in a week ’twas we who larfed,
For we were gettin’ gold.
They say we get an ounce per tub
[They find out what we’ve sold],
An" gather round us at the pub.
Our claim’s the blanky township’s hub,
Now we are gettin’ gold.
The people nod across the street,
The parson’s bow is cold.
[We’ll rent a blanky prayin’ seat,
For we want smiles from all we meet,
When we are gettin’ gold.]
* * » *
We thought that we would make a pile,
But Fortune’s wheel has rolled,
An’ tradesmen now no longer smile.
We’ll have tc pay ’em by the mile,
For we ain’t gettin’ gold.

The Beale Permanent Steel Tuning System renders what was hitherto the weakest part of a piano the strongest,
n e\ery Beale Piano the wrest-plank is made of solid iron. The wrest-plank is that part of the piano into which are
men the tuning pins to which are attached the wires (or “strings”). In every imported piano the wrest-plank is
made of wood, which has to bear the strain (about 20 tons) of all the wires when the piano is in tune. So that in every
piano that has a wooden wrest-plank there is a probability of the wrest-plank splitting and the piano becoming untunable
in consequence. And in every piano but the Beale the wrest-plank is made of wood.
In all Beale Pianos the wrest-plank is solid iron, a part of the iron frame itself.
Write for our special booklets explaining this matter in detail.
We have recently put on the market the Beale Player-Piano. The Beale Player-Piano possesses the finest
mechanism it is possible to produce, fitted in a Beale high-grade piano of handsome appearance. The artistic possi-
bilities of the interpretation of a roll on the Beale Player-Piano are limited only by the artistic temperament of the
performer. The mechanism is so delicately regulated and every control so perfect that it is easier to play than any other
piano-player.

“Gordon G.” : I guess I can put in a fair
claim to having lighted on the Absolute Last Word
in juror’s sagacity. Twelve of us had been
engaged in hearing a charge of larceny from a dwell-
ing. The Sagacious One and I had held out for
nearly two hours against our 10 opponents, who
were in favor of an acquittal. I had harangued our
adversaries at great length, but, in spite of my
most persistent entreaties, was unable to induce my
comrade to utter anything more than a monosyl-
lable. The result was disagreement. I selected
my friend as the only fit person in the crowd to
accompany me from the court to the nearest tea-
rooms. During the stroll, talk naturally reverted
to the “case,” and the 10 mugs with whom we had
been closeted. “I think I put it plainly enough to
them, eh?” I ventured. “Why, dammit, the evi-
dence was clear enough, and the summing-up was
all against the poor devil.” “Oh, yes, you didn’t do
bad,” he returned ; “but I never take much notice o’
the bloomin’ lawyers and judges and witnesses an’ all
the rest o’ them, y’know.” I became interested.
“Yer see, Mister Wotsyername, I’ve ’ad a good bit
o’ hexperience with these ’ere criminal blokes, an’
what I does is this. Soon’s I get inter me place, I
jes fixes me heye on the prisoner. I looks ’im up
and down fur a few minutes —’e knows darned well
I’m sizin’ ’im up—and then I jes sits back and
waits a bit. After a while I ’as another good quiz
at ’im, and then I ses to meself, ‘Ther cow ’e mus’
be guilty, or wot ’ud ’e be ’ere for?’ ” I staggered
and suggested a pub instead of the tea-rooms.

“S. S. Severn”: Garea, my house boy, was
a native of Courabada (Papua). He “made” the
table and served my food with a lordly dignity that
a bishop’s butler need not have despised. One day
two distinguished visitors from a gunboat dropped
in to lunch. The curry looked lovely, but Garea had
forgotten the rice. I tried to catch his eye, but it
was fascinated by the guests’ gold lace. “Garea,” I
murmured, “rice milaia” (bring rice). “Oh, Sina-
bada,” he said apologetically, “I too many bl —dy
fool!” He meant, merely, that it was forgetful of
him.

“R.V.”: It’s cow, cow, cow, till you’d
think there wasn’t another brain-numbing, soul-de-
stroying curse in the country. But what about
sheep? Consider the life of the average station-hand.
He gets up at daylight, or before, eats sheep and
talks sheep, saddles his horse, and musters, droves
or drafts sheep. At 12 o’clock, or thereabouts, he
boils his quart, masticates sheep and blathers sheep.
Then all through a blazing afternoon
he tails sheep. At teatime he chews
and blathers sheep. Then over a pes-
tiferous slush lamp or spluttering
candle he argues, wrangles and some-
times fights over sheep, and all
through a steaming night he dreams
sheep. Day after day, year in and
year out. the same dreary, desolate,
hellish routine with sheep to begin
with, and sheep to go on with,
and sheep to end with. Were it
not for the yearly trip to the
nearest country town, where a week of
lurid living relieves the pent-up, pas-
sionate longings of a healthy human
being, our receiving houses would be
full of Jims and Bills gibbering sheep.
Can one wonder at so many of them
holding pessimistic views? Present
scribe thought his cup of bitterness
was full what time he stood on the
doorstep of his country printing office
and heaped curses on and hurled
challenges at the bailiff as he made
an inventory of his shaky hand-press,
dilapidated formes and obsolete types. But the cup
was far from brimming over; how far he only
found out when he had to earn a precarious living
amongst sheep.
* * * *
“Bucolic”: One of the notable things at
the Melbourne Agricultural Show just held was the
display of Holstein cows. This breed is coming
rapidly to the front in America and Maoriland, be-
cause of its large milk crop. The world’s record for
butter yield is held by Pontiac Lass, a Holstein, in
America. She produced 441 b. in seven days—a per-
formance which whipped her price up to £2OOO
when she was offered for sale. We want some
Pontiac lasses badly. The average for all Australia
is under 300 gallons of milk per cow per annum,
which is less than half Denmark’s average. But,
then, Denmark goes in for systematic testing, and
cows not up to the mark are butchered. What are
called the State Supervision areas in Victoria com-
prise about a sixth of its territory. Within these
areas the average is 475 gallons, but outside it is
only 302. Take another proof of the value of test-
ing. In 1884 the average Danish production was
1121 b. of butter per cow. In 1908 it was up to
just double, 2241 b. The whole explanation is that
Co-operative Testing Associations were started mean-
time —in 1895. The same thing must happen in
Australia, which is now wasting fodder and labor
on thousands of animals that, for the good of the
species, ought to be hurried by the nearest route to
the beef-shop.
“R. Brothers”: A man leans against a
verandah post by the opening of one of the arcades,
quietly enjoying a smoke and sc spit across the
footpath. Towards him shuffles a man in broken
boots and clothes which shout for the destructor. A
battered boxer hat is dragged down at the back of
his head to that extent that the tops of his ears lap
at right angles. His manner is chastened as of one
who has seen light. The smoker spits right in front
of him, and he stops and looks at the moisture with
the look of one who is horrified, and then he turns
to the man it came from with a withering ex-
pression.
“Some blokes oughter go en get shot,” he says.
“What’s er matter ?” asks the smoker.
“Yer oughter go en get boiled,” says the horrified
one. “If yer wants ter spit, why don’t yer do it in
the gutter? S’pose yer don’t know yer can get
fined five bob or the risin’ for spittin’ on the foot-
paths? If yer don’t know yer oughter know, en it’s
little enough. It ought ter be six munce hard.
Wot’s the good of the risin’ when yer goes there for
fun, like enough. It ought ter be six munce.”
The smoker, to show his indifference, spits at the
reformer’s right boot, but the reformer is too quick.
“It ought ter be a year, that’s wot it ought ter
be,” says the vagrant. “If yer pinches anything, it’s
a year, en spittin’ is worse than pinchin’. For
why”
The smoker has another attempt, but the speaker
dodges without an interruption.
“becos spittin’ is murder.”
He pauses to mark the effect of his statement.
“S’pose yer don’t know it’s murder, en I know
why yers don’t know it’s murder. It’s becos of yer
iggerance, that’s why. It’s blokes like yous wot is
killing people; there is people in ’orsepittals dyin’
becos of you, en that’s why it’s murder. Dyin’, I
says, becos of yous blokes spittin’. Don’t yer know
that it causes toober-something or other that is only
another name for consumption, en that’s why I alius
says the bloke wot spits oughter get hung. Wot’s the
good of the risin’ to stop it? Blokes don’t mind
the risin’. They likes it, becos it fills in the mornin’,
en they ’ears the cases bein’ tried. They oughter get
hung be the neck till they’re dead, en a jolly good
riddance.”
Several times during this long speech he hastily
shifts one or the other foot. He pauses to think then,
as if to remember: —
“It’s a disgustin’ ’abit, ennyhow,” he says.
After another pause, he goes on:—
“It’s filthy. S’pose yer don’t think of the skirts
wot ’as ter walk along the pavement after you’ve
done with it? If a bloke with a skirt only took the
law in his own hands, en dealt with yer, yer’d stop
it quick enough, I bet. If the blokes with skirts
pulled out their revolvers (if they ’ad one) en shot
yer dead, nuthin’ would be done to ’em, becos every-
body would say it serves yer right, spittin’ all over
the place where people ’as ter walk. Yer oughter get
boiled, yer ought—boiled in tar. I on’y wish the
Gov’ment would let me deal with yer. I’d put a
stop ter spittin’ quick en lively. I’d bury yer alive,
en play Yankee Doodle on yer grave.”
“That’s enough, bloke,” says the smoker. “Come,
get a move on.”
“I’d give yer wot for”
“Shut up, en get,” says the smoker, angrily. “I’ve
had enough of it. Get, or” And he makes as
if he means to kick.
“Oh, very well,” says the vagrant. “But all the
luck I wish yer is that a cop comes along en nabs
yer, that’s all.”
And he shuffles off.
Another man with his hands deep in his pockets
lounges up at this moment: —
“Do yer know him?” he asks, jerking his head
sideways in the direction of the reformer.
“No. Why?”
“Oh, nuthin’. Only he just got the rising for spit-
ting in Pitt-street. The magistrate gave him a
fair talkin’ to, my oath!”

THE PLYING HEARSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A man was fined £1 at Highgate, London, to-day
for driving a motor-hearse to the cemetery at the
rate of 30 miles an hour. — Cable.
No undertaker’s moke shall trail,
Sad-eyed, in front of me,
To draw me, slowly like a snail,
Unto the cemet’ree ;
I want no red-nosed, pimpled “whip,”
With whiskied breath and sour—
Ah, no ! I’d rather do the trip
At thirty miles an hour.
No plumes shall deck my four-wheeled biw,
When I am cased in lead.
No immortelles all frowsed and drear,
May shiver overhead ;
For if my pace in Life’s been swift,
Be speed my parting dower,
And let me make my final shift
At thirty miles an hour.
Yes, give to me the speedy mote,
Picked out in red, mayhap,
A neat chauffeur with braided coat
And silver on his cap ;
Let coppers wave their arms and curse,
And traffic blokes “go sour” —
For me the flying motor-hearse
At thirty miles an hour.
N.s.w.
HORACE HALLORAN.
The Paces in Our Northern Window.
From an Australian long resident in China ■
In the short period of six years history has re’
markably and unpleasantly repeated itself with
gard to Chinese affairs. In 1907 the cognoscenti
were apprehensive of an outbreak of war between
Japan and the U.S.A., and there were signs that th»
man in the street thought he could read Then
when Japans swelled head had almost reached burst
ing point, the Government realised that such a war
would be impossible, for financial reasons Verv
opportunely the much-debated Tatsu Maru II i nP j
dent happened at Macao, and the Ministry diverted
the attention of the swelled-head people towards
poor old scapegoat China, who got it severely and
unjustly in the neck. Now, again, after rumors of
war between Uncle Sam and Uncle Jap over the
Californian Land Bill and the Mexican concessions
the unscrupulous little brown man has made another
demand for a pound of China’s flesh—in fact, it looks
like a request for liewt. of best meat, with the head
attached.
The utter disproportion of Japan’s demands is seen
when they are placed side by side with the cause of
them. During the recent insurrection at Nankin, Japan-
ese soldiers assisted the revolutionaries. It is now de-
clared by Japan that its soldiers assisted without
leave. After the town was taken the Government
troops found two or three Japs looting, and after
the recognised international custom, shot them. Now
as an indemnity, Japan demands the extension of the
lease of Port Arthur by 50 years, and the right to
establish military bases at Amoy and Foochow. These
two cities are just about opposite the north and
south extremities of strongly-fortified Formosa, and
there is little doubt that the whole province will
ultimately be annexed by Japan, by what have been
described as “Manchurian methods/’ These methods
have been described by Putnam Whale and Mac-
kenzie, and may be regarded as the Japanese ideal
of a peaceful invasion. They are commended to the
notice of the dilatory Australian Defence Depart-
ment as a sort of hurry call to arm and organise
all males of a fighting age.
Jap troops marched through Korea, and their
route was marked by rotting crops and general
ravage. The simple Korean farmer was shot, if
found working in the field, probably on the pretext
that his primitive agricultural implement was really
a military weapon. If he was not working he was
shot just the same. That is how room was made for
the 4000 immigrants that are arriving there monthly.
More hideous still are the suggestions and statements
of Whale, who baldly declares that the Japanese
Government poured hundreds of diseased harridans
into the country. It would be very pleasant to have
a Japanese colony in the Northern Territory.
In its travail China apparently sees no hope for
the present, and expects that it will be able to do
little off its own equivalent for a bat in the imme-
diate future. As a set-off to Japan's demands, it
has, therefore, granted to Russia Mongolian conces-
sions which that country has long desired in order
to strengthen its far-eastern frontier. It is beyond
reasonable doubt that these concessions have been
made by China in the knowledge that Russia must
ASKING TOO MUCH.
OLD LADY (buying a parrot): “ S'pose you
'aven’t got one wiv any Caruso records ,
’ave you ? ”
one day wipe out the stigma of its defeat done,
too, in the hope that that day will soon be due.
Meanwhile, one who has seen the immense army
stores at Vladivostock, and seen, too. the fine type
of colonist that is pouring into the hinterland, can
only wonder how long it will be before the Japanese
frog will be loaded up with something more soliu
than his own conceit.

first dose got relief, and kept on getting better.
Sometimes in the winter I get a return of my old
complaint, but in a very mild way, for Chamber
lain’s Cough Remedy soon puts me right.”*

AUCKLAND, N.Z. : 3 Lome Street.
WELLINGTON, N.Z. : 6 Mercer Street.
On October 1 the N.S.W. Leg. Assembly,
having been sitting continuously for 20
hours, passed without discussion, as
quickly as the Minister for Works could
gabble through a few staccato sentences,
seven or eight Public Works proposals in-
volving an expenditure of £1,450,000. This
crop of Bills was passed without amend-
ment, through all their stages, at the one
sitting, and all of them were galloped off
to the Leg. Council. With the revenue and
loan accounts heavily overdrawn it is doubt-
ful whether any of the proposals will get
beyond the paper stage for years, but with
election day drawing near that is a mere
matter of detail. The advertisement’s the
thing.
George Elmslie, Prendergast’s successor
as leader of the Vic. Labor party, should stir
things up in the country. Melbourne is
fairly solid for Labor already ; yet “Pren ”
neglected to cultivate the country, and went
on cultivating what was well cultivated
already. Elmslie ought to do the other thing.
And if he can travel with his boots off, so
much the better, as then the farmers will
be able to see that he hasn’t cloven hoofs.
Many Vic. farmers believe that all the
Labor crowd have horns and a tail as well;
so the mere appearance of Elmslie will do
good. But he will never do much while
he stays in Melbourne.
Angus, M.L.A., talking to the bucolics of
Kerang (Vic.), deplored the proposed elec-
trification of railways in Melbourne as being
“detrimental to country development.” He
might have forcibly remarked that Govern-
ment expenditure these times should be
mainly directed to carrying civilisation into
the backblocks, but to protest against elec-
trification of town railways because country
folk have no use for them seems much the
same thing as objecting to asphalted foot-
paths, electric lighting facilities, motor
’buses and the Quinlan Opera Co. All these
features of crowded city life are detrimental
to country development, from a one-eyed
Angus point of view, but not so detrimental
as the selfish narrowness of the average shire
councillor to whom Angus was addressing
his bleat.
“Don Q.” : I see that that reliable blather-
skite, Ben Til let t, addressing 10,000 trades
unionists at Southampton the other day,
advised the strikers to "arm themselves with
the most murderous guns, and be drilled by
ex-soldiers and sailors in order to protect
themselves.” It was quite recently that the
same ass was braying against a citizen army
of these same people, to protect the same
identical fraternity.
Australia pulled off a new advt. during
the London ’busmen’s strike. The Tillings
co. there objected to the men wearing a
union badge, and when business was held
up for some days the similarity of the
quarrel to that of the Brisbane Tramway
strike caused the judgment of Justice Hig-
gins to be looked up and widely discussed.
The outcome was that Tillings and co. with-
drew their objection ; and now, according to
a Sydney man in London, you can’t sit
alongside a London ’busman without hear-
ing what a great place Australia is.
The four lovely United States citizens con-
demned to death for murdering Rosenthal,
the “squealer,” at the instance of Charles
Becker, lieutenant of Noo York police, were
named Cerofici, Horowitz, Rosenberg and
Seidenscher. That is to say, a corrupt
policeman of German parentage, desiring to
remove a Jew gambler who had accused him
of blackmailery and grafting, hired one Dago
and three German Jews to do the killing.
Zelig, the captain of the murderous gang,
had himself been murdered by one of his
mates, so the Law couldn’t touch him. The
conspirators who turned informers, in this
interesting case, are named Rose, Yallon,
Schrapps, Reich, Paul, Shapiro and Fax.
This collection of names might be cited as
evidence that the Anglo-Saxon race is pass-
ing out of the United States. On the other
hand, it suggests that the criminality of the
States is mostly foreign to the stock that
Franklin, Lincoln, Garfield, Hawthorne,
Longfellow, Edison and Livingston Hopkins
(of The Bulletin) sprang from. Which sug-
gestion is worth consideration whenever a
scheme for dumping white alien immigrants
in Australia is mooted by cheap-labor parties.
Preserve us from Seidenscher, Zelig,
Schrapps and Shapira—especially the first,
because his name is hardest to spell.
“Orient”: Palpably it is the Japanese
Government that is publishing Ambassador
Hayashi’s despatches, by instalments, in the
principal Tokio newspaper, the Shimbun ;
and the only object of it can be to humiliate
Britain. Hitherto the idea has been that,
with magnificent condescension, Foreign
Minister Lansdowne negotiated the alliance,
on a humble belly-crawl by Japan. Now it
mysteriously emerges that, instead of going
down on its waistcoat, Japan had a rod in
pickle. Britain wanted Germany to be in
the Alliance. Japan wouldn’t have that at
any price, and Britain apparently would not
have gone in on a lone hand but for Japan’s
alternative —an alliance with Russia. Ito
was even sent to St. Petersburg on the pre-
tence of negotiating this alliance, and it was
because he went too far that Russia entered
into the subsequent war with gusto. The
whole business is a puzzle; but out of the
dark I seem to see that our years of grace
may be up much sooner than we thought.
The Chief Secretary of Victoria announces
himself opposed to the abolition of the kilt
“because it means removing the one bit of
bright coloring from our defence force.” Save
us from our friends; —our enemies would
oppose the change for exactly the same
reason. A bit of bright clothing is a fine
mark for a long-range rifle.
“Billy T.” : Dr. Jones, Victoria’s Medical
Superintendent of the Insane, back from the
Medical Congress, emphasises the opinion of
the profession that this old world is going
more markedly off its chump—such chump
as it possesses—every year. He points out
that “something must be done.” Why?
When everybody is mad, nobody will be ab-
normal, and there will be no need for Medi-
cal Superintendents of the Insane, for
every lunatic will be sane and nobody
will be mad save the individual who is in
his right mind. There is a chance, and not
a bad one, that the general tone and quality
of our affairs may be improved under the new
conditions. But by what right do we assume
that the world is sane? Unfortunately,
we have no standards of comparison. One
might make out a very excellent case in
support of the theory that our earth is
really the madhouse of the universe,
From “Yanco”:—
The most diverse yarns are in circulation here
concerning the amount of money which Samuel
McCaughey—the Irishman who doesn’t consider Ire-
land good enough to live in—has given to boost up
the cause which aims at preventing Ireland from en-
joying the same form of government as the country
which McCaughey inhabits for preference. Some say
that the wildly illogical oldster’s cheque was for
£30,000. Others point to the fact that he is at
large, by way of refutation of the charge; and the
argument is mostly held to be valid. It is at this
stage that his dark and guilty Past rises up and
bears testimony against him. It is remembered
that he gave £lO,OOO to the demented old Dread-
nought Fund. A sheep, or other Emperor who was
capable of such folly as that would, it is felt, be
liable to do anything in the way of lavishing good
money on bad, not to say utterly senseless, schemes.
40 years of newspaper work
William Sholto Douglas, editor of Auck-
land (M.L.) Herald, is dead. He started
on the long job with the pen in 1873 in the
Gold Country and five years later climbed
into the editorial chair on the Leeds Daily
News. It was from there the Auckland
Herald imported him, as a leader writer,
in 1882, and 21 years later he was
appointed to the controlling position.
Sydney and Melbourne knew him for
years (although they were not aware of
it) as Maoriland correspondent of the
Morning Herald and Argus.
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Apropos Charles Windeyer (B. 18/9/’l3). He was
the first official Mayor of Sydney, which means
that, when Governor Gipps decided that the people of
Sydney should make and keep in. repair their own
streets, he proclaimed a “Corporation,” which was
the precursor of that elected by the citizens. The
Governor nominated the aldermen, all of them public
officials. For Gipps Ward the nominee was Hutchin-
®on Browne, Immigration Agent; for Bourke
ward, William Augustus Miles, Commissioner of
Police; for Brisbane Ward, Charles Windeyer, Police
Magistrate; for Cook Ward, Francis Lewis Shaw
Mcrewether, Clerk of the Executive Council; for
* " Joseph Long Innes, Police Magistrate;
ioi Macquarie Ward, John Ryan Brenan, Attorney and
Coroner. Windeyer was appointed to act as Mayor,
this nominated corporation got the machinery well
oiled and running for the first election, which took
place on November 1, 1842. On November 9, John
Hoskmg, son-in-law of Sam Terry, was elected Mayor ;
but a commercial storm passed over Sydney imme-
diately afterwards, and in it Hosking was totally
dismasted, and sprang a number of financial leaks
All efforts to pump him out having failed, he resigned
his office, and J. R. Wilshire was elected for the
remainder of the term. Quite recently an official of
the Corporation, seeking for something else, dis-
covered the first ratebook, with the names of the
first ratepayers, their assessments, their holdings and
their homes. The volume when found was damp and
smellful. Deputy-Town-Clerk Layton promptly detailed
several clerks to have the entries accurately copied,
so that it will “appear in its habit as it lived,” and
be an interesting record of the first days of the city
Though incorporated under the same Act and in the
same year, Melbourne did not become a “city” until
1847, when it was created one by Letters Patent
Therefore the ratepayers of Sydney were termed
“citizens” from 1842 onwards, while those of Mel-
bourne remained “burgesses” until 1847.

“Norseman” : For years I was an advocate
for the abolition of pubs in Australia, and
min a at „ °PP° u ents ammunition
bianded Mildura, which was prepared bv
Christian leagues and associations whom I
believed honest. Maybe they were honest
but, it so, they must have been densely
ignorant. Some time ago I was destined to
toil near Mildura, and, since then, anvbodv
can have my share of no-license. It’s a
fraud for everybody but the local clubs. On
a f re J . < i en t Saturday, in one of these, I saw one
of the wildest scenes raging I have ever
witnessed in the booze business. Three bar-
men were pumping beer at the rate of 67
long sleevers a minute. Anyhow’, that was
my calculation in the early part of the even-
ing. Later in the night, I reckon the speed
had increased to 135 a minute, and was
maintained at that until 11.30 p.m. Even
then it wasn’t fast enough for the mob, and
the pathetic appeals to the barmen to “get
a move on” would have melted a whole Band
of Hope conference. Next dav I wandered
along the \ ictorian bank of the Murray, and
it the bottle-oh merchants have any desire
to gather at the river, let me recommend that
locality to the congregation.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“Hock’s” observation :
The Bulletin’s remark that the present system
of party government makes possible the return of
inuckrakers like Fitzpatrick and Levy is true; still no
"tie is much worse off when such inflictions act up to
Fieir natural inclinations. It is when men like
Irvine and Glynn put the welfare of party before
country, as they did in connection with the lately
proposed Constitutional amendments, that the Com-
monwealth pays dearly for the party madness.
Big H. V. McKay, of the Sunshine Works,
near Melbourne, who mixes a lot of public
spirit with his money-making, has just
opened a technical school close by his works.
Some time ago Chief Education Director
Tate spoke of American manufacturers who
had helped technical education, and regretted
i hat Australians were so backward in the
matter. This spurred on McKay. He co-
operated with the State in establishing the
Sunshine school, by giving £2OOO and five
acres of land; and betweep them they have
provided accommodation for 200 students,
half in workshops, half in theoretical class-
rooms. Principally, of course, engineering
and iron-work will be taught, with an eye
1 o McKay’s own requirements. But that’s the
right thing. Only a Freetrader would want
silk-spinning to be taught alongside a bud-
ding ironworks.
A FINANCIAL BOOMERANG.
The accumulated surplus on June 30 was £2,653,223.
Of this amount, £2,457,773 has been invested in
loans to States and otherwise. The Treasurer (John
Forrest) regards this as unfortunate. It ought to
have been foreseen that the amount would soon be
required. —Report of Commonwealth Budget speech.
RELIEVING MANAGER: “What's the matter,
John ? ”
RELIEVING TELLER: “Why, how we battled
to secure the suspension of Fisher and his
staff on the grounds of improvidence and
extravagance! And now Pm hanged if
they didn't carefully invest everything and
hardly left a penny for us to play with ! ”
“J.B.S.’s” snort of indignation:—
“So that the effect of the entry of our Fleet to
Sydney Harbor may live long in the hearts” of those
who saw it, it was commanded that “steamers should
preserve an impressive silence.” The people, in the
simplicity of their hearts, obeyed. So the traitors
who aimed a blow at Australian Nationalism won!
We wanted to shout; we wanted to express our
feelings; we wanted to welcome the Naval Sons of
Australia. But they got the glassy eye instead. Who
gave that order? We ought to know; they are people
worth firing out.
Occasionally the truth leaks out of Syd-
ney Daily Telegraph. In its issue of
October 10:
Turning to Home Rule, he (Winston Churchill)
said the Tories had grown fretful under their long-
enforced continuance in the minority. They used to
rely on the Lards’ veto, but were now inclined to
induce the King to take sides.
The House of Lard ! Good —and we’ll re-
member it was the Sydney Telegraph that
taught us that word.
The imported expert, ignorant of local
conditions, has fallen in again and dragged
the long-suffering country with him. In this
case it is Merz, who owns to an error in
fixing the Melbourne railway electrification
power-house at Yarraville. The quantity
of water needed at the station for cooling
will be 6i million gallons an hour, from 18
to 20 hours a day—more than is consumed
by Melbourne on the hottest summer day.
To get this supply Merz’s Yarraville station
was too high; so though £12,000 has been
spent on a branch railway, the site has
been abandoned and another chosen on
basaltic rock, with hard clay underlie,
nearer Williamstown. There will have to
be a new connection with the Williamstown
railroad, and it will mean lengthening the
main transmission cable by nearly two
miles.
“Public Servant’s” wail:—
The outing of the Superannuation Bill by the
N. S. Wales Leg. Council was certain. The reason
alleged by the party champions for the opposition was
that the Bill was too important to spring on an
expiring Parliament, and that the Council had not
had sufficient time to discuss it. Maybe; but it’s
a different attitude from that taken up in 1910, when
Wade, as Premier, dumped the Railway Superannua-
tion Bill before the Council just previous to the
elections. That Bill had objections which couldn’t
be urged against the P.S. Superannuation Bill, and
even the party’s press was hostile. But Wade was
up against trouble, and wanted to pass a few vote-
catching measures, just as the other party does now—
or did last week. So the Upper House, deaf to all
protests, boosted the Wade Bill through. But,
then, a “Liberal” Government’s job and pay were
in danger. Which made a difference. It generally
does in the House of Fat.
The daily papers get pretty close to the
truth sometimes; but they mostly shy off
before they get right there. In the House
of Reps, the other evening the Cook Govern-
ment proposed to put another £400,000 on
poor sister Tasmania’s plate, Fisher having
dropped £500,000 there already, whereupon
the Opposition stonewalled, and the daily
paper said: —
It is hardly a matter of much concern to the argu-
ment whether there is a political motive in the second
grant, any more than whether there was in the first.
Politics were no doubt at the bottom of both. They
were at the bottom of the baby bonus; they are
at the bottom of all things that Parliament does,
on the well-known maxim that though Parliament
may do many things that are right, it does not do
them because they are right.
The paper knows quite well, of course, that
party politics is at the bottom of the
wretched business. But, being a good party
paper itself, it merely remarked that “poli-
tics” was responsible, and went on barrack-
ing for the old curse.
The occasional announce-
ments made by certain
N.S.W. State Ministers that
they are full of admiration
of their wild loan policy,
and would spend even more
borrowed money if they
could get, are interesting in
a sense. Seeing that these
Ministers climbed into office
on a promise of reduced bor-
rowing, increased economy,
and the expenditure of loan
money only on works that
would pay full interest and
sinking fund, their attitude
defines them as about the
most shameless gang of
political liars, frauds and
deceivers that ever held
office in Australia. They
don’t even take the trouble
to pretend to political hon-
esty—possibly because they
don’t know that such a thing
exists, or possibly because
they don’t know that it possesses a certain
vaiue.
There was an awful row lately because
some suburban Melbourne mayor—a Labol-
ite, of course—left the toast of “The King”
off the list at a municipal shivoo. Now,
according to public report, the knight
McCaughey has sent along a liberal dona-
tion to a party of men who openly adver-
tise that they are collecting arms and ammu-
nition for the forcible overthrow of the
King’s authority. And McCaughey is a
member of the N.S.W. Legislative Council
as well as a knight. Suppose it had been
the Home Rulers who were collecting arms
and fighting funds and a Labor M.L.C. who
had sent a donation, what would the Legis-
lative Council have to say about it?
THE VERY MAN.
One of the most interesting announcements expected from the State Premier in delivering the Buried
speech is the proposal to create a new Department of Labor and Industry.— Melbourne daily payer.
PERMIER WATT: “I suppose you’ll be wauling this, John"'
John Weary : “My oath , yes! Drop it in the hammock.”
Judging by the contradictory reports of
the Moxham-McGirr mill in the library of
the N.S.W. Assembly last week, it is time
the House Committee got busy. Some of
the brands in the Refreshment Room would
appear to require changing, and the immedi-
ate installation of a proper ring with a
padded floor is urgently necessary. A
library is no place for a fight ; some day
some hon. gentleman will knock out what
brains he has by falling against the corner
of a chair. Also, a stipendiary referee should
be appointed, as, according to some accounts,
for the want of a proper supervisor, there
was on this last occasion talk of “putting
in the boot.” This should be guarded against.
If legislators must fight, let them be so gov-
erned that they do it like decent pugs and
not according to Surry Hills rules. One of
the high warlike parties is said to have fallen
down on his way to battle. Which is also
deplorable. There should be a proper exam-
ination beforehand, to see if the hon. gentle-
men are fit to enter the ring. Later on
there should be another examination to see
if they are fit to remain in Parliament.
Conroy, M.H.R., of the Gab, is forming a
party to. agitate for an extension of the life
of the House of Reps, from three to five
years, to reduce that of the Senate from six
years to five, and. to make the whole Senate
retire simultaneously, so that an absolutely
new Parliament, lock, stock and tube, may
be elected every quinquennium. There
would be points about the idea if the Min-
istry were elective but .imagine the present
Parliament and its weird performances
going on wearily, drearily for five
solid years! In any case, if the two
Houses are to retire simultaneously, and be
simultaneously by the same people,
why have two Houses at sill ?
_ After all,’ why this outburst of indigna-
tion and horror when it is suggested that
one member of the N.S.W. Parliament has
been lining his own pockets at the expense
of the public? Hasn’t every member of the
same party who hopes to get re-elected been
attempting to do it? Hasn’t the whole
crowd been using up time (which costs the
country money) in passing railway and other
bills running into about £14,000,000, knowing
well that the works won’t be started for
months in some cases, years in others, and
not at all-in many? There may have been
corruption in one case; there certainly has
been bribery or attempted bribery in the
other. But what in the single member is a
crime, in a whole crowd is—party politics.
“Y’s” verdict after the fight:—
If Moxham you but heard explain
His resurrection from the floor,
You’d think Jack Johnson back again,
But twice as vain as heretofore.
And if you did not quit and run, ■
But heard McGirr his tale repeat,
You’d think he was the biggest gun
On board the flagship of our Fleet.
One of the most insistent deputations that
have harangued P.M.G. Wynne since he
became a Fused Minister arrived from Caul-
field recently, and urged that that
furiously Liberal suburb’s “private contract”
post office should be abandoned in favor of a
State-managed concern. Thus do the advo-
cates of Private Enterprise pour derision on
their creed, going even so far as to earnestly
declare that the beast isn’t fit to be trusted
with the job of passing out a few postcards
or a parcel or two.
Ex-Treasurer Twaddell, getting a chance
in the N.S.W. Assembly the other evening
to talk about Sydney’s inevitable under-
ground railway, moaned, as might have been
expected, that “it would make the present
tramways to the railway unremunerative.”
No doubt it will. The present tramways
also made the ’buses of old unremuUefative;
but nobody but Twaddell would regard that
as any reason why the ’buses should have
been allowed to run for ever.
Page, M.H.R., in from the saltbush and
the mulga, is brutally frank about Parlia-
mentary morals. Men from his own side,
playing the party game, told the Govern-
ment that it was merely bribing Tasmania
with its bid of another £400,000 for the
Speck’s support. “Rats !” said Mulga Jim ;
“the late Government did not give Tasmania
£500.000 without thinking it was going to
get a quid pro quo.” But it seems an urn
tidy way of doing things. Why not have a
regular auction day, and do the business in
a business-like fashion?
OBSERVATION BY A DESPAIRING
SOUL.
The late Fisher Ministry’s proposals to start a
horse-breeding establishment, meat-freezing works and
other things, to help develop the Northern Territory,
have been turned down by the Cook Administration.
THE LONELY ONE: “Ye gods! The old bottle
and hot even a biscuit to chew between
drinks. ”
In Westralia the jarrah and karri cos. of
the South-West, and the firewood combine of
the Goldfields, are so busy with their axes
that decent-sized trees will soon be rare.
Though the South-West cos. should, according
to agreement, replant all denuded areas, no
Government, not even Scaddan’s, has ever
ventured to suggest to them to keep to the
agreement. Recently in Perth a Forestry
League was formed, with the object of look-
ing after these matters, and it will do a
national service if it insists on the right
thing from the jarrah and karri people, and
hustles for the Kalgoorlie-Esperance rail-
way. The line would mean shortening the
route for Newcastle (N.S.W.) coal to Kal-
goorlie mines, and thus give the trees a
chance.
THE POOR CAPITALIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
S fact which appealed to Sir Stephen Collins was
Fiat there were apparently no poor in Australia.
“ there may be a few poor people in the big cities,”
he said, “but in every large town there are bound
to be a few hangers-on. In the country, however,
'.here is no poverty, no poor, and all that seems to
Ic wanted is more men.” —Daily organ.
No poor the Commonwealth within ?
It is a sad, fantastic tale!
Pray search more closely, and begin
Where strikers sleep against the bale
Upon the wharves, or, on the ground,
Beside the wheels that don’t go round.
Go where the horrid Anarchists
Are clamoring for lower rents,
Or where some other wretch insists
That with a meagre 10 per cent.
The man with money to invest
Should deem himself completely blessed.
Go round the six accursed States,
And note the movements of the crowd
Where Sloth with Socialism mates,
And common workers cry aloud
For distribution, fair and square,
Of products of the earth and air.
Rude agitators sit and rule
Where only men of means should be,
And Caucus minions play the fool
With sacred rights of Property.
The claim’s absurd! No poor? Alas!
What of the capitalistic class?
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
N.S.W. owners have little truck with
South Aus. “classic’ races, but Vic. owners find them
well worth picking up. The £3OO added to the
Morphettville Plate for two-year-olds was annexed
by E. D. Clarker’s Achenar, and J. N. McArthur’s
Kildalton scraped home in the Derby ( £500) from
the local Devron. (The finish was like that of the
Sydney Derby, but, if possible, closer.) The totali-
sator on this race showed the difference between the
correct mathematical odds and those engineered by
the interested bookmaker. Kidalton, reckoned a cer-
tainty, paid 265. for a pound ticket —10 to 3 on.
What would the man with the Bag offer about a
second favorite in a field of seven? The machine
offered 74 to 1, the others being at 9, 10, 37, 50 and
85 to 1.
“0.K.” : The liose of common sense should
certainly be turned upon those heated citizens who
groan at “Baron” when he wins a race with the Duke.
Did the howlers want Duke Foote to run stiff in
the big handicap at Randwick, or did they expect
him to incur a 101 b. penalty for the longer journey
at Flemington ? Seems to me that the haughty
Baron has them dead beaten when he asks: “Is the
person who backed Duke Foote for the Metropolitan
more entitled to consideration than someone who may
have supported him for the Melbourne Cup?” As a
matter of fact, the horse had been more widely
backed for the Cup than for the Met., and the
scratching of him for the Randwick event delighted
more people than it annoyed.
What a wonderful horse Duke Foote is!
In the recent Spring Meeting at Randwick, the hand-
some brute got away with the Spring Stakes, the
Craven Plate and Randwick Plate. This brings his
list of wins to 12, including a dead heat with Auro-
fodina. and his loot to £14,119, including the £4145
raked in last week and the £958 scooped at Tatter-
sail’s (Chelmsford Stakes) just previously. “J.
Baron” tops the winners with six wins worth £5813.
The only ones to get near him are J. E O’Brien, with
two wins ( £4861) and J. and P. Brun, with the
Metropolitan ( £4264). “K. S. Macleod” won two
races, but they were not so heavilv gilded, and
brought him only £1595. Baillieu Bros., whobagged
the Epsom, got £2465. Altogether, £34,023 was
distributed during the meeting to the struggling
owners. After all, it must have been a pleasant week
for these gentlemen.
♦ * * *
With very few Sydney horses figuring on
the card, Guineas Day, at Caulfield, threw little
light on the Cups question, and even the defeat of
Anna Carlovna in the Stakes was less debated than
the news of Duke Foote’s third victory at Randwick.
The mare’s performance was strikingly inconsistent
with her runaway win on the previous Saturday.
Then she looked as fresh after the event as before
she started. This time she was never near the front,
although she finished a respectable third. Presum-
ably something had happened to Anna Carlovna in
the race to account for her much sweat and fretful
mood when she returned to the birdcage. This was
the second time that Mountain Princess had beaten
the Maoriland mare, and the second time within a
week that Audacity had chased the little black lady
home.
Andelosia is either a better colt than he showed
himself at Randwick, or his opponents in the Caul-
field Guineas were a poor lot, for he beat them very
easily. If, as was stated before the race, the Son of
Fortunatus is only slowly recovering from a wrench
which spoilt his Sydney Derby chance, he may, after
all, prove the best of his year. In the meantime, the
form of the Victorian horses has to be tested by the
full strength of the Sydney onposition. Valido made
hacks of the lighter-weighted locals in the Toorak
Handicap, and Aleconner’s canture of the Epsom on
the previous Saturday looks like a bit of good luck.
Before The Bulletin is published the results of
Wednesday’s racing at Caulfield will have settled the
prevailing doubt as to whether Sea Prince is the Cup
“moral” that rumor has talked about for months
past, or is merely a moderately good animal, with a
had English temper and a false reputation. The
present writer pre-supnoses that Sea Prince has done
well in the Eclipse Stakes, in which case he is
entitled to start a hot favorite for the Caulfield Cup.
Seeing that E. A. Connolly offered last week to take
2 to 1 in thousands that he named the winner in
two tries, it seems a fair thing for a prophet to limit
his outlook to seven probables—say, Aurifer,
Anna Carlovna, Mountain Princess. Offa, Sea
Prince, Uncle Sam and Willow Green. The writer
nrefers Aurifer, Anna and Sea Prince to the others,
but because he reckons that the Maoriland mare is
nuite likely to get away in the front rank and escape
the risks of a jostling race he picks Anna Carlovna.
Apparently the trouble with Audacity
just now is a slightly damaged hoof. When the
handsome gee returned to the birdcage on Saturday,
after putting in a run which was an improvement on
his previous best, tnere was a little blood in the
hollow of bi« lame foot. On Monday he was scratched
for the Caul” "Id Cup, to the great grief of baronet
Clarke and all those punters who had got long shots
about the “good thing.”
* * * *
A surprising crowd of about 200 men
were discovered on Eight-Hour Day deeply inter-
ested in a cockfight in the bush at Canterbury (Syd-
ney). When the police appeared the crowd melted
with extraordinary rapidity, and the police managed
to capture onlv two spectators. The principals, the
seconds, the timekeepers, referee, judge and promo-
ters all got away. So the police let it out on the
two spectators, whose afternoon’s amusement cost
them £5 each, in default two months. Which shows
that watching a cockfight can be almost as expensive
as listening to a grand-opera.
South Australia’s golf championship was
won again by Tom Cheadle, locally bred —he won it first
when still a schoolboy—from Forbes, a comparatively
new resident. The latter was two up with four to
play, but a succession of amazing long putts gave
the game to Cheadle on the last green. The ladies’
championship went to Mrs. H. L. Rymill (nee Blue),
from Mrs. Maxwell Fowler ( nee Ayers).
Manly (Sydney) “goffers” put up a viru-
lent performance in the inter-club premiership this
season by winning the A, B, and C grades. In fact,
it seemed quite possible that had there been a whole
alphabet of grades, little Manly was capable of swal-
lowing the lot. It was a beautiful, well-polished
clean-up.
* * * *
Owing to a holiday, Sydney’s First-Grade
cricketers were able to crowd the second round into
one week. Gordon, which on Monday had declared at
411 for nine wickets, continued the same high-handed
action, and smashed up Middle Harbor’s first innings
for 127 and then put the follow-on through the mill
for 134. Thus it won by an innings and 150 runs.
Which was decisive. North Sydney, the premier
club, which had not tasted defeat for 14 matches,
came to grief against Petersham, having a deficiency
of 43 runs on the first innings. But for the speed
with which the light faded North would have had a
three-point win scored against it, as Petersham
wanted only 22 to win outright and had seven bats
to do it with. A mighty narrow escape for the cham-
pion club. The same thing happened with University
and Paddington. The latter had replied to Uni.’s
327 with a miserable 96, and still had 105 long,
weary minutes to keep its wickets up if it wanted to
stave off an outright win. Uni. played desperately
to wipe out the whole side, but when the day fell
dead Paddington had still four wickets standing, hav-
ing made 115 runs for six wickets. So Uni. got the
verdict on the first innings by 231 runs. Balmain
smacked up 278 in the first innings to Sydney’s 133,
and won by 145. The feature of this match was the
marvellous display by veteran Folkard. He had smit-
ten 101 on Monday, and on Saturday, in Sydney’s
first .innings, he took seven wickets for 81 runs. His
total performance that afternoon was 10 wickets for
128. Sydney had made 105 for five wickets in the
second innings when light failed. Waverley made
only 124 in its first stand against Central Cumber-
land, which smacked up 271 for seven wickets, and
then sent Waverley to the sticks again. The effort
to send it to the woods for a three-point win failed
utterly, as the Funereal Suburb had lost only seven
wickets (for 228) when the curtain fell. So C.C.
had to be content with a first-innings’ victory by 147.
Western Suburbs smacked 370, and Redfern replied
with 318 for seven sticks, so the game ended in a
draw. Two centuries graced the afternoon, Rix, of
Redfern, smiting 135 his side badly needed, and E. L.
Waddy, of Central Cumberland (Parramatta), com-
pleting his little lot at 131. Grace, of Waverley, hit
75; Gee, of Middle Harbor, 58 and 71; and Norris,
of Petersham, 70. Among the bowlers the lion was
T. J. E. Andrews, of Petersham, who took 11 wickets
for 144 runs in the match, Saturday’s contribution
being six for 66. Next came Folkard, of Balmain,
with 10 for 128 (seven for 81 and four for 47) ;
Bowden, of Gordon, seven for 58 (six for 38 and one
for 20) ; Stack, of University, six for 45 (five for
25 and one for 20) : Massie, of University also, six for
62 (four for 43 and two for 19) ; and Maclean, of
Gordon, six for 59.
PRAYING-PUG SUMMERS DEFEATS
CROUCHING BVERNDEN.
For verily, brethren , it is the straight left
and the blessed right that giveth the victory.
Melbourne’s club cricket, the first round
of which concluded on Saturday, promises to be live-
lier this season than for some years past. Already
the century list has started, Cheswass, of ’Cote, carried
his bat for 117 ; Seward, of Carlton, biffed 125 ; and
Baring, of East, 118. Per contra, teams that parti-
cularly wanted to make a large score failed miser-
ably. Collingwood, the premier club, was set the
task of beating Richmond’s 208, and only just man-
aged it with 217. Still, it managed it. Similarly,
’Roy, runner-up last season, being faced with St.
Hilda’s distinctly moderate 163, with difficulty
snatched up 43 and had to follow on, losing ulti-
mately on the first innings by 120 runs. NOrthcote
put together a satisfactory 342 for six wickets, then
declared —and almost fell in, the Easterns piling up
339 runs. ’Cote deserved to win for its pluck. Carl-
ton declared with eight down for 277, a score which
Essendon could have equalled if it had got a move
on ; but the tired feeling encompassed it, and 176 for
eight wickets was the total. South ran up 312 runs
and left P’ran barely sufficient time to catch them, the
ultimate tale of the latter being 186 for five sticks.
The Melb.-’Varsity match didn’t count,, and was only
notable in that W. Armstrong scored 5 and Ransford
99. The only bowlers to push themselves into pro-
minence were youngsters Morrison and left-hander
Moule, of St. Kilda. with four for 21 and three for
18 respectively ; and Mackay, of Richmond, with five
for 71.
* * * *
The long-drawn-out struggle for the out-
ward and visible sign of the Soccer football cham-
pionship of N.S.W., the Gardiner Challenge Cup,
came to an end on Saturday, when Annandale came
up against Newtown in snlendid fettle. The heat
was stifling, the ground hard as rock, and the struggle
grim. It is doubtful if Newtown ever had a chance,
being outplayed and outgeneralled right through. But
whatever chance it ever had was absolutely thrown
away by the illegal play of over-anxious ’bailers.
The combination, the temperate order and the high
speed of Apnandale were invincible, and its kicking
was deadly. Newtown was overthrown and stamped
flat by three goals to nil—a very decisive win in this
branch of hoofball. A tremendous crowd of Soccer
enthusiasts rolled up to watch the final struggle.’
* * * *
At time of writing the Coburg Cycling
Club (Vic.) is leading in the Dunlop 100-miles Relay
Desnatch Test, with 4hr. 35min. 13sec. It was run
very close lately bv the Bathurst Club, which did the
distance in 4hr. 40min„ 22sec, The members of both
teams have won the medallions for riding the 100
miles in under the five hours. A notable thing about
the Bathurst club was that the average age of the 10
riders was 18 years. The end of this month will see
the allocation of the £lO special prize for the in-
terim advantage, so there should be some tall riding
during the next few weeks.
Those two apparently invincible English
cyclists, W. J. Bailey and Leon Meredith, have won
the amateur world championships again. Bailey
took the sprint race and Meredith the 100 kilometres
(62 i miles), at the Berlin Stadium, Germany. The
100 kiloms. is a motor-paced championship. Meredith
led from the start, and won easily with over a mile
to spare from Beyer, of Germany, and Blekemolon, of
Holland, covering the distance in lhr. 27min. 58sec. The
Frenchman, Fossier, who was a hot favorite in some
quarters, was quite out of it. The professional cham-
pionships were decided at the same meeting and over
the same course. Walter Rutt, who was here in the
days when Sydney Thousands glittered, won the sprint
premiership of the world, while the long-distance
event went to P. Guignard, since dead. A. H. Shep-
herd, the Australian, did not compete, owing to the
death of his child.
If the toiling backbone of the country
during the next few weeks should hear hymns of joy
ascending from the motor-cars that speed by, he need
have no fear. It will be merely the road hog express-
ing his joy over an announcement that the price of
Dunlop tyres is to be, or has been, substantially re-
duced, as the result of improvements in manufac-
turing methods.
* * * *
A motor-cycle meeting at the Melbourne
Sports Ground on Saturday afternoon turned out
more or less disastrously. In the Dunlop Scratch
Race Mapleston’s machine skidded on the greasy
track, and the rider found himself lying prone with
a dislocated shoulder. That was bad enough, but
in the Dunlop Handicap McColl’s belt came off ; his
machine wobbled, and crashed into a fence at 50
miles an hour. McGoll’s head struck the post and
he was taken to the hospital in a serious condition,
insensible, and suffering from concussion and other
injuries.
The mermen and maidens of Sydney were
abroad on Saturday when the swimming season was
formally opened by the North Sydney Club’s handicap
at Lavender Bay baths. The race, spread over two
heats and a final, was won by E. C. Cox, with the
Wright Brothers a dead heat for second place. The
day was ideal —and so were the attendances at all the
baths. In fact, in some places there were more
bathers than there was bath accommodation; for,
owing to the encouragement given to the practice of
swimming by the Education Department, the rising
generation in the Ma Capital threatens to become
amphibious. Australia has done well at swimming,
though, as pointed out by Cecil Healy, it is the only
sport—barring, perhaps, ping-pong—that has not had
interest in it fostered by the visits of foreign expo-
nents. But one such is to drop in early next year—
to wit, Duke Kahanamoku, the Hawaiian, who is so
mighty a swimmer that he recently beat an octopus
in a stand-up fight in the ocean. The impending visit
has driven most of the top-notchers into training
before their time this year, and already quite a crowd
of young men are hard at work, and all in splendid
fettle, to qualify for a chance to measure themselves
against this truly wonderful swimmer.
After much thought aud wrestling in
prayer, the N.S.W. Baseball Association declined to
allow the affiliation of the Limited Liability Co. that
proposes to run the Summer competition. Unfortu-
nately, it seems that the Association has no power
to disqualify or otherwise prevent members of its
clubs playing for the co. So on Saturday the lat-
ter started its competitions at the Agricultural
Ground, and pitted Leichhardt against the Waverley
Red Sox and Petersham against the Waverley Blue
Sox. Waverley won both games, Leichhardt being
downed by 23 to 10, and Petersham by 22 to seven.
As a regatta, Henley-on-Parramatta was
very pleasant and successful on Saturday; but as
Henley it was an utter failure. In pomp, social
tone and “boiled dog” in general, it failed to come
up to within seven miles of even Henley-on-Yarra.
But the management worked hard, and, by means of
the short-courses system of racing, provided a finish
about every 10 minutes or so. The afternoon was
perfect, and the racing spiced with excite-
ment. The finish of the Maiden Eights
was a very stern fight between Sydney and Glebe.
Sydney got its nose in front, and, though the boats
were together right to the finish, Glebe could never
recover that lost nose, and finished two
lengths behind. That sort of race is always worth
watching. For the Champion Fours no less than five
crews entered—Leichhardt Nos. 1 and 2, Balmain,
Enterprise and Sydney. The difficult task of starting
them was well negotiated. After a strenuous struggle
Leichhardt No. 1 got about a length in front of
Enterprise. Half-way down Enterprise and Balmain
were making desperate attempts to overtake Leich-
hardt No. 1, and away to perdition in the rear
Sydney was chasing Leichhardt No. 2. Balmain
closed up on Enterprise and they both gained slightly
on slippery Leichhardt. Then Balmain spurted past
Enterprise, but failed to get closer than half a
length to Leichhardt, that crew swinging along
quite unperturbed. And so they finished.
Boxing is now quite a polite profession.
When Jonel Sullivan blazed like a bonfire in the
United States, it was necessary to take elaborate
precautions that “the men” shouldn’t meet. If they
did the furniture was liable to get damaged, and the
peacemakers marked. But on Saturday night, after
the scrap, Evernden presented Summers with his
gloves as a souvenir of the other’s victory, and later
on the two drank sodawater in the managerial office,
and Summers examined the gashed eye of Evernden
with a brotherly interest.
Gladiator James Sullivan, who so mys-
teriously disappeared previous to a fight in Melbourne,
has returned to the fold, and next Saturday night will
meet Jerry Jerome in the place where the latter made
such an amazing stand against Pat Bradley and
broke his hand on the latter’s head. It speaks won-
ders for the recuperative powers of this 44-year-old
darkie that he is able to meet such an opponent in
so short a time. Sullivan, it will be remembered,
was once middleweight champion of England.
The much-discussed meeting of the Eng-
lish welter-weights, Arthur Evernden and Johnny
Summers, took place at Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night, in the presence of over 20,000 men, most of
them roaring with excitement. Summers is t h P
welter-weight champion of England, and i n th P 1
of his birth had already scored a victory over
youth with the mighty wallop. But it was argued
that at .13 Summers was getting no younger am!
Evernden, at about 26, had not got old so fast n
the other hand, there was a firm conviction thal if
the battle lasted 20 rounds it would go to Summed
on points ; for not one in a score would have it (hat
Summers could knock the long-lipped warrior out
On the other hand, no one doubted the ability if
Evernden, provided he could deliver the wallop at
the right address, to put Summers among the poppies
Summers weighed lOst. 3ilb. to Evernden’s lOst
641 b. About a week before he weighed in (or out!
Evernden went list. 81b., and it is quite posible that
the shedding of so much tissue in so brief a time
may have affected his stamina.
At the sound of the gong Evernden attacked with
the cold, temperate fury of a steam-roller. He bored
right in, and Summers met him with quick stabbing
lefts that baulked his attack and disconcerted him
badly. That stabbing left spoilt Evernden’s timing
and caused his sleep-laden swings to go wild; and
when they did go straight to the mark, Summers
shifted that object with some of the smartest ducking
ever seen in the Stadium. It saved him again and
again, and Evernden had the mortification of seeing
absolutely decisive blows miss by—just enough. Sum-
mers was magnificent, and the way he stood in and
mixed it with his gritty opponent, anticipating his
blows with counters that got home first, and swing-
ing three and four rights and lefts to the head and
neck before Evernden could let one go, made the
conqueror of Picato and Burns look as slow as a
beetle. In the fifth round their heads crashed toge-
ther, and Evernden reeled back with the blood trick-
ling in a fast-running stream from a bad cut over
the right eye. Even that terrific smash did not
seem to affect him just then, and he bounded at
Summers like a tiger and shook him badly. But Sum-
mers came through. In the sixth, Summers cut
loose, in an amazing manner, with right and left
swings, hurling in the full force of his body. Evern-
den placed his hands over his jaws and took the
blizzard stoically. Towards the end Summers charged
him, but was met with a heavy counter that all but
sent the champion down. On the way back to his
chair Summers touched his sock-hidden scapular three
times and repeated the operation so often in his corner
that his attendants had difficulty in getting him
ready. In the seventh, Evernden became very aggres-
sive, and showered a quick succession of blows, any
one of which would have dazed an elephant; and
Summers dodged them all or smothered the attack
with a quick clinch. Summers’s mouth was bleeding,
and his left eye was cut, but that ghastly gash in
Evernden’s cheek simply welled blood, and Summers
bashed it unmercifully. It was after the 10th that
Johnny began to take dopes from a colorless medi-
cine bottle, which was said to contain holy water.
He had followed up every repulse in splendid style
and landed about four blows to every one he had
received, and still Evernden seemed unshaken. Sum-
mers still had to duck blows which would have
knocked his head clean over the gasometer, and,
strong as he was, the champion was beginning to
feel the strain. Compared with the other’s celerity
in side-stepping and back-stepping and duck-
ing and getting in his blows, Evernden was
deadly slow. He wasted valuable seconds
waiting for an opening which was never
given, and every time Summers would anticipate the
attack. Had Evernden been as quick as Summers he
would have knocked the latter out half a dozen times
while the young man brushed his hair with his
glove. But*he wasn’t; and as the fight went raging
on, Evernden’s strength began to ebb. His blows at
close-quarters lost their sting, and it became evident
that he was done. Summers might have gone for a
knock-out, but he didn’t; whether it was from caution
or chivalry no man knoweth. In the 18th he closed in
and swung a heavy bash to Evernden’s diaphragm
which caused him to emit a loud yell. After that,
Evernden was done. Though he raged somewhat in
the last two rounds he had ceased to be dangerous;
and at the end of the term the hand of the referee
descended gracefully on the sanctified head of Sum-
mers amidst thunders of applause.
* * * *
The struggling N.S.W. Government, which
is skipping round so nimbly in pursuit of the
elusive shilling, should seriously consider the Tote.
In little Maoriland last year no less than £3,600,000
hurtled through the machine, an increase of
£1,766.132 in five years. How much money do the
bookmakers take out of the pockets of the N.S.W.
public every year? It must be an enormous sum.
Anyhow, it is a certainty that by permitting the
depredations of the penciller, the State Govern-
ment not only fails to protect the best interests of
the public, but loses money on a large scale.

4 Billy T.”: Circumstances alter advertisements.
The Quinlan Opera Co. was in Johannesburg at the
time of the July riots; but since their arrival in
Australia the members have been conspicuously reti-
cent on this subject. What makes their silence the
more clamorous is the fact that when the horror of
the abomination they saw in Jo’berg on the two
most fatal days was strongest upon them, a few of
the pros, wrote letters to England in almost hysterical
condemnation of the soldiers and the police, stigma-
tising the frequent shootings as “cold-blooded
murder.” London Daily Herald published a couple
of these letters ; but no Australian paper has yet been
prodded by any press agent into reprinting them,
although any old, unimportant item concerning the
color of the tenor’s socks is eagerly seized upon.
“Stargazer”: The curious unreality of American
fiction attaches to the American play. Both are un-
commonly clever, both have frequent features that
are human and convincing, but neither is really con-
vincing. The obtruding artificiality destroys the
general effect. The same sensation of wrongness
stands out in all the recent Uncle Sam dramas seen
in Australia, yet one finds it hard to put a finger
on the feature or features responsible for the artistic
failure. Probably the Yankee writer’s affection for
verbal smartness causes much of the mischief. His
characters are all smart in the same way, and their
flippancy is very cloying. When they cease being
flippant, their pathos is turgid by contrast. Per-
haps Americans are like that.
“Petramos”: An Australian syndicate threatens to
import sundry cinematograph celebrities, including
John Bunny and Maurice Costello, of the Yitagraph
Co., and Max Linder, of Pathe Bros. Costello, by the
way, i? boomed by the Vitagraph people as an ex-
Australian stockman. Linder, the French comedian,
made his first big vaudeville hit in Berlin. The
comedy, “The Pedicure,” in which present writer saw
him, was notable for its opening. When Linder was
due at the Wintergarten the manager appeared and
stated that the engagement was the most expensive,
etc., etc. Two minutes later he reappeared to say
that the comedian had not arrived at the theatre,
and craved the audience’s indulgence. The orchestra
played a brief selection. A telephone was (natu-
rally) attached to a wall of the theatre, and the
manager, again appearing, stated that, as Linder
was still absent, he would ring up his apartments.
Immediately he rang a moving-picture was switched
on, showing Linder in bed and reaching for the tele-
phone receiver. The manager informed him that
the audience at the Wintergarten was waiting, where-
at the picture-Linder made a hurried toilet and had
an amazing number of adventures in his rush to
meet his engagement. Eventually he got aboard an
airship, which sailed over the Wintergarten. There,
of course, he fell out of it in the good old flicker -
scopic way. The moment he reached the roof the
celluloid Linder vanished, bricks and mortar tumbled
from above, and in the midst of them came the real
Linder.
“Rosna” (London): An early and cheerful item
of the “Proms,” which have begun to cram the
Queen’s Hall nightly, was the performance of two
new Percy Grainger orchestral pieces, conducted by
Golden Locks himself. They were “An Irish Tune
from County Derry” and “Shepherds Hey,” and
both of them sent the huge house clamoring for
more. “Shepherds Hey” is an ancient Morris dance
~one of many collected by Cecil Sharp, who one
time was choir-master at the Adelaide Anglican
Cathedral. Since those days Sharp has been hunting
folk-songs in Britain, and from one old dame alone
he is said to have recovered more than a hundred
tunes. The Grainger piano version of “Shepherds
Hey” bears the engaging inscription:—“Shepherds
Hey: Morris Dance, collected by Cecil Sharp. Dished
up for the piano by Percy Grainger.” The inside
is equally colloquial, the performer being requested
to play “fast,” “acidly,” clingingly,” “clangingly,”
“bumpingly,” etc., in plain English instead of mys-
terious Italian. The title page naively informs the
world that the setting “is not suitable to dance
Morris dances to.” The Graingers have let their
house at Chelsea and gone abroad. Percy is to
noises in St. Petersburg, return U< to London for 'n St

“Androcles and the Lion,” Shaw’s latest effort
shows us the Early Christians in the Roman arena’
The lion, of course, recognises in the chief martvr
the friend who, in the prologue, had extracted the
anchor from his foot. W T hen one of the martvrs is
goaded to fight the gladiators he slays the lot of
them. Caesar thereupon pardons the Christians • but
as the lion has to he fed, Androcles offers himself
Tf 16 h.on pardons Androcles, and, presumably, gives
. m a / ree P ass > good for life, over all the railways
* n Africa. After that, the lion ought to sing a
hymn, but doesn’t. Doubtless he is hoarse. It is an
excellent piece of fooling that has come rather too
* onK after “The Sign of the Cross.”
.The veteran G. R. Sims has got back to the stage
with a “human interest” melodrama, “The Ever
Open Door,” something akin to “Two Little Vaga-
bonds,” “The Christian” and “The Lights of
London.” There are a couple of “waifs,” both, of
course, played by girls. A midnight march of the
Rescue Mission through Westminster slums (which
are some of the worst in London) is a big feature,
Hilda Spong, in the principal grown-up part, made
her firs t London reappearance after her world tour
with “Everywoman.”
“Petramos”: Regarding “The Miracle,” shortly to
be filmed in Australia. Only two cities—London and
Vienna—could stand the expense of the original pro-
duction, the people in which totalled 3000. The
mystic play came to London Olympia, chiefly at the
instigation of one Carre, who as a versatile showman
in a big way beats the whole British band. He for-
merly worked in a circus. Rheinhardt, the producer
was a vaudeville performer. Partly because the prices
were so high, and partly because Olympia’s reputation
was more horsey than churchy, London stayed away
in great numerosity, until the Daily Mail asked the
city where it had hidden its reputation for culture.
Then “The Miracle” grew into a well-merited suc-
cess, and in some mystic way the Mail ultimately
ST°t control of the film, which thus comes to Aus-
tralia via Fleet-street.
“Motley”: May Wirth, niece of Phil and George,
is, at 18, the foremost circus equestrienne in the
world. She is a Queensland native. Her superla-
five ability became evident in Australia, which she
left two years ago. Engaged with Barnum and
Bailey in America, she was the brightest light of
that monster organisation. Now Hagenbeck and
Co. have engaged her as principal star in their im-
mense circus and zoo, to show at Olympia, London,
I remember two supposed lady equestrians who vied
with men, but they both turned out to be men. One
was the famous “Ella” Zovara, and the other was
Ernest Shand, l#iown as “Daisv.” Ella Zoyara was
Omar Kingsley, and when he bewitched Australia
his wife and two children were with him. The
Melbourne Public Library has an outrageously rhe-
torical and unreliable biographv of “Ella” Zoyara,
written by a certain (or uncertain) Walker, known as
Tom Cringle. Years after he had left off being a
circus lady Kingsley revisited Australia as a ring-
master.

THERE are over 200 “ Chalmers” in N.S.W. to-day. Where there’s rough, sandy
country and terrible roads —where there are steep hills and hard going—where there
are long distances and practically no roads —you’ll find the “ Chalmers.” The car

Big Ben solves your rising problem
Put jolly old Big Ben
on the job. Let him solve
your early rising problem.
You have a rising prob-
lem, if you put your whole
heart in your work.
For if you are a heavy
worker, you require heavy sleep
and plenty of it.
So leave your call with Big
Ben. Go to sleep and sleep your
best. Forget the rising hour.
His merry morning call will
call you every day at any time
you say. It will keep on call-
ing until you’re wide awake.
Big Ben stands sev,en inches
tall. He’s massive, well poised
triple plated. His face is frank,
open, easy to read —his keys
large, strong, easy to wind.
Hd’s two alarm clocks in one —a
long alarm ringing for five min-
utes continuously, an intermitt-
ent alarm ringing for ten min-
utes at half-minute intervals.
Big Ben is made in La Salle,
Illinois, U.S.A., by Westclmx. His
price, is 12Y6, postage 1/- extra. If
you cannot find him in your town,
send f a money order to the makers’
Australian office, Westclox, 63 Pitt
Street, Sydney. They will have
one mailed to you at ©ace by the
nearest jeweller.
