The Inky Way.
Dead, in South Africa, as the result of a buggy
accident, W. H. Milton, lightweight boxer, racehorse
owner and good sportsman generally, who was sport-
ing editor of the Kalgoorlie Miner for nearly 10
years. He was one of the first men who left Sydney
for the Westralian goldfield, and (to his credit or
otherwise) was the introducer of whippet-racing
there. He had been in Africa about two years, and
was at the time of his death judge for the Johannes-
burg Racing Club.
* * * *
Dr. Cleary, R.C. Bishop of Auckland (M.L.), tha
new Coadjutor-Archbishop of Adelaide,' has trav-
elled the Inky Way, having been editor of the
Tablet, the organ of the R'.C.’s in Maoriland. He
was formerly a priest in Ballarat. Archbishop
O’Reily, to whom he coadjutes, has also done a
variety of presswork, in Westralia and elsewhere.
* * * *
“Absalom Jones”: Bert Bailey says he will stake
Dad’s whiskers on the Charles Garvice drama, “Just
a Girl,” which he is to give Australia shortly. Ap-
parently Garvice had never thought of developing
into a dramatic genius till Bailey suggested the
offence. The Australian manager describes Garbage’s
correspondence as “most unassuming.” In England
he is by no means discredited in the best circles. I
recollect a London dinner 1 at which he was the lion.
Other celebrities Were there (Maurice Hewlett among
’em), but they simply weren’t in the menagerie.
Few Australian pressmen who have gone to the
Great Fog to follow the Inky Way have made such
rapid progress as Harry B. Manderson, formerly of
Melb. Aoe. Manderson went to London for the
King's Coronation. An Australian who knew
Dominion politicians was handy at the moment, with
the Imperial Conference just meeting. Manderson
wrote “specials” about Australian politics, with
sketches of Fisher, Pearce and others, and they were
accepted by the Daily Chronicle and Westminster
Gazette and others. He walked past the flunkeys
guarding the Foreign Office, interviewed the Premiers,
and got away with the earliest Conference news, while
the English pressmen were waiting outside the build-
ing. The transport workers, dockers’ and railway
strikes were occurring about that time, and the way*
was paved for reliable articles on Australia’s arbi-
tration laws. Manderson, making good on these,
too, was sent by the Chronicle to India for the
Durbar. He brought off a couple of “scoops,” for
which he received a bonus cheque upon his return.
Securing an engagement immediately -as sub-editor
of Lloyd’s Publishing Agency, he was despatched
last year, almost at a moment’s notice, to the West
Indies to write a book, “Twentieth Century Impres-
sions of the West Indies.” The book is In the press,
and the author received word on his 27th birthday
a few months agq that his salary had been raised to
£lOOO a year. Incidentally, he married the daughter
of a United States banker at New Jersey (U.S.A.).
The honeymoon is being spent in Peru, where the
young Australian has, gone to write another “Twen-
tieth Century Impressions” book. Lloyd, of Lloyd’s
Publishing Ageney, is an Australian, who, 12 years
ago, was business manager of the now defunct Perth
Morning Herald. With the dawn of the 20th
century he conceived the idea of writing up places
and people and disposing of handsomely-bound, well-
printed works at a substantial figure to the persons
whose biographical sketches appeared therein. He
started with “Twentieth Century Impressions of
Western Australia,” the literary work being done by
F. Humphries, at one time chief of staff of the
Aoe. Lloyd then went to London, and with his
“Twentieth Century Impressions” of many countries
has been coining money ever since.
The Editor. —Sir, —I have long been perturbed by
the coarse, ungodly tone of your Bulletin, and harve
ever viewed with alarm the possible effects of such a
publication upon the youth and innocence of our
community. However, sin, I am not one to bear
malioe. On the contrary, lam willing to co-operate
with you in a Reform campaign, which will, inci-
dentally, give you an opportunity of demonstrating
the sincerity of your protests that you desire to
uplift humanity. Now, sir, in the August issue of the
Ladies’ Home Journal appeared a pictorial sermon
by the editor. It consisted of a page of snapshots
taken on the various Atlantic beaches, and depicted
the same outrageous goings-on that disfigure our
holiday resorts, only here (alas, that it should be
admitted) to a much more shocking extent. The
central picture is one, alas! only too familiar to
this city. It was a portrait of a hussy in bathing
costume (with skirt so short that it displayed her
stockinged legs), and being embraced by an uenscru-
pulous profligate, also in bathing attire! Both were
facing the camera, smiling and unashamed. There
were many other harrowing scenes, including one of
a group of people lolling about the sands, a mincjled
mass of male and female. (“Sunbaking,” I believe
this is called. “Sin-making,” I call it, sir!) Across
this thrilling pictorial sermon appeared in large black
letters: “How Much of This Would You Like Your
Daughter to Share t” Now, sir, such a series of views
would probably delight t-he class of readers you
cater for But a touching editorial appeal to home-
puritv like that quoted above might turn sueh a
work of evil into an instrument of Righteousness and
an influence for Good! Hence my appeal to you to
go and do likewise. I will help you. Although mere
words cannot describe the emotions that thrilled my
entire being on seeing these things, yet was I so con-
vinced of the efficacy of this form of pictorial sermon
that, should vou accept my offer and decide to pub-
lish something similar. I would undertake the dis-
tasteful task of personally assisting in it. I am
indeed prepared to bear the brunt of the work.
Amongst my flock of converts in this city are several
estimable young ladies, who might be prevailed unon
to co-operate with me, out of sheer kindliness and a
spirit of self-sacrifice. They include Miss Knock.y
Dopeall (domestic duties, Crown-street), the Misses
Maisie Fliekett and Jiggy Dashall (both of whom, I
admit, might have once been met in a well-known
city bar Gy the Religious Editor when on city mission
work), Madam Vere de Vene (masseuse, Darling-
hurst). Birdie Wobblegait (Coogee Surf-Siren Club),
and Miss Spemmedays O’Halloran. late of the
Hotel, Lower George-street). But should the Religious
Editor’s pocket diarv contain the names and addres-
ses of any of his disciples upon whom we oould
positively rely for active assistance in this seheme, I
would offer no objection to all arrangements being
left in his hands. However, I would again emphasise
my readiness to take an active part in the presenta-
tion and preparation of these really excellent pic-
torial sermons; and would urge upon him the neces-
sity of such a good work being taken in hand as im-
mediately as possible.' —Yours anxiouslv. Ephraim

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Scotch comedians had come
To make a stir in Brumby Town.
With skirl of pipe and beat of drum
They drew the eager people down
from Dingle Hill and Burgoo way
lo hear them singing “Scots wha’ ha'e.’’
They danced in kilts the maids to shock
With show of wild and shaggy shins;
They chorused “Stop Yer Ticklin’, Jock,’’
And told o’ Glaskee’s little sins ;
Then acted in a Highland play,
Which Tavish said was “awfu’ gay.”
The second morn McDougal went
To air his “ain peculiar views”
To Mr. Smith, the writing gent
Who edited the Bkumby News ;
And sore he knocked poor Smith about
Before he cleaned the office out.
“Scratch company!” McDougal roared,
And shook the News in front of Smith-
“ Scratch company, ye things abhorred !
I’ll teach ye who y-s’re dealin’ with.
Ye scurvy, low-lived human botch
To ca’ us scratch because we’re Scotch!”
victoria.

What Guns Are Offered
At Your Dealer’s?
FOR a horse or a dog, you of course go to the
dealer who is known to stock thoroughbreds*
You have confidence, in his judgment.
So, too, in sporting goods equipment, you may rely
on that dealer who stocks Remington-UMC rifles and
shotguns* That, in a measure, is proof of his being
well-informed.
Remington-UMC repeating rifles and shotguns are the
last word in breech-loading arms construction. The clean,
trim, thoroughbred lines that make them the sportsmen's first
choice are the result of designing which, among other things,
eliminates the protruding hammer and closes the breech.
You should be informed on these and
other important points in modern gun-making.
Your name and address on a postcard will
will bring an explanatory booklet.
Remington Arms-Union Metallic Cartridge Co.
Australian Agent, D. D. Bryant, P. O. Box 686, Melbourne’

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—-
the strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What
a relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt
the ear. It fits the ori-
fice of the ear with pre-
cision and comfort. The
division that runs across
the mouth is to allow the
removal and insertion of
the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the he a r ing-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
if you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
£>
cured, and you can be cured, too. You inav wght
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without
the least danger.
. f*he price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order Form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hogskin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of The
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from ,J. P. Talty. 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

HOW TO PREVENT ACID STOMACHS
AND FOOD FERMENTATION.
By a STOMACH SPECIALIST.
As a specialist who has spent many years
in the study and treatment of stomach
troubles, I have been forced to the conclu-
sion that most people who complain of
stomach trouble possess stomachs that are
absolutely ljealthy and normal. The real
trouble, that which causes all the pain and
difficulty/ is acid in the stomach, usually
due to, or aggravated by, food fermentation.
Acid irritates the delicate lining of the
stomach and food fermentation Causes wind,
which distends the stomach abnormally,
causing that full, bloated feeling. Tmus
both acid and fermentation interfere with
and retard the process of digestion. The
stomach is usually healthy and normal, but
irritated almost past endurance by these
foreign elements —acid and wind. In all
such cases—and they comprise over 90 per
cent, of all Stomach difficult : es —the first and
only step necessary is to neutralise the acid
and stop the fermentation by taking in a
little warm or cold water immediately after
eating, half a teaspoonful of b : surated mag-
nesia, which is doubtless the best and only
really effective antacid and food corrective
known. The acid will be neutralised and
the fermentation stopped almost instantly,
and your stomach will at once proceed to
digest the food in a healthy, normal manner.
Be sure to ask your chemist for the bisu-
rated magnesia, as I have found other forms
utterly lacking in its peculiarly valuable
properties.—F.J.G.*

THE BANANA LAND
The Rockhampton end of the northern
coastal line was shoved a little further north
the other day when the bits of steel were
laid as far as Yaamba. Of old, Yaamba,
which is “at the head of navigation of the
Fitzroy waters,” had ideas of becoming a
“seaport” for the western country. In those
days folk climbed up the rivers as far as
they could go with their small craft, and
then sat down to wait for townships to
grow. But, unfortunately for the budding
Yaamba, boats started to grow too, and the
city-to-be shrivelled to nothing. Even Rock-
hampton is miles too far up the river to-
day. When it knew it couldn’t be a sea-
port, Yaamba pined for a railway. After
a delay of a little over 50 years, it has
come, and now there seems nothing what-
ever for Yaamba to live for.
A curious little row is raging in Bris-
bane. Some time ago the Walker whisky
people began to send out their goods in
patent stoppered bottles that are practi-
cally non-refillable. It seemed a good thing
for the hotel-keeper who wants to sell stuff
true to label in his own shop, and therefore
wants to see others do the same in their
shops and thus compete fairly. But, in-
stead of meeting the innovation with three
cheers, the Bung Association met it with
47 curses, and promptly talked of putting
the price of this whisky up to 9d., so as to
knock the non-refillable bottle out. If Bung
were charged something extra for the
special bottle, or if he couldn’t get as much
for it empty as he can for the ordinary
empty bottle, there would be something in
his complaint; but as he isn’t charged any-
thing extra, and as he can get as much for
the special bottle as for the ordinary, the
only conclusion the public can come to is
that Bung doesn’t like the patent-stoppered
bottle because it isn't refillable. And if
that is a reflection on Bung, Bung has only
himself to thank for it; and if he persists
in his objection, be will deserve all the re-
flection that he will certainly get.
Passed out, the other day, in the Gympie
Hospital, James Nash, the discoverer of the
goldfield which he stuck to to the last. In
1866 Nash, who had been trying his luck at
Nanango, decided to give it best and shoulder
bluey—and then Gympie blazed forth, and
Queensland lifted its tired head. Within six
months 15,000 miners were pot-holing the
alluvial. Curtis discovered his famous nug-
get of 9750 z., worth £3675. (Curtis didn’t
get much out of it when the lawyers had
cut their costs out. He died in Rockhampton
about four years ago. Which is all another
story.) When the alluvial gave out, the
shallow shafts put in their appearance and
the rich specimens were dollied for their
metal; and soon the stamper batteries fol-
lowed. There were some phenomenal crush-
ings in those days. The Caledonian treated
10 tons for 9980 z.; the Lady Marv bestowed
23200 z. on its owners from 13cwt. Within
five years of its birth Gympie had robbed
£1,500,000 from the pockets of Mother Earth.
Then other things happened. The first floor
of black slate, which carried the gold-bearing
quartz, gave out, and was duly succeeded by
green stone which carried nothing worth
carrying, and the slump hit Gympie hard.
But some of the most financial shows amal-
gamated and started to probe the earth for
a second floor of black slate —and found it
and more gold. Nowadays Gympie spends
most of its time looking for the third and
fourth floors ; but it is getting very worn in
the search. Last year, when Gympie had
produced 2,653,2930 z. of gold, a generous
Government opened its heart and gave Nash
£lO0 —but it doesn’t want the news of this
generosity to get into the papers. The old
man was 79 when he died.
Another “honorable understanding” has
gone wrong. Some time ago the Brisbane
breweries decided to raise the price of beer
to the publicans. Then the publicans put
their heads together and decided to raise the
price to the public. A glass was to be
4d. instead of 3d., and a whole pint
was to be fivepence. But the new prices
had been in force only two d.ays when it
was discovered that trade was slack at most
bars, while a few pumps were running hot
through excess of toil at the old prices. Bung
then realised sadly that a man will walk
much further to save tuppence towards the
next pint than had been supposed.
Bananaland’s metropolitan police have
made a new record for smartness. A few
months ago two cheques were forged and
cashed at Ipswich. Cahill’s men soon smelt
out the forger, and one William Smith was
landed in Boggo-road Gaol. After waiting
for 114 days for trial, he was one day in-
formed that he didn’t forge the cheque, and
with a parting word not to do it again he
was set free. He was the victim of mis-
taken identity, and it took the law only 114
days to find it out.
A fine Australian mother has just gone to
join some of her children—Mrs. Sophie
Corrie. Forty years ago she put her young
brood of six under her wing and went on
the land at Colo Vale. For 30-odd years
she worked the farm herself, and made it a
successful proposition. At the age of 74
she sold out, and, unaccompanied, proceeded
to tour the world. Then looking for a place
to settle down, she decided that Queens-
THE LAST STRAW.
THE LAW ( to cyclist toiling up
precipitous road) : “ ’Ere !
what are ye doin' without a
brake ? ”
land gave her the best chance of a long life ;
so she went to Brisbane, and lasted out till
she was 83. Incidentally she wrote a book
or two; but it would take a lot more than
that to hold all her knowledge and wisdom.
“Kobbi” : A deputation asked Digby Den-
ham to consider what a grievous wrong had
been done the-Public Hospitals by depriving
them of the “drunk” fines from the local
courts, the last Licensing Act having found a
new use for them. But Denham has no money
to spare these days, wherefore he asked
whether the deputation didn’t think that a
subsidy of £2 for everv £1 of private sub-
scriptions was a fair thing. As a matter of
fact, it is a lot more than a fair thing so
long as the people who put up the £1 have
all the say in spending the £3. One institu-
tion the writer knows once poured out £157
on a fowlhouse!
Governor Macgregor confesses to having
once struck a black man. Up in Papua, he
says, he saw a young dude sawing off an
animal’s tail with a blunt knife. Pie did
not attempt to reason with the butcher, but
gave him a clip on the ear. “Humanitarian
impulse prompted the blow,” says the public,
of course. Not at all —merely trade rivalry.
Macgregor. as a good surgeon, hated to see
the thins: done in a bungling fashion.
Daylight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I woke at morn. The silver light came in
And brought me back again to Earth, the
Ileal;
It brought again the Joys of Earth, the Sin,
And drove afar the ghostly Night’s Ideal.
Ai d when I wake in death, I may discern
How this poor Life was but a shadowy
stream ;
And in the actual daylight I may learn
Its ioys were but the phantoms of a
dream.

QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 11, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
Governor Macgregor is glumly inspecting
the prickly-pear pest at Dulacca; Lady
Macgregor is busy supporting a f§te in aid
of the Ministering Children’s League; and
the Vision returned yesterday from Belle
Vue, where she has been sojourning under
the hospitable roof of Mrs. Lumley Hill.
That is the vice-regal roster for the week.
The Nudgee College Boys’ concert on
Wednesday was a huge success. The Cen-
tennial Hall bulged with sisters, cousins,
aunts and the rest of the family; also his
Ex., Lady Macgregor, Judge Real, Ted
Real the aide, and a few professors. Lady
Macgregor was handed a big bunch of red
and white roses by a bashful youth, and the
audience (with its feet) helped a fledgling
orchestra to play Irish airs. The item of
the evening was “The Miser,” written by
Mr. Moliere, and wrestled manfully with by
the boys, who pulled through much better
than the average school actor usually does.
Henry Alcock, M.A., of Magdalen Col-
lege. Oxford, has been appointed lecturer
in Hislorv and Economics to the Queens-
land University. At Magdalen he won
three exhibitions, and gained honorable men-
tion for the Stanhope historical prize essay
in 1907. He has also studied in France
and Germany, and has been master at Tet-
tenhall College, in Staffordshire, and at
Kendal Grammar School.
Dr. Hopkins came back last Monday by
the Shropshire. He looks quite well again
and years vounger. While he was away he
attended the International Medical Con-
gress in the city of Big Smoke, and saw
George V. bricklaying on the foundation job
of the Commonwealth building in London.
By the way, George is the most expensive
bricklayer in England.
Mrs. W. IT. Walsh, mother of A. D.
Walsh of Dalgety’s, and of Western Walsh,
died in Toowoomba this week. She was
one of the founders of the Governesses’
Home, and was also intimately connected
with most of the Brisbane charitable organi-
sations. Unlike most other charity workers,
she disliked publicity, so the greater part
of her work was done without the clamor
of press paragraphs. She was a handsome
old lady and a gentlewoman of the old
school, w T hich went down before the rush of
the school for scandal, and the woman’s
frights school, and the X-ray skirt school
and a few other modern acadamies for de-
preciating femininity, my dear.
On Thursday morning a deputation of
women boxed up the Minister for Lands,
and demanded a piece of the ancient Pad-
dington cemetery as a playground for chil-
dren. Minister Tolmie promised, so a play-
ground and a kindergarten will soon hang
out its shingle on the site of old Skeleton
Park.

Dalgety’s staff held its annual outing on
Monday afternoon in the presence of its
best girl, a brass band and some superior
kinds of food, etc. I’m not a judge of the
etc., but the rest of the appurtenances was
uncommonly cheering.
The outback parson needs to have a sense
of humor. One was conversing to an audi-
ence of cockies, and he chose the Gadarene
swine for the meat of his discourse. One
old man, who had listened attentively, sud-
denly broke in with great emphasis, “But
what I want to know is, who paid for them
pigs t”
Stone, the aviator and jockey of the
clouds, again essayed to fly at Ascot to-day.
He did not attract rain, but the rest of his
ill-luck still pursued him. When getting a
start a limb of the air-horse was sprained
or bent or hamstrung, and the animal
promptly went on strike and refused to
skychase again. Young Jones circled round
about eight times, at the rate of 40 miles
an hour or thereabouts, but the race that
the public had paid to see did not come off.
This is the third disappointment, and
presently somebody will get annoyed.
Captain G. A. Richard, who for many
years managed Mount Morgan and made mil-
lionaires, has been doing some air-sailing in
one of the Zeppelin craft. The party
set out from Potsdam, and. after circling
about Berlin, finished a 100-mile tour by
returning to the starting-point, r Miss
Richard was with dad, and has recently
dropped to Rockhampton the postcard she
wrote when the wind was howling through
her tresses. The other Australians who
made this trip were Mr. and Mrs. Hobbs, of
a Brisbane legal firm, and Vance Jones.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
One of Bananaland’s itinerant teachers, P.
Jensen, has to scuttle round a district of
25,000 square miles in the course of
12 months, and the annual report
of the Education Department complain-
ingly notes that he “can get over it only
three times a year.” What is the Depart-
mental standard of hustling if Jensen rates
as a slow coach?
“Boro-Belle” : “Don Q’s” tale about rail-
way matters (B. 25/9/’l3) leaks at every
joint. Commissioner Evans did not import
the McKeen auto-motor cars; the contract
was made prior to his appointment. When
the first car was put together and weighed,
Evans found that it was 2 tons 9cwt. over
the axle load at the driving end; and seeing
that an 8-ton axle load was specified for,
this overload rendered the cars useless for
light lines, so the Commissioner very rightly
refused to accept them in that state. The
co.’s representative then reduced the weight
on the leading end, bringing it down to the
specified axle load, and cars so fitted are
being accepted, one at a time, after com-
pleting the 500-mile test. Evans has cer-
tainly not developed the importing craze;
and as to his being kind to the foreign con-
tractor —well, ask Beard, the McKeen Com-
nany’s Brisbane representative, what he
thinks about it

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Tom Noddy: Hell begins to bulge witb
sub-editors who get there through cursing at con-
tributors for jambing their type-written lines so
close together that the slimmest pencil can’t squeeze
in between.... Don Gaspar: Right off our beat....
Ormiston: At the time when the Spanish Armada
tried to do things, the British ships were painted
any old color. Of 201 vessels only 50 were Govern-
ment craft —the rest belonged to buccaneers, pirates
and other maritime bushrangers, and there was but
a single rule under Heaven that applied to the lot
of them. Any modern commission agent will tell
you what that rule was. .. . Longworth: Sorry, but
can’t oblige you with any remedy for falling hair.
You might try glue, but we don’t recommend it....
J.8.H.: No copies now available. .. .Rusty Cuss: It’s
all of it vigorous. But there’s a lot of it impos-
sible and your ear for rhyme wants sharpening. Still
here goes for a bit of plain talk: —
In winter time I waded through the mud
And drove the cows, still chewing at the cud,
Through filthy yards that stank of rank manure,
To dirty bails that fire alone could cure.
They waded to their bellies in the slush,
Each step a stinking geyser that did gush,
Like Waimunga a-scattering spray that spread
In spots o’er all the beast from tail to head.
The milk’s pulled out from foul and grimy udders,
Yet senseless parents watch their children’s shudders
As stricken through this milk by dire disease,
And pray “God's will be done, if so He please.”

we’ve consigned you to. . . .W.E.: Stygian darkness
pervades the office so far as the article which you
mention is concerned. Blame it on to some other
paper. .. .Jerry : The only man we know who delves
deeply into that question is very ill at present with
a beast in his feet —and the name thereof is gout.
Will ask him for the facts when he gets better—if
we don’t forget, or elope, or chance to be run in, or
something... .A.C.: Poverty makes us acquainted
with strange dead fellows. The man you inquire
about passed out some years ago in a public institu-
tion, and was buried in premises adjacent. . . .Ulster-
ite: You are a bad guesser. All the articles you
enumerate were written by the editor himself, and
he admits being born in Glasgow, and having had a
Presbyterian upbringing. There is one man on the
staff whose name begins with O’, but, curiously
enough, he has never touched that subject or shown
the slightest interest in it. He specialises in fiction
and verse. And the sub-editor of Irish descent
whose name doesn’t begin with O’ only gets really
excited when writing on the absurdity of Party Gov-
ernment or the cussedness of Trusts. .. .Nemo: “If
an irresistible force should meet an immovable object,
what would be the result?” It would go into the
waste-paper basket... .M.R.: Story unsuitable....
Bried Dliu: No value here. You might place them
with another paper. . . .A.O’D.: MS. returned. Can-
not reply by letter. Story too conventional....
Bellerive: Room for a condensed romance: —
FOR THE LOVE OF A LADY.
Lady Esther’s more precious
To me than gold,
Exclaimed a young gallant
To a rival bold.
High words.arose over
The lady fair,
Aroused were the rivals
To furious despair.
Prepared for combat
Their swords they drew
And a duel between ’em
Did grimly ensue.
Fierce passes were exchanged
Tragic ended the fray,
Mortally wounded the
Illfated one Lay.

A PERMANENT
OPERA SEASON
There are thousands of people who will not be able to hear the Quinlan Co. in
their many delightful productions—there will be thousands who do hear them
who will wish to hear the operas again. You can all have a PERMANENT
opera season in your own homes through the media of
His Master’s Voice Records
These, with “His Master’s Voice” Gramophones, will bring to you not only the
of Madame Edna Thornton and Miss Perceval Allen (now in Sydney),
but also those of the illustrious Caruso, McCormack, Tetrazzini, Melba, Farrar,
and many others.
The operas include :
The Meistersingers, Faust, Rigoletto, La Tosca, The Rhinegold, La
Eoheme, II Trovatore, Madame Butterfly, Lohengrin, Aida, Tales of
Hoffmann, Lucia di Lammermoor, Carmen, La Traviata, Samson and
Delilah, Tannhauser.
Complete record lists, instrument catalogue, and name of nearest accredited
dealer on request.

IN THE £
TT DISCOUNT
off Table Silver.
Our experiment of a couple
of years ago, of taking the
wholesale catalogues of the
great English (Manufacturers
of electro silver and solid sil-
ver goods, and deducting 20
per cent., or 4/- in the £,
from their prices, was of a
revolutionary character, and
might have landed us in heavy
loss, but
IT DID NOT,
owing to our great output,
and so we are continuing the
discount. It only gives us a
bare commission on many
lines, but commissions count
if numerous enough.

Government Notices.
Proposed Government Buildings,
Ottawa, Canada.
A RCHITECTS are invited to submit sketch designs
n in a preliminary competition for the erection
of Departmental and Courts Buildings.
From the designs submitted in this preliminary
competition, six will be chosen by the Assessors, the
authors of which wall be invited to submit more
matured designs in a final competition, for which
the five unsuccessful competitors shall each receive
an honorarium of Three Thousand Dollars
($3,000.00).
The author of the design placed first by the
Assessors will be entrusted with the work, which the
Government intends carrying out at once.
The competition is limited to British Subjects
practising in the British Empire.
The Government has appointed Mr. T. E. Collcutt,
Mr. J. H. G. Russell and Mr. J. O. Marchand as
Assessors to act in its behalf.
The designs in the first competition must be
delivered in Ottawa (carriage paid) not later than
12 o’clock noon, 2nd January, _1914, addressed to
the undersigned.
Designs sent by San Francisco mail closing at
Sydney on 13th December will be accepted.
Conditions for both competitions may be
had on application to the undersigned, and at
the office of the High Commissioner for Canada, 17
Victoria Street, London, England; and the offices
of Mr. D. H. Ross, Canadian Trade Commissioner,
Melbourne; and Mr. W. A. Bedd’oe, Canadian Trade
Commissioner, Auckland, N.Z.
By order,
R. C. DKSROCHERS,
Secretary.
Department of Public Works,
Ottawa, Canada,
18th August, 1913.

* * * *
A new World’s Track Unpaced Hour
record on a bike was recently put up by Marcel
Berthet at Paris, when he blew round the Buffalo
Veladrome for 60min , and covered 26 miles V26yds.
on his dead lonesome. It was a night ride, too, and
at half the distance Berthet was well inside the pre-
vious record. The reception he received after put-
ting up this most strenuous and least artificial of
records beggars description. The Parisians went mad
over him. So this is the third of the world’s fastest
records held by Frenchmen.
* * * *
Apparently there is nothing so unstable
as a world’s record, and the one hour unpaced bike
record of 26 miles 726 yards put up by Marcel
Berthet at Paris, mentioned above, has already
been sent to the woods. And the man that
did it was Berthet himself. He was not, it seems,
satisfied with his previous performance, and a cable
to the Dunlop Rubber Co. announces that he has
now succeeded in covering 27 miles 361 yards in the
60 minutes. The new record was established at
Paris, and probably on the same track on which the
busted record was established. The chances are that
it will be a long while before the new figures are
upset—or that it will be wrecked almost immedi-
ately. You never know.
The fastest speed at which a human being
has travelled 100 miles (barring the fall of Mephis-
topheles from Heaven) was the 119 miles in 50min.
which the French aviator Guillaux traversed at Paris
a week or so ago. The fastest speed done in a motor-
car for 100 miles was the 106 miles 387 yards per
hour, put up by Jules Goux on the Brooklands track,
England. Rather interesting that the two swiftest
human beings (Mephistopheles wasn't human) are
Frenchmen —but then the Gaul always had the repu-
tation of being fast.
The makers of the Sunbeam, the English
car that made a splash in the last two French Grand
Prix races and the Indianop.olis International Speed
Contest, have built a special 12-cylinder car for the
express purpose of smashing all rivals. At a recent
trial on the Brooklands track it got over one half-
mile at the rate of 120 miles per hour. If the car
can live up to this bewildering promise, it means
that a new record book will shortly have to be
printed, and the old one torn up and used for pipe-
iights.
John Roberts, the grand old man of bil-
liards —the big personality among a crowd of common-
place experts—has retired from the game which his
father (the original John) raised to pub-
lic respectability, and proposes to end his
days in nursing his health. The ex-cham-
pion, who has turned 66, owned a pictu-
resque little property in Devonshire (Eng.), but the
climate thereabouts is too mild for the veteran, and
he is shifting his domicile to a breezy seaside district.
He shoiffd be capable of a very readable book of re-
miniscences, for he has travelled a lot in his time.
His first trip to India with the original William
Cook dates back 40 years, or more,
Ernest Barry, the champion sculler, Is get-
ting some gate-money out of the game by appearing
at the London halls. A sketch called “The River
Girl’’ is the medium for the introduction of Barry,
who gives a performance with his sculling apparatus
and delivers a lectuip'tte on the rival methods of
pushing a boat the water. If he gets on
fill-’em-up-again terms' with the music-hall wasters
and sporting has-boens, Barry’s championship will
soon be at the mercy of the first lusty challenger who
takes him on. #
London Evening News is publishing a
series of “Life Stories of Famous Cricketers, told by
Themselves. 99 Frank Tarrant* now the lecognised
“greatest all-round” in England, is the latest. His
story is a dreadful reflection on the Australian
cricketing habit of choosing the has-beens for the
bi" games. Born in Melbourne in 1881,
was educated at Melbourne Christian Brothers- Col-
lege and was taught cricket by his uncle Ambrose
in a back yard. At 15 he joined the Fitzroy club ;-
at 16 he performed the hat trick, two of his victims
beino- Warwick Armstrong and Harry Graham. Next
he was chosen to play for Victoria against Tasmania
and M.L., but was keenly disappointed at being left
out of other inter-State games. He thought he would
have'been selected to play against P. F. Warners
English team in 1902, but he wasn t. Warner,
however, saw him bowling on the Melbourne ground,
and being mightily impressed, asked him whether he
would like to come to London “I had almost given
up hope of ever getting into the first flight of Aus-
tralian cricketers,” he says; so lie jumped at the
offer and became a Middlesex professional Coached
bv C. B. Fry, Ranji and others, he developed into
a first-class batsman as well as a bowler. Now he
makes money in the winter as well as in summei ,
for during the past five years he has spent the
English winter in India as a member of the cricket
team of the Maharajah of Patiala. #
Among tbe camp-followers of the Murkan
baseballers who will presently descend upon this
country the newspaper reporters will be boss bounc-
ers, sure. Twenty years ago, or more, a bast ball
crowd visited these shores, and brought its own his-
torians with them, but that luckless expedition was
only a small matter compared with the threatened
tour of the Chicago “White Sox and N ® w
“Giants.” If the party, including conductors,
advance agents and non-commissioned barrackers, is
to number 100. a half-dozen wnters-up can be taken
for granted. Baseball literature is appalling in its
grandeur. The pitcher of the ball figures as a
miracle-worker; the man who wallops it exhausts the
Yankee scribes’ vocabulary of technicalities in the
description of every stroke he makes. A glamor of
romance envelopes the movements of each outfielder.
The game, on American paper, looks equally subtle
and exciting, and to read about a meeting of Sox
3 contain the field is to itch for an opportunity

The Rolling Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ERE’S to the Long, Wet, Rollin’ Road,
That’s bore me ’ere an’ there“~an’
thence,
An’ cheer again for that same road
That soon again will bear me ’ence—
The oldest road that ever ran,
The longest man ’as ’ad to slog
That ’as been since the world began
An’ the first savage launched a log.
Since the first savage launched ’is log
An' paddled it with ’ands an’ feet,
Through all the long years’ mist an’ fog,
Till now we ’ave the steamers’ beat,
An’ strainin’ sails in every sea.
Where trade ’as come or man ’as go-od ;
From Gabo Light to Galilee
The ships tramp down the Rollin’ Road.
As ships ave tramped the Rollin’ Road
Since skipper Noah's day, unchecked
By all the storms that ever blowed
An’ sunk the earth-roads deep an’ wrecked.
The Road that, ever since, ’as scrolled
Acrost the world's big ’ist’rv book —-
The Road that empires fight to 'old,
The Road that Drake an’ Nelson took.
The Road that Drake an’ Nelson took
To ’umble, sinful Dago pride,
The Road their keels an’ cannon shook
An’ later on. whereon they died.
The Road w’ich Anson, Benbow, Blake,
An’ Admirals o’ high degree,
Was free to loose, or leave, or take—
An’ just as free to you an’ me.
An’ just as free to you an’ me
To pick an’ choose the course we’ll take,
By rutted tracks unmarked to see,
By made roads each ’imself must make.
On no two runnin’ days the same,
An’ yet unchanged as years go by,
From blinkin’ berg to tropic flame
It’s still the same old Road we ply.
It's stili the same old Road we ply.
An’ cheer to make our landfall plain;
But yet an’ yet, until we die,
The road we turn to take again.
For stay we can’t, so go we must,
Till —ships an’ men—to Davy Jones
Our hulks drop down, in dust to dust,
The only dust the Wet Road owns.
The only dust the Wet Road owns—
The salt that crusts the funnels grey,
The salt that’s in our blood an’ bones
That whips our lips wi’ stingin’ spray;
The sprayin’ dust that heads the flood
W’ich surges on the rushin’ keels,
The dust that swirls in drivin’ scud
Beneath the wild white ’orses’ ’eels.
'Ere’s to the wild white ’orses’ ’eels.
The plungin’ backs I 'ave bestrode ;
’Ere’s to the love for them I feels
An’ to their Long Wet Rollin’ Road,
That gave the sweetest I ’ave earned.
That bore me to the best I’ve knowed.
That taught the most of love I’w> learned —-
’Ere’s ’er —the Long. Wet, Rollin’ Road—
The Long, Wet, Lonely, Rollin’ Road.
N.s.w.
BOYD CABLE.
Only the Kid.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
summer boarder pulled down the
brim of her old panama to shut out a
dazzling streak of light. A westering
sun made a white flash on the iron roof of
the little slab house where visitors were
taken in—on strictly moderate terms. The
dairy-farm was per lied askew on the side of
a hill. It belonged to a large family of hills
which heaved themselves up against the
shoulder of a parent mountain covered with
stiff, spiky whiskers of dead scrub. In front
the paddocks bou ided over bare knobby
mounds till they tell down in a hollow at
some sliprails by the roadside. Here a huge
placard mentioned, in dingy white letters on
a mouse-colored ground, that “Sea View
farm was licensed to sell pure milk.” Board-
ers were alluded to as a postscript, for the
shadow of the Cow brooded over the land.
Jane Smart, hovering on the border of her
thirtieth Christmas, was the only summer
boarder at Sea View. It was cheap, and not
nearly so nasty as many other cheap things
which a clerk in a city rag store has to crowd
into a weekly wage of 30s. In fact, the
effort of supporting existence in a bed-sitting
cupboard, and doing all her own cooking,
washing, and most of her dressmaking, in ad-
dition to grinding from 8.30 a.m. till 6 p.m.
a t a typewriter machine, had worn her
almost as fine as the blade of a crosscut saw.
bhe was getting the same kind of jagged edge
to her nerves. Taking what was left of her
severely in hand, Jane had saved up sufficient
cash to spend her annual 14 days’ holiday on
a dairy-farm. She meant to fill her lean
framework day by day with milk. Unfortu-
nately, she wandered down to see the process
m all its native dirt. After 48 hours’
acquaintance with the conditions of
the milking-shod, the boarder drank
tank water and squirmed at the cream
Jng. But there were compensations,
-the wind blew fresh and cool across
a sapphire wash of sea which lapped the
onse of ironstone cliffs a mile away. And
tnc bald, brown hills, cut into slices by low
stone walls, made a delectable promenade
or city feet. An occasional tail that might
nave a snake at the other end of it could be
seen afar off. The frequent horizon of horns
mat loomed over every other knoll could be
aiagnosed as the property of mild young
or the crumpled antagonism of his
m ?j® sty the Bull, taking his ladies for a
midday stroll. In the latter case there was
ways time to run. When the summer
wanted excitement she followed, afar

airs. Muggins yelled this hospitable querv
over the back fence—if the shabby house
could be said to have more back than front
in any special part pf its ramshackle anat-
omy.
“I’ll come inside !” screamed Miss Smart,
glad that the three o’clock snack gave a
chance of respite from too much bull.
“I’ll bring out your tea, Father —seein’
as you’re busy with him !”
It was a family tradition, loyally invented
by Mrs. Muggins, that “Father.” in spite of
rheumatics in both hands, which naturally
prevented his doing any milking, was a man
of toil. The evening meal was always gar-
nished with stories of the days when Dad
worked his fingers to the bone to make the
farm what it was to-day.
“Seein’ as there’s some rose bushes and a
few chrysanthies I put in this ’ere garden—-
and took out a ton o’ stone afore I could get
me spade down —I’ll jest keep a eye on
Dook so that ’e don’t nip ’em orf.”
Silas sank into the boarders’ low canvas
chair —a sacred chair reserved for paying
guests. He lit his pipe, and mused on the
hard lot of a small dairy farmer. Mrs.
Muggins, a little wizened-up Methuselah of
a woman who had squeezed a century of
toil into 38 years of home life amongst the
cow bails, flicked a kitchen chair, for Miss
Smart.
“Not too much milk,” said the boarder
hurriedly. “Er—no —l’m not very fond of
it- —that is—l like the tea just colored.”
In the interval of washing, scrubbing
floors, scalding cans, milking cows and bak-
ing bread when the baker’s cart forgot to
call, Mrs. Muggins had once found time to
present her husband with twins. They
came tumbling into the kitchen now. after
chasing the milkers' into the stockyard.
“Hello Mum —got some tea? By crikey,
it’s hot goin’ after them busted”
“Ladies present!” said Mum; “now yous
just wait on yourselves. I’m goin’ to have a
bite in peace, and so is Miss Smart.”
“Hi, here comes the Kid !”
Seth and Ike gave ’Melia a vociferous wel-
come, and pushed her into a steep declivity
in an ancient settee that wobbled on three-'*
legs near the fireplace.
“Gammon you ain’t found thet calf!”
“What kind o’ a calf is it, Kid? I hope
you ain’t had the cheek to find twins. One
pair’s enough, I reckon, on this here farm!”
“Yous jest let ’Melia be—and behave
yourselves—or I won’t give yous another
bite. Here you, Ike, take this out to
Father!”
’Melia mopped her hot face, flung her
mane of tawny hair off her forehead, pulled
her rag of a skirt well down over her knees,
and swallowed a cup of scalding hot fluid
before she replied.
“I ain’t found it!”
“What!” Mrs. Muggins dropped the
kettle on the hob, and stood rooted to the
floor.
“I ain’t found neither of ’em !” ’Melia s
sullen air of defeat surrounded this remark,
which was. dislocated by passing through a
mouthful of scone.
“G’arn —ye’re just kiddin!” Then the
red-headed twin who was Seth, rushed out
to tell the bad tidings to Father. He fell up
against Ike and the two raced off to break
the news.
“Well, I’m blest!”
Mum’s hair was done up in tight little
plaits screwed together on top of her head.
Every plait quivered with shock as she ab-
sentlv stabbed the whole coiffure with a long
hairpin. She had picked this weapon out
of a crack in the floor, and kept on driving
it through her hair as she stared at ’Melia.
“If that heifer gets away an’ dies in the
bush it’ll just knock spots out o’ Father!
She’s the best beast on the place ’cept the
“I’ll find ’em ter-night if I die fer it,” said
the Kid, stung by the unaccustomed sense of
failure. To her warped intelligence the great
round world swung on the axis of the Cow.
A fierce loyalty to Mum and the twins drove
her day and night like the lash of a whip.
To the boarder, used'to the frank hatred
of the underpaid girl employee for the
wealthy employer, and the contempt that
mutiuous young person always showed for
his property, this devotion was a whole chap-
ter of revelations. ’Melia, child of the
State, gave gold for copper, new lamps for
old, the best that was in her of honest ser-

Doan’s Ointment cured me two yea..
ago, so I reckon I am cured for good.
Doan's Ointment is sold by all Chemists and Stores at
2/9 per pot, or posted free by Foster-McClellan Co.,
76 Pitt Street, Sydney .
vice for the bare right to live. She slept on
a torn mattress full of lumps like clods of
earth. This she dragged out of a mouse-
haunted cupboard every night and flung on
the kitchen floor. She got up at four in the
morning, winter and summer. The vigilant
Silas had always attended to this part of
the Kid’s training. lie set an alarum to
that hour, and automatically bawled from
his bed in the next room: “Git up there,
’Melia, yer lazy Kid!” By force of long
habit, she could not only stumble to her feet
in the middle of a dream, but half dress her-
self before she was properly awake. Mum
and the twins fell into their clothes in the
same half-dazed fashion, while the head of
the house wanted to know what time o’ day
they meant to start the milking. Then Silas,
having finished the hardest part of his day’s
toil, turned over and snatched his morning
nap.
, “I’ll get ’em right enuff, when ther milk-
in’s done.” ’Melia added this codicil to her
will, as she rushed out of the door. Ike and
Seth clanked big milk cans and yelled to the
Kid to wait a jiffey.
“I seen where they got out. The fence is
broke up behind the lantana on the hill. I
tracked ’em down to the waterhole. Maybe
they’ve got away to that green feed down
by Marley’s,” she called back.
“Yer must be kiddin' yer carn’t find ’em,”
bawled Silas as the Kid raced down to the
stockyard. But the swollen udders of the
mothers of the herd were an insistent call,
and the family had no time to argue with
Dad.
“If ’twern’t for the rheumatics in me fin-
gers” began Silas.
“That needn’t stop yous from walkin’
round an’ having a look for ’em!” This
unexpected parting shot from Mrs. Muggins
drove Silas to the only comparatively easy
chair in the kitchen. “When a man’s own
missis turns agon him, it’s a bad lookout —
eh. Miss Smart?”
But the boarder affected not to-hear. Sh ■
was putting the ends of the fire together and
wondering how she could concoct a meal for
the family on the • jiggling bars of an old
colonial oven.
“I’m going to give them a surprise and
have the tea all ready. Then we can all go
off and help ’Melia.’*
“She don’t expect it! By crikey, I’ve
brought her up to work. There’s/nothing in
this world I’m up agen so much as loafin’.
Y\ ork is the finest thing a nipper can liev.
T giv £l2 for that Jersey heifer, and the
Kid d —d well "knows she’s got to find ’er!”
Then the father of the family cut up a
small wad of tobacco and went forth to
ruminate on the uncertain habits of dairy
cattle.
A pale young moon hung low in a sky of
pearl and amethyst. The cabbage-tree palms
rustled their stiff green petticoats in a lazy
breeze that made furtive catspaws on the
summer sea. Silhouetted against the glory
"f the paling sunset, the great Moreton Bay
figs stood like carved stone, fashioned in the
form of foliage. Jane Smart, with the
macadamised instinct of a, dweller in cities,
had followed the road till she came to the
track that turned off to Marley’s cultivation
patch. This skirted the edge of steep cliffs.
The Muggins family were distributed
amongst the sheltered gullies behind the
house. A wild bellow from the edge of the
cliff sent Jane flying over the loose stones on
the cattle track that meandered to the end
of Marley’s fence. One panel was down, and
the lost beast was tearing up and down on
the top of the wall of scarred rock that made
a sheer drop of a hundred feet to the sea
below. The tide was out. A thin line of
white foam licked the creamy sand where a
few belated gulls scavenged amongst the
leavings of the ocean.
“The pest of a calf has fallen over the
edge, Jane told herself. But a hideous fear
knocked at her heart, and drove her forward
stumbling, panting in the gathering dusk.
A hundred yards away a precipitous track
made a possible descent to the beach. Once
she nearly fell off its narrow foothold. A
dismal wail from the calf caught in a ledge
of bush and rock, not ten feet below the top
proved that the cause of the trouble was
alive. With torn hands and lacerated shoe
leather, Jane reached the bottom, and blun-
dered ankle-deep in the soft sand till she
found It. A small heap of shabby frock
and a dead white face, with a screaming
gull fluttering overhead.
“Gome, ’Melia, don’t be frightened! I’m
going to carry you home. You didn’t guess
what strong arms I had —did you, poor
Kid ?”
But she knew that the sea fowl’s instincts
were sure. This was only a body—flotsam
and jetsam, to be inquired into With the
sharp point of a beak that probed like a lan-
cet. She tried to lift the Kid, but the dead
weight of that small limp figure was awful.
She sat down and tried to think. Then she
remembered that less than a quarter of a
mile along the coast the cliffs fell away to
sandy knoll covered with coarse tussocks.
Some fisher folk had a small humpy on this
rise. It took a long time for these two —
the Living and the Dead —to reach this shel-
ter. and Jane heralded their arrival with
shrill screams. The raw edges of her nerves
had to be soothed with uproar. Tenderly
enough they took It in, for the Kid was quite
an old and much-respected serf in Cowland.
“She must have chased ’em out o’ Mar-
ley’s, an’ got too near the edge. To think
of ’er, the cutest kid in the district with
beasts to come to. ’er end like this !”
“I'd like to wring the neck o’ Silas Mug-
gins. Why doesn’t he do a bit o’ work him-
self —the skunk !”
They cackled drearily over the body while
Jane, with her brain on fire, raced over the
hills to the farm. She wanted to say some-
thing ; to do something that would pierce
the hide of Silas. Like a mad thing she
burst into the kitchen. He was dozing on
the old settee.
“Wake up—you lazy beast! She’s dead
—broken her neck ! Why didn’t you look for
her yourself?”
She fell upon him, shaking him with the
fury of a wild cat, before he had time to
leap to his feet. The dull brain of Muggins
searched for an idea. This crazy woman—
he had never liked her quiet ways—was the
secret emissary of some gang of fools in Syd-
ney who sent people to gaol for alleged cruelty
to animals. The heifer was dead—a horri-
ble loss —but he was more concerned about
it than anyone else.
“I give ’er the best feed I ’ad.” he said
sullenly. “She were a silly fool to clear out.
Serve her d—d right if she did break ’er
bloomin’ neck !”
Jane picked up the nearest missile, a small
wooden stool, and flung it at his head. It
missed him and struck ’Melia’s kangaroo pup,
which fled howling into the night. Silas
followed, yelling for the family to come and
save his life. They tumbled down the near-
est hill and rushed breathless into the kit-
chen.
“Gawd lieli) us—what’s the matter?”
cried Mum. Ike and Seth flung themselves
on the summer boarder, but she beat them
back.
“Keep away. I want to hurt that lazy
beast of a slave-driver! She’s dead, I teil
you! Fell over the cliffs!”
“An’ I’ll never see my 12 quid agen!”
groaned Silas emboldened by the presence
of Mum and the twins.
Jane made another rush at. the enemy,
this time with the poker, but the room was
running round, and Silas was only a sway-
ing shape, of something detestable in the
middle. Mum held her boarder with both
hands, and implored her to tell them if
she’d been bitten by a mad dog.
“It’s ’Melia —she’s only a body—she—the
gulls were fighting over her —I carried her
to —she’s quite dead” Then the little
slab house seemed to fall on top of the sum-
mer boarder. Mum failed to grasp the
hideous truth. “Git the dipper, Seth, an’
douse ’er with water. She's a bit light-
headed !”
Rut a light pierced the thick skull of
Muggins. His mean face looked almost
cheerful. The State had thousands of chil-
dren. He was a poor man and had onlv
one Jersey cow.
“By Gosh, Mum. ’tain’t the ’eifer, arter
all! It’s on’y the Kid !”
M. C OX TAYLOR.
A Tale of Suburban Enterprise.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Burglary and robbery from the person must still
be regarded as pleasant evening amusements for
some of the pale residents of the suburbs until the
police force is materially strengthened.
—Sydney payer.
A LITTLE moon all thin and pale
Was sitting in the simple sky ;
A small bird wagged its small black
tail,
The long, bright day began to die ;
The harbor boats made frequent wail
From brazen throats, with coal-dust dry.
The sun arranged for a display
Of colored fire before he sank;
A little wind across the bay
Blew in my whiskers saltly dank ;
And I had twenty pounds to pay
With four-and-sixpence in the bank.
I cast about for some wise plan
To raise the cash to meet the bill;
I thought to slay my fellow man,
Or crack a crib, or rob a till;
For sitting there the world to scan
Had positively made me ill.
On every boat that crossed the tide
There were at least one hundred men
Whose bank accounts so swelled with pride
That with one flutter of a pen
They could such woes as mine deride
And multiply the sum by ten.
The person that I tipped last night
For bringing me some drops of wine
Had never been in such a plight,
Or known such poverty as mine;
He, too, the ready cheque could write
That hardest banks would not decline.
Thinking such thoughts, my soul grew thin,
And bitter envy moved me sore ;
The cash that seemed so hard to win
Was freely dumped at every door,
Save mine. With heart as hard as sin
I gazed upon the darkening shore.
Such was my mood when eve grew late,
I burned to slay and smite and rob,
To grab the world with hands of hate
And make it yell and writhe and sob ;
Then Thompson through the garden gate
Came in to borrow fifteen bob.
Here was a pal to match my need ;
I rose and gripped him by the hand ;
I told him that some purse must bleed,
And made him swiftly understand
That as we needed cash with speed
Some golden fish our hooks must land.
Armed with revolvers forth we fared,
The night grew very dark and cold ;
We stuck up twelve lone wights, and shared
Some silver and five pounds in gold ;
Our looks were fierce, and no one dared
His private treasure to withhold.
I revelled in my glad, gay sin,
But Thompson with remorse was torn;
I had to rake those dollars in.
And treated his affright with scorn;
By hook or crook I needs must win
My twenty pounds before the morn.
We struck a prize at Alfred-street.
A heavy person glad with beer
Approached us on unsteady feet
And yielded eighteen guineas clear;
We merely made him take a seat,
And cocked a pistol at his ear.
We wandered round by Neutral Bay
Collecting cash from all we met;
We only tried to take away
As much hard coin as we could get,
And everyone seemed pleased to pay
The simple tribute that we set.
Light hearted as a homing bird
I took my bedward way at last;
Some folk might deem my joy absurd,
But every danger now was past—
“ Bail up !” I heard the sudden word.
And through the darkness peered aghast.
“Bail up !” I knew the voice that spoke
In accenfs full of wrath and dread.
I smote him with a counter-stroke;
“Still out collecting. .Tones?” I said.
And like a whiff of frightened smoke
That highway robber turned and fled.
But I passed on with laughter light,
And gained the home I deemed secure.
There paused in wonder and affright
At trouble past my power to cure—
Some dark marauder of the night
Had stolen all my furniture.
